THE RED PAGE.
Other Irrelevancies and Grass-Tree
George.
THE HAWKER he.fi invited me to tea, and
we were gipsying in the caravan on a neat
grassy slope which descended to the Parra-
matta River. I watched the host stirring
something in a saucepan with an iron spoon.
“ Has a saucepan a soul ? ” queried the Hawker
meditatively. “At first sight, my friend, this
seems a question ridiculous enough to make a pig
squint itself silly; one of those absurd queries
which a mad monk might have posed to an insane
friar in mediseval times. But a little reflection
opens out before us the wonderful ambiguity of
the word —Soul.
“An oyster has a soul, according to that
learned scientist, Haeckel; and he is right, inas-
much as he defines soul as the sum of the experi-
ence the thing has received in its life-time.
“ An onion has a soul, according to an American
writer. He remarks seriously— 1 The onion in its
satin wrappings may be said to have a soul. You
take off coat after coat and the onion is still there ;
and, when the last one is removed, who dare say
that the onion itself is destroyed ? If there is one
thing on this fallen earth that the angels weep
over more than another it is the onion.’
“ Gold has a soul, according to the Theosophist,
who affirms that everything has a threefold nature
—the spiritual, the astral, and the earthly; the
two former, so to say, being the soul of the latter.
“Therefore the saucepan may have a soul, and
my grandmother’s old tin kettle may have a
ghost; in like manner, in this mad world of words,
you may argue that a Jerusalem artichoke has a
guardian angel who actually saw the dish run away
with the spoon—the prehistoric ancestor of this
spoon, may be. Sit up ; it’s done ; and don’t
ask what it is.”
It tasted as well as it smelt, anyway. “ And
where have you come from last?” I asked. “I
have come last,” said the Hawker, seriously,
“ from the neighbourhood of Grass-Tree George.”
“Years ago, as you don’t know, the gathering
of grass-tree gum was a promising industry round
Sydney-side. But the varnish was not a success,
and the little community of gum-gatherers died
out until there remained only Grass-tree George.
The guin itself was used as a colouring matter in
the'varnish ; also as a wood-stain. Attempts were
made to manufacture picric acid from it, but other
sources proved cheaper.
“And I have just travelled from the purview
of that Last of the Mohicans. You don’t know
George ? Well, he has the appearance of a person
carelessly constructed of second-hand material.
Lots of him don’t fit the other parts at all. His
shoulders are precipitous, and although it is absurd
to think that he exi-ts without food, the closest
examination of the region covered by a strawberry
oalf-stkin waistcoat reveals the existence of nothing
in which food may be contained for digestion.
His eyes are a very poor match, and all the more
confusing because one, an ordinary-looking brown
Optic, is sad and fixed-looking, while the other, of
a sea-greeri tint, doesn’t seem to be under his con-
trol at all.
“George is of a mysterious and confidential
disposition. He will drag you up a gateway and
unfold his views about ‘as good a little lotter
gum ’ in a soft, mildewed whisper. Then, while
you are wondering whether his damp voice will
give you your death of cold, George will burst
into a smile, revealing rows of teeth irregular as a
cockatoo fence, and all the time the loose eye will
lurch in every direction. According to calcula-
tions he has made with a piece of soap on the
looking-glass in a little bar-parlour, George
reckons that up one of the bends of Middle
Harbour there exist untold quantities of gum ‘ if
a man could only get it.’
“Well, after years of fruitless endeavour to get
a syndicate interested in tha scheme, the old boy
determined to commence operations in spite of
‘the ring agen him.’ Borne years ago he came
panting to me one day and announced that he had
just got the outfit he had pined for all his life.
‘She’s laying down there’—and he indicated
as nearly as a singularly-gnarled finger would
permit, the direction of Pyrmont Bridge.
“Now, before the wager-boat was known in
Sydney Harbor, it was the pride of Jim Richards
and Suggard and Reid and many of the old-time
watermen to have 22-foot skiffs for racing pur-
poses. They were beautiful boats —cedar plank-
ing, joggled timbers, and would last for ever.
After 20 or 30 years service, however, these skiffs
would get a bit tender on the keel and hog rather
badly. The craft George had secured was one of
the real old style, with a keel like a see-saw. In
her lay a frayed sand-sieve, a shovel, a few bags,
a loaf of bread with a piece cut out and filled with
butter, and a billy-pot with a bottom like a rose
stuffed with rags.
“ ‘ What more could a man want ? ’ exclaimed
George, in an ecstatic whisper. ‘Bought her
right out for 25 bob.’ He clambered aboard, and
I watched him row away with a perfectly circular
stroke, and his loose eye revolving like the South
Head light.
“About a week afterwards I saw a man lying
on his stomach on the old A.S.N. wharf, tana
every now and then lowering himself over the
edge till nothing but a pair of of women’s cloth
boots with bulgy elastic could be seen from the
road. After a long peer under the wharf he got
up and straightened himself. It was George, and
an emaciated and anxious-looking George, too.
‘“Believe in luck ?’ he asked in a nervous whis-
per. ‘ Of all the thieving hounds, them
Middleharberers ought to be shot. There I was,'
and as good a little lotter gum as ever I see. Had
the seeve rigged up, and had strained out all the
hadders—42 of ’em—thick’s a man’s leg, and was
ready to bag up. I goes down to where I left the
craft ’ —here George underwent an appalling series
of spasms—‘and she was clear gone—sich a nice
a craft, too ! Of all the thieving ! Did yer ever try
• to carry a sieve and a shovel and a billy-pot oyer
mountainyous country ? ’ and George’s erratic eye
waved wiidly.
“I confessed that 1 had not, but recalled the
circumstance of a careful, asthmatic man I saw at
a camp one moonlight night, clambering up a
rocky slope with a wire-stretcher. His ease could
not, however, be said to coincide with George’s,
because, so far as the transient beam of the moon
permitted me to observe, it appeared to push him
downhill every time, except when he adroitly
rolled himself on top of it and toboganned down.
“‘Ah, yes,’ continued George, ‘it was jist
dreadful. Yer see, I was clean lost, but I reckoned
that, so long as I headed about nor’-west and kep’
a big scrubby-looking hill well on my right shoul-
der, I’d be pretty sure to pick up the Spit. But
, my cargo was that shifty, and I gets to thinking
d so much about them Middle-harberers-—the
thieving hounds—that what’s I do but lose my
r i? S \l ,i ier . e was wanderin’ round and round
till about three in the morning, when all of a sudden
1 goes nop over a sotter precipice, and lands right
against a shed at Folly Point. When I got across
1 starts huntin’ and peerin’ under these here
wharves and places for the boat, but she’s clean
gone— and sich a nice craft too ! ’
“Well, this time, when I met George, he was
going across to the Bay with some clothes tied up
in a spotted handkerchief. He dragged me into
a gateway and whispered, ‘Just shipped aboard
the Diver—beautiful little craft, 10 tons—River
trade, you know—-firewood and punkins.’ -Then
he burst into a smile and went aboard, and with
him passed from the sphere of industrial enter-
prise the last of the gum-gatherers. Have some
more stew ? And a drop of drink ?
“You know,” said the Hawker, “I’ve often
often thought there’s a lot of philosophy in drink.
Man is sent into this world to open liis mouth,
and, as it is a one-sided world, only one of his
jaws, the under one, reallv works; the upper one
is passive. At his birth, lie is the prettiest little
squeaker imaginable, a cradled sunbeam to his
mother. His first drink is at ‘baby’s hotel,’or,
as Keats elegantly puts it, at ‘ those dainties made
to still an infant’s cries.’ As the boy grows in
years, his mouth is continually open in wonder at
the domestic emergencies of this inconvenient
planet, where slumbering children smile, and men
so often swear in their sleep. Often in boyhood,
he opens his lips with the wild instinctive yell of
freedom, but he finds to his sorrow that he is but a
kid forced to sneak a ride behind the c*irt of
creation, and that he must conform to conven-
tionalities.
“Grown up, the boy sometimes takes strong
drink in imitation of other people. Later in life
he becomes jaded and yawns, for it is a principle
of nature to insist upon the active jaw; if a man
can find nothing new to wonder at he must yawn.
Then, in time, lie may begin to habitually open
his mouth for alcohol; but Providence help him
otherwise !
“The drink mania,” said the Hawker dogmatic-
ally, “is not a thirst. A man goes to that para-
dise of pint-pots, the hotel, for company, as
women go to church for society. Nor is alcoholism
a liking for the mere flavor of strong beverages ;
it is often a repugnance. See an early-morning
toper ! shuddering over his pick-me-up ; yet he
forces himself to engineer it down for the effect it
will have on the mind. Again, drink is not a
disease ; it is a poor human attempt to get away
from disease, whether an unknown ailment in"
some bodily organ, or a malady of the mind. A
wish for happiness, the .supreme aim of humanity,
is at the root of alcoholism.
“The habitual drunkard, that red-faced fungus
of humanity, is an everlasting puzzle to the social
reformer. It is enough to make a barrel of ‘ jolly
good ale and old ’ laugh through its base bunghole
to think that the world does not know what to do
with him. If he has no self-will, if religion and
woman’s love are powerless, it almost seems as if
he were fated to be weeded out by a process of
Natural Selection. His mind is a psychic piano
repeating one awful chord of self-contempt; drink
silences this and wakes up merry-go-mad melodies.
He wants to forget the past, so he repeatedly
christens himself over again with alcohol. Inebri-
ate asylums simply herd a lot of dipsomaniacs
together, who despise each other for their common
failing, and recruit themselves in health for future
carousals.
“ Drink, like the nectar of Nemesis, runs through
the panoramas of past history. Extraordinary
contrasts present themselves; from that wonder-
ful, unknown, primitive man who first tasted
alcohol, and no doubt felt that, within his barbaric
form, he was enshrining a live god falling down-
stairs, to the modem temperance orator affirming
that drink is a dying fiend, vanquished by the
triumphant tea-leaf; from the ancient orgies of
men and women, dancing half-naked in worship of
Bacchus, to the up-to-date company of Salva-
tionists, gesticulating in honor of temperance ;
from Queen Elizabeth, drinking beer for break-
fast, to the slum-wife of to-day, waiting outside
a midnight gin-palace for her unpurifiable lord to
come to supper ; from the ivy-bush, hung outside
the ancient tavern as a charm against excess, to
the pawned swag behind the counter of the
sophisticating bush-pub. in latter-day Australia.
“Water of life! the world is marvellous with
its ‘wine which cheereth God and man,’ as the
Scriptures say. In those romantic Middle Ages,
when men drank .‘ with a jolly rumble, O ! ’ any-
thing was an excuse for an ale-carousal; there
were church-ales to obtain money to repair the
church, cuckoo-ales when that bird came in spring,
and bride-ales before a wedding. From Alexan-
der the Great to Shakespeare the world is remark-
able for illustrious tipplers ; while in the domain
of Natural History we find the flying-fox getting
drunk on cocoa-nut juice, and the bumble-bee
intoxicating itself with the fermented honey of
the sunflower. (By the by, it is said that the word
Sydney is derived from St. Denis, the patron
saint of France, and that St. Denis is an abbrevia-
tion of Dionysius or Bacchus.)
“In these days, when Art has become the
attractive war-paint of commerce, our cities
appear to be elaborate advertisements for Drink.
You might imagine that man had only one duty—
Thirst; and only one pastime—to choose between
the variety of liquors offered to him. The modern
hotel is a constellation of mirrors where a man can
drink himself drunk, and see how he looks from all
points of view when inebriated. The free lunch
in some of these plaoes is a remarkable pantomime.
A little time before the banquet is spread, you
may see a number of hungry and shabby men
scowling in the bar, like a funeral waiting for a
corpse. Soon there enters an hotel assistant over-
laden with the free collation, and immediately a
sort of panic seizes the customers ; they mob the
man till he feels like a dumb-waiter attacked in the
rear-front by a stampede of desperate swine. They
make a dead-set at the feast before there is
time to place it on the counter; then, a
mob of evil-eyed gourmandisers, they fall to, and
animalism is triumphant.
“ To some people in Australia, the seventh day
of the week is Sneaking Sunday. In back streets
of the city you can see a variety of lackadaisical
loafers, slinking round the side-doors of pubs. ;
they can’t get in to their liquid lady-love. There
is a pathos of vile vulgarity about them, as if they
had committed some unchristened crime years ago
and were trying to hide from their own shadows.
Church-bells are ringing in the distance, but they
are parched profanities waiting for a morning
drink. Presently, when all is safe, the landlord
opens the side-door, and they sneak in like a lot
of excommunicated tragedians. Then comes the
matchless joy of saturation on the sly. Swine of
Circe ! how beautiful is the sub-rosa rum, and the
amber ale imbibed in fascinating stealth, when
the tippler feels as shaky as a spectre in a night-
mare of nerves. No, the drunks of Australia
want no Sunday Opening Bill; it would take
away the precious joy of their Sneaking Sabbath.
Now—have another drop, and we’ll change the
subject.”
When and Why the Boers Armed.
For the benefit of the man in the street, those who
made the Transvaal war have invented the myth of a
vast Dutch conspiracy against the British Empire.
[ln Australia men likeQ. Chief-Justice Griffith have
swallowed and circulated the myth.J They dare
not admit that the British were the aggressors. So
the war is described as “inevitable,” the result of
a plot to overthrow the empire which has been
maturing in President Kruger’s brain ever since
Mr. Gladstone’s so - called “ surrender ” after
Majuba. For evidence of all this we are referred
to the Boer armaments. In words that have
found an echo in the speeches of Lord Salisbury
and Sir Michael Hicks-Beach, the Daily Mail has
assured us that —
“The Boers have for nearly 18 years been accu-
mulating artillery, rifles, melinite, German and
Hollander officers, with which to oust England
from South Africa.”
If this can be proved, then we allow that there is
a solid basis for the theory of a Dutch conspiracy,
we will admit that the Boers have provoked the
war and are morally the aggressors.
The burden of proof lies with the Yellow Press.
So far, if we except Mr. Hugh Price Hughes’s
vague reference to the testimony of unnamed
“ Christian missionaries and laymen,” it has pro-
duced not a single item of positive evidence. We
are left to the testimony of the Blue-books and the
correspondents of the Times. The earliest refer-
ence to the subject that'we can find is in an
article on “Natal and the Transvaal in 1890,”
published by the Times on 7 November last. The
writer states that he visited the barracks, arsenal,
etc., in Pretoria. The “standing army,” he tells
us, was limited to a “single battery” of obsolete
guns, and he adds “ the military inefficiency in
every detail was in 1889 beneath contempt.” Six
years later Major Robert White was sent to Pre-
toria at the cost of the Chartered Company to spy
out the land. He, too, visited the Pretoria
arsenal, and his investigations were assisted by
Colonel Frank Rhodes and Captain Ernest
Rhodes. Nine months later the diary in which he
recorded his impressions was picked up on the
field of Krugersdorp. It contains a quaint inven-
tory of the guns which he found in Pretoria (See
Cape Report on Jameson Raid, p. 169) :
1. Half-a-dozen very old pieces of ordnance,
mortars, - &c.
2. One bronze gun of the date of the Second
Empire.
3. A broken Maxim-Nordenfelt.
4. A small muzzle-loader in bad condition.
Besides these broken and obsolete weapons there
were at most nine other light guns and three
Maxims. The list concludes with the remark—
None of the guns I saw were fit for mnch work.
Clearly then the best evidence which the spies of
the Chartered Company, with all Mr. Rhodes’
millions behind them, could find, went to show
that, so far as artillery was concerned, the Boers
were, on the eve of the Raid, almost absolutely
unprovided.
But it may be argued that Major White was
hoodwinked. He may not have seen the modern
armaments at Pretoria. That is possible, but we
have other evidence to show that when they
actually did begin to arm, the Boers were by no
means reluctant to reveal their resources. From
Captain Younghusband, who visited Pretoria for
the Times early in 1896, they did not conceal the
fact that—
“ Orders for batteries of field guns, quick-firing
guns and Maxims, and for sufficient rifles to arm
every Dutchman in South Africa were being sent
to Europe ; European drill instructors and
artillerymen were being imported, and forts were
being constructed round Pretoria on the most
approved designs. One attempt had been made to
take their country from them ; they were thoroughly
convinced that the attempt would be renewed at
some future date ; so the Boers were determined to
be thoroughly on their guard the second time.”
Yet he entirely corroborates Major White’s esti-
mate of the Boer armaments before the Raid,
when he writes (“South Africa of To-day,” pp.
141-3):
“ The Boers had very nearly been caught napping
at the beginning of the year.”
But there is other evidence still. The Daily
News has appealed to the famous Uitlander mani-
festo and to the Transvaal budgets. The mani-
festo is dated 27th December, 1895. It was
written when the Johannesburg conspiracy was
no longer a secret. That manifesto informs us
that £250,000 “ is to be spent upon the completing
of a fort at Pretoria,” &c., and that orders have
been placed in Germany for heavy guns and
ammunition. Note the future tense. The docu-
ment shows that at the end of 1895, on the admis-
sion of the Uitlanders themselves, the Boers were
were adequately armed and had suddenly begun to
set to work to remedy their deficiencies. The
Transvaal Budget tells the same tale. For 1892
the military expenditure of the Republic was only
some £29,739, in 1893 it was £19,340, in 1894
£28,158. In 1895, the year of the Raid, it rises to
£87,308, and leaps in 1896 to a round half-million.
It has been suggested that payments for arma-
ments lurk under the headings “ Public Works,”
“ Special Payments,” “Sundry Services,” but the
totals for these items tell the same tale. In 1894
they figure out to £528,526, in 1895 they rise to
£1,485,244, while in 1896 they stand at £2,007,372.
The trail of the Raid is over all these statistics.
But we are not left to conjecture. We happen
to know exactly at what stage Mr. Kruger took
alarm, exactly which of the preparations that led
to the Raid persuaded him to reorganise his army
and send to Europe for his guns and his instruc-
tors. In the St. James’s Gazette of 29 August last
there appeared over the signature “Anglo-African-
der ” a remarkable account of a consultation which
the writer, who was then in the service of a foreign
power, had wi th Mr. Kruger and General Joubert
m September, 1895. Mr. Kruger asked him why
the Chartered Company was buying “ hundreds of
horses ” for presentation to the new Volunteer
Rhodesian Horse. Mr. Kruger would see in this
only one meaning, which he summed up in the
saying, “ Rhodes 'is going to jump my country, ”
and Gen. Joubert also ?same to the conclusion that
“ it is time to shut our doors, as robbers are about.”
Here, then, at last is the whole story of the Boer
armaments. They were Mr. Kruger’s answer to
Mr. Rhodes’s Raid. They were undertaken not
for aggression but for defence, they were continued
because the Boers knew right well that the men
who had conspired against their independence in
1895 would renew the attack before the party see-
saw had removed from the Colonial Office their
ally and accomplice—Mr. Joseph Chamberlain.
The Boers, then, armed only after the Jameson
Raid had warned them that their independence
was threatened. That fact in itself all but
demolishes the myth of a Dutch conspiracy to oust
Britain from South Africa. It proves at all events
that up to 1896 such an ambition was not a practical
factor in Transvaal politics. But it may be said
that with the resentment that followed Mr.
Rhodes’s freebooting expedition there came also
the determination to erect South Africa into an
independent Boer State. To a certain extent this
is plausible. There was throughout South Africa
a closing of the ranks against the British “ Im-
perialism ” as represented by Mr. Rhodes and the
De Beers group of alien financiers. But all this
involved absolutely no thought of disloyalty to
the British Crown. Of this one needs no more
convincing proof than an article which appeared
in the Times on 16th May, 1899. It was written
presumably by Miss Flora Shaw, who acted as
Mr. Rhodes’s accredited agent in London at the
time of the Raid. None will dispute her authority
on South African affairs, or deny her party bias
against the Boers. Yet she pens a striking
defence of the loyalty of the Cape Dutch,
quoting, for example, Mr. Hofmeyr’s recent
declaration that “if he could do anything to
make the English flag wave in South Africa
yet many and many a year, lie would
do it.” She has misgivings only regarding some
of the Republican Dutch, whose ideal, she tells
us, is an frikander Confederation, independent of
Downing-st. , yet under the British Hag and within
a Federated Empire.. This, after all, is the ideal
that has long been realised in Canada, and will
shortly be accomplished in Australia. It is not
inconsistent with fervent loyalty.
The conclusion then which remains is that the
Boers armed only for defence after the monstrous
invasion of 1895. They dreaded its repetition,
and with reason. Its real authors went unpun-
ished. Mr. Chamberlain still sat in Downing-
street, and publicly whitewashed Mr. Rhodes’s
honour in the House, while the raider j . became
the heroes of the Empire. And we now know
what reason they had to arm and to antici-
pate a fresh invasion. If Reuter’s corre-
spondent can be trusted, the Boers found at
Dundee among General Symons’ papers conclusive
proofs that in 1896 and 1897, immediately after
the Raid, the British Government set to work to
devise plans for the invasion not only of the Trans-
vaal, but also of the Orange Free State. These
papers include “ portfolios of military sketches of
various routes of invasion into the Transvaal and
Free State, prepared . . . immediately after the
Jameson Raid,” and also “Reconnaissanceßeports
of Lines of Advance through the Free State,”
prepared in 1897. The Boers had, after all, some
excuse for defensive preparations. After this dis-
closure it would be a graceful, if ineffectual, act of
reparation to desist from further talk about Boer
aggression and Dutch designs to drive us into the
sea.
SIR ALFRED MILNER AND THE "DUTCH COS-
SPLLAUY."
The following despatch was sent to Mr. Cham-
berlain by Sir Alfred Milner, in August, 1897, or
about eighteen months after the Raid :
Government House, Cape Town,
August 23, 1897.
Sir,—l have the honour to report that the pro-
ceedings in connexion with her Majesty’s Jubilee
have passed off in the most successful manner,
both at Cape Town and, as far as lam able to
judge, throughout South Africa. ... I have no
doubt the same loyalty has been displayed in other
parts of the Empire; but it appears to me to be
of peculiar interest under the special circumstances
of this Colony, and in view of recent events, which,
as you are aware, have caused a feeling of con-
siderable bitterness among different sections of the
community. All that I can say is that, as far as
I am able to judge, these racial differences have
not affected the loyalty of any portion of the
population to her Majesty the Queen. People of
all races—the English, the Dutch, the Asiatics, as
well as the African natives—have vied with one
another in demonstrations of affection for her per-
son and devotion to the Throne. ... It is impos-
sible to doubt that the feeling of loyalty among all
sections of the population is much stronger than
has sometimes been believed. —I have, &c.,
A. MILNER
Governor and High Commissioner.
FACTS THAT SPEAK FOR DUTCH LOYALTY.
(1) In 1895, before the Raid, not only the Cape
Dutch, but the Orange Free State, believing the
Transvaal in the wrong over the Drifts Question,
were prepared to support us, if necessary, with
money and arms.
(2) In 1898 the present Dutch Ministry voted mi
annual contribution of £30,000 to the Imperial
Navy.
(3) It also made over to the Imperial authorities
the splendid naval station of Simon’s Bay.
Of this Mr. Goschen said so lately as 18th May,
1899: “The motion to grant £30,000 a-year was
passed unanimously. The Afrikanders were in
power. The power had passed from'Sir Gonlon
Sprigg ; the Schreiner, the Bond party were a£ the
helm. But it made no difference, and let the
country understand it,”—and he called upon other
colonies to imitate “the patriotic example.”—
London Morning Leader.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Omen?
Mr. Justice Simpson appeared in N.S.W. Divorce Court
one day recently without his wig and gown. His Honor
explained that nothing but abnormal circumstances, over
which—although a Judge-he had no control, would have
caused him to appear on the Bench while not wearing his
insignia of office. Of course nobody suspected, that he
had pawned his wig and robe, but at the same time, and
although the business of the Court went on as usual,
nobody appeared quite satisfied. The shock, especially
coming from so dignified a quarter, was too great and too
sudden. The audience which regularly attends this
court seemed to be divided in opinion. Some of them
looked uneasy ; the rest treated the proceedings with
scarce-concealed contempt.
Take it now from me,
In this humble ode,
Foolish policy
Justice Simpson showed—
Letting the profane
Crowd of common men
See he was a plain,
Common citizen.
Barristers with glum
Humour tried to make
Little of his dumb
Desperate mistake.
Well they might! They feared
That, from root to stem,
This exposure weird
Would be ill for them.
When the Judge is seen
Stripped of majesty—
Who will pay the keen
Barrister nis fee ?
Litigants will probe
His accounts so big—
Say: “So much the robe 1
So much for the wig !
How much now the man
Underneath these rags ?”
I can see a clan
Shouldering their swags.
* * *
Some of the queer folk
Who frequent the court,
Thought it was a joke
Made in Easter sport.
Others, more concerned,
Spoke about his brain —
Said the Judge had turned
Suddenly insane.
Their belief was big
That his law, for sure,
Lurked within his wig,
Or his robe, secure.
And, though business went
In its usual way,
They were not content —
Very sad were they.
“ What will happen next ?
Tell me if you can
Looking sore-perplexed,
Said an ancient man.
“I can tell you, sir—
Said a Person wan—
“ What will quick occur,
If these games go on.
“ When the Law men view,
As to-day did we,
Law and Order, too
Soon will ended be.
“ When the Wig and Gown
People thus contemn—
Sceptre, Globe, and Crown
Fast will follow them. ”
Spake a Voice : “ There will
Be no need for rage :
We shall see them still
Used—upon the Stage.”
Y.J.D.
“In the Morning."
We’re a sixty-five ton hooker, and we’re jammed j
up in the Bight
’Twixt Egmont and Manukau, with Gannet Rock
in sight;
From Nel.'O'i to Kaipara, for kauri—but we’re
light;
And we’ll fall away to loo’ard ’fore the morning.
Then it’s luff her up to wind’ard with every slant
you get,
And watch the weather roller when it’s needed to
be met —
If she starts a stick, or parts a sheet, or sags to
loo’ard —bet
The fishes will have cleaned our bones ere
morning!
At two bells in the morning watch, it hauled to
west-nor’-west
And broke her off four points or more, when
lookin’ up her best;
Now ’tis close-reefed jib and mains’l, and the devil
take the rest!
And it’s less than that she’ll want before the
morning.
So ’tis shove her up to wind’ard, or she’s off to
Kingdom-come ;
The old man’s full of she-oak —tight as any blessed
drum;
The “doctor’s” boilin’ stinkin’ beef, and drinkin’
hell-fire rum ;
So Sam and I must keep the deck till morning.
Well, we cannot fetch the Gannet—keep her just a
point away,
If you “choke” her now—in ballast—with this
sea she will not stay ;
Ready ’bout ! —down helm ! —let draw !—now,
thank the Lord she gathers way!—
And it’s south’ard, on the starboard tack till
morning.
Then it’s jam her up to wind’ard, and it’s luff her
in the squalls,
And for two-pound-ten a month we have to go
where duty calls.
We can’t be much worse off, by gum ! whatever
luck befalls,
In Kingdom-blanky-Glory in the morning.
M.L.
MARSHALL NALDER.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The parsons bend and the parsons pray 5
For the boys who’re off to a distant fray;
The hold is packed with the choicest cheer,
The hero’s chest is aburst with beer. f
The girls have laughed and the girls "have cried,
The crowd it crushed till it nearly died,
For the shufflers stay while the heroes roam —
And the Lord will hustle for the folks at home i

The drought is fierce and the drought is fell,
The plains are black as the hobs of hell,
The cattle are a-dyin’ and the sheep are dead;
The folks are famished but the crows are fed;
There’s no fine talk for a fight like this,
No beer at call with a girl to kiss :
It’s a slouch that’ll stand it when the heroes roam ;
O, the Lord will hustle for the folks at home 1

There’s drums and fifes and a fine old flare—

Though the young ones droop and the old ones die ;

SATURDAY, APRIL 28, 1900.
Independent Australia and Attorney-
General Wise.
When Australia’s part in the Transvaal
war comes to be judicially considered, it
will seem to the historian one of the
maddest freaks of which an apparently
sane people could be guilty. That a
cluster of professedly-democratic Aus-
tralian communities should think it neces-
sary to aid the great British Empire in
destroying the liberties of two little
Dutch Republics in Africa—should abet
the lion in devouring a comparative mouse,
and waste men and money recklessly to
ruin the very Freedom which they are
supposed to be proud of possessing—
this is a marvel which will puzzle
logical people. And the hollowness
of the whole thing is so apparent.
Doubtless the adult Britons in Aus-
tralia are still about as numerous as
the adult Australian-born—and vastly
more energetic : doubtless the mob, hav-
ing once caught the fever, became heated
to an insane pitch ; doubtless Australian
Parliament after Parliament, with few
open dissentients, has acquiesced in the
sending of contingents. But the sham
has all the time been obvious behind the
scenes. The political leaders—tainted
with English influence, or hoping for
profit and “ honors ” from English patron-
age—have themselves been astonished at
the length to which things are carried.
Of the political followers, it is well-known
that a large number are half-hearted,
faint-hearted, and fear to oppose what
they dislike as much as they can dislike
anything. Young Australia has shown
once more its devotion to “ Sport.” The
crowds have cheered, and the children
have collected pennies, as crowds and
children will, because at the moment it is
the popular thing to do. But, in spite
of all the subscriptions and demonstra-
tions, the unreality of the whole affair,
for a great number of those who let
themselves be passively borne before the
wave, has always been evident—behind
the scenes. The political augurs have
hardly been able to meet without laugh-
ing, in private. In public, they have
waved flags and yelled with the rest, and
mouthed astounding rhetoric about that
Empire which is God. In private, one
presumes, they have maintained their old
convictions, or no-convictions, and waited
till the crowds roll by.
* * * *
Take the case of B. R. Wise, Q.G., an
Australian and Attorney-General in the
N.S. Wales Cabinet. He goes with the
crowd. He shouts with the mob for the
Flag, and the Empire, and the obvious duty
of Australia to immolate herself on the
altar of somebody else’s country. But,
twelve years ago, when the Soudan
frenzy had passed, as the Transvaal
frenzy will pass, and a man could express
an honest opinion without losing his
billet or being knocked on the head in
the interests of Freedom (in Africa)
B. R. Wise, now N.S.W. Premier Lyne’s
Attorney-General, sqng a very different
song. In Macmillan’s Magazine for
June, 1888, he pointed out the folly of
the Imperial Federationists of that epoch
in language which is strange reading now.
He was not, indeed, opposed to Imperial
Federation—of a sort. But the ideal
which he advocated contained no aspect of
Australian dependency, no conception of
a loyal Australia doing Britain’s bidding
and sending troops to aid Britain in
foreign wars, however unjust. His view
of Imperial Federation was Parkes’s
view of a Federation of free and indepen-
dent communities, united in one grand
whole. Ho dependency, no subserviency,
no bartering of Australian ideals at the
beck of self-seeking politicians, no wasting
of Australia’s resources in foreign wars
without profit to Australia. The
Soudan expedition was characterised by
Attorney-General Wise in 1888 as a
“ lamentable eccentricity .” How comes
it that in 1900 Attorney - General
Wise is member of a Cabinet which
urges N.S. Wales towards an “ eccentri-
city ” even more lamentable I —towards
what is much more than an “ eccentri-
city ” —towards a crime against free men
with whom Australia has no quarrel 1
Is Attorney-General Wise, of the Lyne
Cabinet, so far removed from Attorney-
General Wise, of the Parkes Cabinet?
Or is he content to be a political chame-
leon, and take this year’s color from his
leader Parkes, and next year’s color
from his leader Lyne ?
• • * «
Attorney-General Wise believed in
alliance with England in 1888, as
he does now. But in 1888 he was
eloquent in support of a party which
saw clearly that “ the full development
of civic life can never be attained in a state
of dependency ; that subjection to a distant
country , however seldom that subjection
may be manifested , exercises a depressing
influence upon the national character , by
creating a false standard of public virtue
and private taste.” In 1900, B. R. Wise
is Attorney-General in a Cabinet which
wastes Australian resources in British
wars at Jingo bidding, without direct
reference to the people ; which looks to
Britain for honors and rewards, and—as
in the matter of Transvaal annexation—
obeys servilely every nod of the ruling
Jingo party in Britain. He will pardon
the dullness of common men who fail to
see in the contrast that consistency of
honest conduct which is prized even by
those who know that consistency of
opinion is an ignoramus’ dream. And if
he hold fast to the words which he wrote
in 1888—
Union vnth England we gladly believe will
come ; but it must be the union of an equal
with an equal , and not the union of a superior
with a subordinate ; so that it is no paradox
to say that the first step towards union must
be —(these are The Bulletin’s capitals)—
SEPARATION—
if the Lyne Cabinet of which he is a
member hold fast to it, —then now is the
time for Attorney-General Wise and his
Cabinet to shout those words from the
platforms and the housetops for the last-
ing benefit of Australia.
Oom John’s Promise.
Premier John Forrest has done At last
what he should have done at first—or done
part of it, at all events. He has publicly
announced that if the Federal Constitution
is altered in one clause, so as to permit
Westralia to retain its separate tariff intact
for five years after joining the Common-
wealth, instead of, as is now proposed,
retaining a separate tariff that diminishes by
instalments spread over five years, he will
be a satisfied man. If his one amendment
is agreed to, he promises to call Parliament
together, re-introduce the Federal Bill as a
Government measure, and do his best to get
it passed and to make Westralia a member
of the union. It is evidently no use further
inquiring now why Big- John didn’t discover
that he wanted this concession at any time
during the three years or more when the
Constitution was being constructed. The
question has been asked very often,
and has evoked no response. Possibly
Forrest doesn’t know himself. And
it is apparently no further use in-
quiring why it was so extremely difficult
to extract from Forrest the assurance that
this one concession was what he wanted,
and was all he wanted, and that, when it was
forthcoming, he would really put his shoulder
to the wheel at last. For many weeks
anonymous letters in the press and ap-
parently-inspired newspaper articles declared
that, this one alteration made, Premier John
would do his best to bring Westralia into
line with its neighbors, but, while Premier
John didn’t publicly say so himself, there
was obviously nothing to be done. The five
eastern provinces could hardly be expected
to start the vast, laborious process of amend-
ing their great work to satisfy Forrest, if
Forrest wouldn’t commit himself publicly to
any assurance that, when the amendment
was done, he would be a whit more satisfied
than before. Now that he has committed
himself, the situation becomes wholly dif-
ferent.
• • * *
The alteration which Premier Forrest
desires'is really a very small affair. In the
present Draft Constitution there is a clause
(No. 95) which reads :
Notwithstanding anything in this Constitution, the
Parliament of the State of Western Australia may, during
the first five years after the imposition of uniform duties
of customs, impose duties of customs on goods passing into
that State and not originally imported from beyond the
limits of the Commonwealth ; and such duties shall be
collected by the Commonwealth.
But any duty so imposed on any goods shall not exceed
during the first of such years the duty chargeable on the
goods under the law of Western Australia in force at the
imposition of uniform duties, and shall not exceed during
the second, third, fourth and fifth of such years respec-
tively, four-fifths, three-fifths, two-fifths, and one-fifth of
such latter duty, and all duties imposed under this sec-
tion shall cease at the expiration of the fifth year after the
imposition of uniform duties.
If at any time during the five years the duty on any
goods under this section is higher than the duty imposed
by the Commonwealth on the importation of the like
goods, then such higher duty shall be collected on the
goods when imported into Western Australia from beyond
the limits of the Commonwealth.
And in place thereof Premier Forrest de-
sires to insert a new clause to this effect :
During a period of five years next following the imposi-
tion by the Parliament of the Commonwealth of uniform
duties of customs, the State of Western Australia may,
notwithstanding anything in this Constitution, continue
to receive the same duties of customs as are in force there
at the passing of this Act, such duties to be collected by
the Commonwealth. The Parliament of Western Aus-
tralia may during that period repeal or alter, but may not
increase any such duties, and at,the end of the said period
such duties shall absolutely cease. During the said
period no duties imposed by the Commonwealth shall be
collected in Western Australia. During the said period
all goods imported into the other States of the Common-
wealth from Western Australia shall be subject to such
duties of customs as the Commonwealth may impose.
* » * *
One notable difference is : the old clause
provides that the duties shall fade away
gradually during five years, and the new
one provides that they shall fade all at once
at the end of five years. Another is that
the new clause provides that Westralia’s
separate duties may “continue” for five
years, whereas the old one said that
they might be “imposed.” The latter
is held to mean that Westralia’s separate
duties would cease automatically as soon
as the Federal tariff came into force,
but that they might be re-enacted, if the
Groper party could fight the necessary Bill
through the Assembly. The statement that
they may “ continue ” means that they will
remain automatically unless the Outlanders
can force a Bill through the Council for their
repeal. This is a difference—yet not so very
much of a difference. If the duties are
oppressive, as the Outlanders allege, by
joining the Commonwealth on Forrest’s
terms they are assured of relief in five years
from the date of the Commonwealth’s tariff,
and that, after all, is not very long. And
they will have the same chance to fight
Boer John in the interval for a reduction of
duties in their own State Parliament as they
have now, and as they would have if West-
ralia stood out. But if Westralia comes in
on Forrest’s conditions, they will always
have a certainty of relief a few years ahead
if they fail by their own efforts. If West-
ralia stands out they will have no such hope
in front of them. Even from the Outlanders’
point of view, the compromise doesn’t look
very bad.
* • * *
But Premier Forrest again begins at the
wrong end. He announces that he will
summon the two Groper Houses and “ do
his utmost to pass the Bill through Parlia-
ment as a Ministerial measure and to obtain
its acceptance by the people of Westralia ”
if his amendment is agreed to. He got one
tariff concession before, and declared him-
self fully satisfied with it, and pledged him-
self to do his utmost to pass the present
Bill as a Ministerial measure, and to secure
its acceptance by the people—and he did
norle elf these things. The new promise is
probably worth a good deal more than the
old one, but even if he goes straight this
time—and it seems reasonably probable that
he will—what is John Forrest’s utmost ?
It may mean that, if the Bill is rejected
by either the Assembly or the Council,
he will dissolve the only dissolvable
House of the two, and appeal to the country
with all his might for such a mandate as will
overawe all opposition, and that, if he fails a
second time, the Forrest Ministry will
resign as the last possible protest against an
obstructive Legislature. Or it may mean, as
it so often has meant in Westralia, that he
will carry the Bill if he can conveniently do
so, and just go on as usual if he can’t.
• * • *
In any case The Bulletin holds that the
Commonwealth Constitution should not be
altered in any way until after the Federation
of the five provinces is accomplished, if it
can possibly be avoided. If the British
Government absolutely insists on altering
the appeal clauses that is a different matter.
Then the trouble becomes unavoidable, and
in that case the Federal delegates should be
instructed to obtain an alteration of the
Westralian tariff clause also, on the ground
that there is no more labor and loss
of time and risk of dissension in
making the two alterations than in
making one. But if the appeal clause is
not altered, then Westralia’s concession
should wait until the first Federal Parlia-
ment meets. To patch the Constitution
afresh before Federation is accomplished
means going over the long and weary busi-
ness of applying to the ten Houses of five
Parliaments for their ten separate consents,
and, if they don’t care to take the responsi-
bility of altering the peopled Bill without
the people’s consent, then it also means ap-
plying to the five communities for their five
separate consents. If anything serious and
unforeseen happens in any of these fifteen
separate stages, one member of the Federa-
tion may drop out after all, and the only
guarantee that another member will drop
in by way of compensation is John Foil
rest’s assurance that he will do his utmost
—exact nature of John’s utmost unspecified.
After the Federation is consummated the
alteration which, it is sincerely to
be hoped, will heal the breach with
Westralia, can be effected with the
sanction of one Parliament and with one
referendum, at which the assent of three
provinces can easily override the objections
of two. And, in this case, even the rejec-
tion of the amendment at a Referendum
won’t block or delay Federation, or bring
about a deadlock. Moreover, it is quite
possible one process will be as rapid as the
other. If the amendment which Fokrest
asks is submitted now, and two provinces
reject it, and three accept it, then there
must either be a Federation of two provinces,
or one of three provinces, or the two dis-
sentients must have still another Referen-
dum to see if they can’t think better of it,
or the three that assented must withdraw
-their assent again, and all will be chaos.
A compulsory amending of the Constitution
now would be ill-luck. A purely voluntary
one would be sheer imbecility.
* * * *
In any case Forrest should move at once.
The Cabinets of the eastern provinces should
give Westralia their joint and several assur-
ance that every man of them will do his best
to obtain the amendment asked for. That
should at least mean that so many prospec-
tive votes and so much prospective influence
in the Federal Legislature were pledged to
vote for the new tariff clause as to make its
acceptance reasonably sure if the Federal
Parliament had to decide the matter. It
should also mean that its acceptance was
made reasonably sure in the State Parlia-
ments if they had to decide the matter.
They should also pledge themselves that, if
the Federal Constitution has to be amended
before the Commonwealth starts, through
the British Government’s insistence on the
alteration in the appeal clauses, they will
request that Government, on their own res-
ponsibility, to alter the Westralian tariff
clause also, and will lend all their weight
to securing the acceptance of the new clause
by their Parliaments and their respective
peoples. They should pledge themselves to
do this, relying merely on Premier Forrest’s
promise to do his utmost, with a hope that
his utmost will be a great deal better than
his last utmost. And if the appeal difficulty
is smoothed over after all, they should
undertake not only to do all in their power to
persuade the Federal Parliament to grant
the concession asked for by Westralia, and
to secure its acceptance by the people, but
that they will start doing this as soon as
Westralia has enacted that it will come in
when tbe concession is granted.
And the instant these assurances arrive
Premier Forrest should call his Parliament
together and introduce a measure to the
effect that Westralia will automatically, and
without any further action on its part,
become a part of the Commonwealth as
soon as an amendment is made in the
Constitution giving Westralia five clear
years of tariff independence. Also he
should send the Bill to his own people
for acceptance on these terms. In this
case some one must go first. Either the
five provinces must make the alteration
trusting that the one province will accept it,
or the one province must make it trusting
that the five will accept it. Either the live
eastern premiers must trust Forrest to do
his utmost or he must trust them to do their


utmost. And considering the relative sizes
of things, and the relative cost of things,
and the fact that of the five eastern
Governments three have gone straight all
the time, and the other two have only
wobbled in moderation, while the western
Government has wobbled in a truly dreadful
fashion, it seems reasonable to expect
Forrest to trust to the pledges of the fed-
erating provinces. Only these pledges
should be given promptly and fully. And
after that Oom John should show his sincer-
ity by getting to work without delay. The
promise to do something after some one else
has done something that will probably take
12 months is too slow a process.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Unsewered Melbourne.
Some of the explanations offered by the Mel-
bourne press as to the why and wherefore of the
smellful city’s smellfulness are quaint and curious
reading. It has been discovered that in Melbourne,
as in Sydney, many city properties are not con-
nected with the sewers even now. Among them
are large hotels, warehouses, and other big build-
ings owned by people of importance. Melbourne
city was properly and completely sewered in Jan-
uary, 1898, and city landlords were ordered to con-
nect their properties by April, 1898, at latest.
Things ended there or thereabouts. Many owners
paid no attention whatever to the order, and the
city authorities knew that they paid no attention,
for their malarious night-cart kept calling regularly
at the unsewered buildings, and is doing so to this
day. It called simply because the owners of the
premises broke the law; if they hadn’t broken it the
vehicle whose two unwholesome wheels circulate
by night would have had no work to do. The city
authorities have now sat up suddenly in their
sleep, and the following incredible explanation
appears in a morning paper
We are informed that in the near future owners who
have been served with what the hoard officials call a
“ final notice ” will receive an intimation from the Town
Hall that unless they connect with the sewers within a
certain time, the City Council’s nightsoil carts will cease
to call. The city was declared a sewered area in January,
1898, and by the end of April in that year all the notices
had been served. Two years have therefore elapsed since
owners received the first official reminder of their civic
duties. In sortie cases, we are informed, excuses have
been offered. The facts that the ownership of certain
properties is in dispute, and that the permission of owners
residing in Europe has to be obtained before work could
be commenced, are among the causes for the prolonged
delay. There are, however, numerous instances in which
criminal carelessness and wilful avoidance of responsibility
towards tenants and the public health are the sole expla-
nation of the position of affairs. In these instance pres-
sure will be brought to bear.
Observe that the law says all premises shall be
connected with the sewers whether the owner gives
his permission or not. Therefore the most inane
and futile excuse tendered above is that the owner
was absent, and the agent had to spend two years
writing to him to ask whether he meant to obey
the law or whether he intended telling the law to
go to perdition. Nothing much more foolish
could have been invented. When the law wants
to arrest a poor man for failing to obey it in any
way it doesn’t spend two years writing to him to
ask if he will consent to be run-in ; it simply
says that the law is mighty and must prevail, and
runs him in. When the law issued its ukase that
all buildings must be properly sewered by April,
1898, it should have impressed on the absentee’s
agent the fact that his owner’s property had to be
sewered by then—that it didn’t require the
owner’s permission to obey the law —that if the
agent hadn’t had sense enough to explain to the
owner in the past three years or more that a com-
pnlsory sewe age system was coming it was his
own funeral —and that if he didn’t connect
straightway it would be done at the owner’s ex-
pense. But Australia is full of good sani-
tary laws which its dirty authorities never
dream of enforcing. So certain absentee
landlords have been allowed to go on mur-
dering Australian citizens with typhoid be-
cause it took years to extract an answer from
them (when they were only five weeks distant by
mail-steamer) as to whether they meant to break
the law or not. And other landlords were allowed
to go on murdering their fellow-citizens without
even this flabby excuse. And the authorities
knew this, and helped these landlords to break
the law by sending round their stealthy midnight
vehicle to do sewerage work on their premises.
And now, instead of getting them all fined to the
utmost extent, and hauling them up afresh
every week to be fined again, or put in gaol
if possible, and making the agents for premises of
disputed ownership do the work right off and pay
it out of the rents, no matter whose rents they are
—instead of this it is merely proposed to send out
another notice at some unspecified date, and inti-
mate that after some other unspecified date the
lurking midnight vehicle will abet the statute-
breakers no more.

Adelaide Tram Syndicates.
S.A. Democikats need to keep a watchful eye on
Adelaide’s Municipal Council. Awhile ago it
bartered away its municipal electric lighting rights
in exchange for thirty free lamps in tlie principal
thoroughfare, and now there is imminent danger
of the tramway systems being handed over to a
London syndicate. One Bingham, a London
civil engineer, came along the other day, and, as
a result of negotiations carried on in comparative
secrecy, the corporation of Adelaide has completed
a preliminary agreementtoconferupon ßingham’s
syndicate of the future—it has not been formed
yet—the right to run eleotric trams through the
streets for thirty years. The Council is to
receive £lOOO a year and 10 per cent, of the
net profits for this concession. For some time
there has been a movement among city and sub-
urban councillors for the formation of a Municipal
Trust to purchase all existing lines, and follow the
precedent so profitably adopted in many other big
cities. This movement was actually started by
one or two metropolitan councillors, who are now
enthusiastically championing Bingham’s proposed
monopoly. A Municipal Trust would require a
capital of about £700,000 to work the existing lines
—54 miles in all—and it has been shown again and
again that it would be a profitable investment
from every standpoint, particularly the stand-
point of the people. Anyway, a Municipal
Trust can obtain the money much moro cheaply
than the proposed syndicate, and, instead
of getting 10 per cent, of the net profits, it
would get all of them. To throw away £1 for 2s.
looks like sheer insanity. Despite this obvious
fact, oertain councillors, hitherto considered
Democrats, are asserting that a Municipal Trust is
impossible, though they are quite unable to say
why. S.A. Democrats are up in arms against the
scheme, and it is now suggested that the tramways
should be taken overby 8. A. Govt., which already
owns every inch of railway line in the province,
and could just as easily own and work the
tramways as well. Anyway, the stinkful
example of the labor - oppressing Melbourne
Tram Company should be sufficient precedent
for squelching any further tramway monopolies
in Australia. Bingham’s concern, by the way, has a
rival. Another private concern, the Westinghouse
Electric Co. of England, is negotiating to get the
trams, paying the Council £lOOO a year and no
share of the profits. So the Council has three
possibilities in view—to get all the profits, or a
tenth of them, or none of them, and it proposes to
take the tenth ! There is the further considera-
tion that if the municipal authority took the work
in hand it can be sure of doing the work. When the
private syndicater takes it up he sometimes gets
his company floated in three years and sometimes
not at all. Auckland (M.L.) has been waiting for
two years, or something like that, for a gorgeous
English company to start making its tramways.
And apparently it may be still waiting two years
hence.

The Compensation Problem.
When the N.S.W. Government has a moment to
spare (it hasn’t had many of late and even the
most rapid Government can’t do impossibilities) it
should start an enquiry into the case of some of
the tenants in the lately-isolated areas of Sydney.
A few of the poorer ones have undoubtedly been
ruined, and ruined undeservedly, by the isolating
process, and these are entitled to liberal compensa-
tion. Some of these sufferers have pathetic stories
to tell. One old woman of over (50 who ran a
cheap eating-house with sixpenny beds attached,
and made a scanty crust for herself and family, is
a case in point. Her story, shortly, is that her
business stopped and her rent went on, and she is
now a hopeless old woman with no money where-
with to start again. Hardly any of the land-
lords in the infected area deserve compen-
sation. With very few exceptions they were
primarily responsible for the plague by
owning ramshackle premises, unsewered, un-
repaired, unpainted, and devoid of any of the
decencies of life. They did this for purposes of
sordid usury, for the dirty tenement which is let
out in one-room houses is about the most profitable
kind of property on earth. The few clean land-
lords should certainly be compensated (at the
expense of the many dirty ones) for any loss they
suffered, but they are so very few that this paper
hasn’t heard for certain of any of them as yet.
As regards the tenants, many were wilfully un-
clean—more unclean than even their awful sur-
roundings compelled them to be—and they deserve
no sympathy worth mentioning. But some few
were simply as dirty as their poverty, and the
dreadful circumstances supplied by their landlords
and their neighbors, made unavoidable, and they
deserve liberal compensation for their losses where
there were any. These few exceptions didn’t live
in these regions from choice. No moderately-
clean person would. They lived there because
there are no one-room houses to be ob-
tained at Darling Point ; because they had
to be near to their employment ; because
they found an opening to make some kind of a
livelihood there, and such openings are so scarce
in these times that even a chance to run a
solvent threepenny restaurant in Tophet isn’t to be
lightly thrown away. Then their unclean land-
lords, and their unclean neighbors, and the
unclean Corporation which tolerated these land-
lords and neighbors, and the unclean Government
which tolerated the Corporation, and the blankly
indifferent community which looked on, and the
Water and Sewerage Board which helped to look
on, brought the plague among them. When they
were isolated they lost their Work in many cases,
and consequently their wages ; or the poor little
business went to pieces; and as everything
stopped except the unclean landlord’s rent —which
usually went on as usual —they were ruined.
Every person in the infected area whose own
premises were clean deserves to have his or
her loss, whatever it was, made good by
the State at the expense of the dirty
people. And the dirty people—especially the
dirty landlords—should also pay the general clean-
ing bill to the last farthing. Some pitiful cases of
distress have arisen through these same landlords.
The Bulletin, by the way, heai’s of one landlord
who has already struck the idea that his property,
by reason of having been cleaned at the public ex-
pense, should bring in more rent than it did before
the State cleaned it! And a crowd of landlords
from the incredible slums of Wexford-street has
already demanded compensation for having its
premises cleaned at public expense. It is time to
take strong measures with this kind of landlord,
and to take them in a hurry.

A Vic. Railway Scandal.
Meagre details of a breach of Victorian public
honor, which if it had occurred in England would
(as in a similar case there a year or two ago) have
ere now resulted in the hauling up of several
highly-placed individuals for contempt of court or
some such offence, are dribbling out in the
southern metropolis. Three or four years ago a
commission was appointed with a Judge as chair-
man, to enquire into the working of the railways,
and to further its objects invitations were issued
to all and sundry to forward information or to
come as witnesses to the enquiry. Railway ser-
vants were, we are informed, specially encouraged
to come forward by a promise of protection against
subsequent persecution for giving unpalatable evi-
dence. Whether the promise was expressly given
is, however, neither here nor there, because such a
promise is impliedly made to everyone giving evi-
dence before a Govt. Board, even as the English
railway directors’ castigation alluded to above
shows. A fortiori, of course, when the Gov-
ernment giving the promise is itself the
employer of the “protected” witness. One rail-,
way workman sent, in a letter to the chairman of
the Vic. Commission, a number of serious criti-
cisms on railway administration. The fact that he
had done so came to the ears of departmental
officials interested, a departmental board sat upon
the “protected” individual, the letter was by
some singular method or other, which needs
some explanation for the sake of the aforesaid
chairman, procured by the departmental board,
and the public-spirited individual who was foolish
enough to rely on the sacred promise of the
Government of Victoria, was recommended for
dismissal (possibly on aside issue, but that doesn t
matter). The railway commissioner considered
the matter and asked the “ culprit ” what he had
to say for himself. He had the temerity to express
the wish that the evidence against him should be
taken on oath. He was dismissed or some-
thing and lost his retiring allowance. Various
members of Parliament were alleged to have
became interested in his case, and the allegation is
now about four years old. It is hard, however,
for any member who depends on the good graces
of railway officials for the lubrication of his de-
partment-trotting routine, to really feel more than
platonically interested in any case which might
lose the aforesaid officials their billets. However,
Dr. Maloney has, we understand, the case in
hand at present, and hope begins ; to pre-
sage a speedy wiping out of this disgraceful
blot on Victoria’s national honor. To the philoso-
pher, the apathy of the Vic. press and people in a
matter which, if proved, would in England have
stirred up such a stink of protest as to have driven
the Government tolerating the injustice into pri-
vate life dishonored, is the most interesting and
amazing part of the affair.
«
Far-western N.S.W. squatters had their chance
when Premier Lyne was at Bourke. Among the
requests they made were (1) that their leases
should run for 50 years ; (2) that rents should be
fixed now for the whole period. As to which —(1)
The existing leases run 28 years. It isn’t very
long where a scheme is involved for developing
the country ; similar country in S.A. is let for
over 40 years. Still, as there is “ tenant right ”
in improvements (that is, the squatter continues
to have a right to the improvements even after
the land is taken) there is little in most of the
protests that the squatter dare not make necessary
improvements on account of the shortness of the
term. The original squatter took up the best
land ; hence it had to be provided that he should
be moved on as soon as the land was wanted. It
was a good axiom for legislators ; the trouble now
is that the move-on is still provided for in
respect of land which is suitable only for
squatters’ use. In the driest parts a big outlay is
necessary ; and big outlays where the profits are
not big ought to mean fairly long terms of lease.
There is not much sense in denying those long
terms with regard to country which cannot be
used by selectors with any prospects of success.
The cry is now for a Commission to guide legisla-
tors. There is, however, the evidence and report
of the South Australian Commission, appointed
to probe a precisely similar difficulty, available for
N.S. Wales’ use. What N.S.W. wants is a
division of the country into the area which is
likely and that which isn’t likely to be wanted for
settlement for, say, 42 years; then the State can
let the squatters have the latter on the long terms
which they plead for. Mistakes will be made in
the division; but that can’t be helped. (2)
To “fix the rent for 50 years” would
mean that if the conditions became worse the
rents would be reduced. Three or four years ago,
though a re-appraisement was almost due, the
afflicted squatters couldn’t wait—they got Parlia-
ment to sanction a special reappraisement. But
if the conditions improved, no Parliament would
venture to “ break a solemn agreement.” Fixing
rent for 50 years is much the same, then, as giving
the squatter a double-headed coin to toss with.

Brisbane, like Sydney and Melbourne, is cut up
into a number of mean and petty municipalities.
When a comprehensive water-supply scheme was
devised for the whole metropolitan district it
couldn’t be entrusted to the councils for the
divided authority couldn’t agree about details, so
a Waterworks Board was formed—a paid body
nominated by Government, where useless and
broken-down politicians find a lazy job in their
old age. A Transit Commission was formed on
the same basis, because the numerous councils
couldn’t agree about keeping order in the streets,
granting cab and ’bus licenses, &c. A Fire
Brigades Board followed because they couldn’t
agree about the work of fire extinction. A Vic-
toria Bridge Board was created because the Vic-
toria Bridge was in two municipalities, and they
couldn’t decide as to the exact plank or half-plank
at which Brisbane’s responsibility ended ani
South Brisbane’s began. A Sewerage Board
must follow, when there is any sewerage,
and all these mean less and less control by the
people, and more perfunctory management by
broken-down party hacks who want to get a few
shillings easily in their old age. About 20 muni-
cipal and shire councils, boards, trusts, commissions
and miscellaneous thingumbob's govern Brisbane
and suburbs among them, and do it from badly to
much worse. A small agitation begins to arise for
a Greater Brisbane Council, elected on a wide
suffrage, which will absorb the Transit Commis-
sion, Fire Brigades Board, Waterworks Board,
Victoria Bridge Board, and all other boards and
planks and shavings, and it is to be hoped it will
be pushed on vigorously. At the same time it will
be a sorrowful reflection on slow-going Sydney if
the little Northern city gets ahead of it in this
matter.

Launceston (Tas.) Examiner lends its feeble sup-
port to the Tory party in the dispute over the
“ right ” of appeal to the British Privy Council:
We are gravely informed by some writers that the right
to appeal to Her Majesty does not exist, that it is only the
right to appeal to her Privy Council. No one, we pre-
sume, with any reasonable conception of Royal functions
imagined that Her Majesty donned wig and gown and
took her seat among the learned members of her Council,
or discussed abstruse points of law with them, therefore
the information tendered was supererogatory. But the
Privy Council is the highest court in the realm, and there-
fore should be open to every British subject.
It is about time that somebody hit this silly old
maundering a severe discouragement on the ear.
The Privy Council isn’t the highest court of the
realm ; the highest court is the Judicial Commit-
tee of the House of Lords, to which Australians
have not access. And if the Privy Council
“should be open to every British subject ” then
there is something very seriously wrong in the
arrangements. The inhabitants of England, Scot-
land, and Ireland have no access to it. It is a
mere colonial tribunal, so the Englishman is
actually supposed to be shut out of the “ highest
court in the realm.” This glorifying of the court
which is “good enough for the colonies” as the
highest judicial institution of the empire is a trifle
ridiculous.

Now that the hidoous slum properties of Wexrord-
street, Sydney, have been compulsorily washed
and fumigated, a deputation of slum landlords
has had the ineffable cheek to wait on Dr.
Graham, M.L.A., and ask his assistance to get
compensation. Also they alleged that “had there
been reasonable notification that the houses were
in an insanitary condition they would have been
glad to have priced them in repair.” These
awful people have the gall to imply that they
didn’t know the state of their, own property, .that
they didn’t know dirt when they saw it, that they
never inspected their own houses, that they
hadn’t heard that the state of Wexford-street
property in general had made it, for years and
years, one of the most notoriously foul slums in
Australia. Twelve of _ them rolled up in .all
the beauty of their pristine ignorance to point
out that they were all quite ignorant of all
these things, and if they had only known that
there was anything wrong with Wexford-street
they would have polished it up till it shone like
Solomon in all his glory. Twelve of them never
went to look at their properties to see if there was
anything wrong. Twelve of them never called
round for the rents and smelt their own tenements.
Twelve of them would never, never, never have let
things be like that if they had only known, but
they thought Wexford-street was like Potts Point,
and really believed that Attorney-General Wise
and Chief Justice Darley and Premier Lyne lived
there, and that the Bench and Bar in general were
only waiting till there were vacancies to move in
there also. And twelve of them wanted compen-
sation for having their premises cleaned for tnem
by the State. Also twelve of them were very
much shocked.

When N.S.W. Parliament meets will some mem-
ber move for a return of the owners of properties
in all the infected areas of Sydney and also in
other well-known slums —city and suburban ? The
result would probably fully explain why _ the
various councils —Sydney City Council especially
—never enthused about cleaning up. It would
also, probably, explain why the Sydney City
Improvement Board died a natural death some
years ago. The Board had power to order the
demolition of hopelessly awful properties, when
they were reported to it by the Council, but as the
Council refused to report anything to it the
Improvement Board adjourned some years
ago, and never met again. Common report
has it that the worst slums in Sydney
are largely owned, or controlled as agents,
by civic magnates. They couldn’t well report
other people’s slums for demolition without
reporting their own also, and they couldn’t well
order other people to clean their slums without
cleaning their own. And the solemn fact is that
dirt pays. The way to make 10 or 15 per cent,
out of house property is to buy or build a cheap
tenement as near the city as possible, crowd as
many rooms as possible on the ground space—even
by leaving out all the decencies of life, let it to as
many poor tenants as can be compressed into the
space, and pay nothing for cleaning, papering or
painting, and as little as possible for repairs. The
unclean tenement hangs together about as long as
the clean one, and the kind of tenants wanted are
always obtainable in abundance. There is a groat
silence about the owners of these dens. Even
when 12 Wexford-street proprietors rolled up the
other day to demand compensation, their names
weren’t given by the daily papers which reported
the event.

Considering the rage of ex-Premier Reid’s organ,
Sydney E. News, against Lyne for his “uncon-
stitutional” conduct in submitting the N.S;W.
finances to a committee of experts, and thus
casting doubts on the wisdom of Parliament, it is
interesting to note that Reid did the same in
1896, and the News cordially approved. And
considering Reid’s fury because Lyne nominated
one of the three experts himself without reference
to Reid, it is interesting to note that Reid nomi-
nated all three of his experts himself without
reference to anybody. Reid asked his .three
carefully-selected experts to guarantee that his
surplus for 1896 was genuine. And they re-
fused to do anything of the kind—“ We
feel we are unable witli the information at our
command to give an answer,” was their reply.
But the appalling cheek of George Reid rose
superior even to this. He had selected his. own
judges, and asked them to say that his one great
surplus was a genuine, honest surplus, and pre-
sumably gave them all the evidence of its honesty
that he could raise. And they had replied that
the evidence was insufficient. And Reid pre-
sented this blasting document to the Assembly
and said, with the light of truth and enthusiasm
blazing in his glassy eye, that he was completely
vindicated, and that the report cleared his char-
acter of all suspicion.
The Efficacy of Prayer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" . . . . Prayers moved Omnipotence to espouse the
cause of those by whom and on whose behalf they were
uttered.” —Rev. Dunstan in a recent sermon at Enmore,
Sydney.
(Feeble and timid must be the tale—
I shrink from the task dismaj 7 ’d—
For language falters and accents fail
At the Greatness to be pourtray’d.)
The Good God sat on His Great White Throne
In the midst of a million suns,
The light of a myriad gems unknown
Shone over the Shining Ones ;
For thronging the Halls of Eternal Space
A legion unnumbered stood,
Each one a God of benignant face
In that Heavenly Brotherhood.
An angel came to the Great White Throne
And a moment’s audience pray’d—
He knelt not down, and he lay not prone,
Nor other obeisance made ;
For the Infinite God and His Infinite Peers
Dwell neither as high nor low,
No worship is there, no dread, no fears,
No caste such as mortals know.
And lo ! the resplendent angel spake,
Each word was as gold refined,
His voice did the sweetest music make
As the Infinite One inclined :
“I have sped through the maze of a million suns
From a speck on.the rim of space,
Far out where the uttermost ether runs
And planets their courses trace.
In my wake I have seen great suns drop out,
Obeying Eternal Law-
Systems and comets mixed in the rout —
And Chaos again I saw.
But this, as Thou knowest, must ever be—
Though the universe rock and swing,
Thy Law is Thy Law unalterably—
And from ruin new systems spring.
On a speck as I passed lo ! a creature pray’d,
And cried in his earth-born pride :
‘ Omnipotence, moved by our prayers, is made
To fight on our praying side ! ’
For the creature’s race was at war with a race
That fought against hopeless might,
And the creature held that Omnipotence place
Was to back-up its side in the fight.”
The Good God spake from His Great White
Throne,
And unto His Peers He said:
“ So little is there to these creatures known
Their Pride is by Ignorance fed.
The speck where they dwell is their universe,
Its masters and lords are they,—
They believe we exist but to bless or curse
E’en as they may beseech and pray ;
But the greatest ‘ sin ’ and the ‘ noblest ’ deed
With which their world has rung,
The failures their mortal fate has decreed,
And the triumphs their bards have sung,
They weigh alike in th’ Eternal Scales —
They are all but incidents—-
But cause and effect of the Law that prevails
Thro’ the measured March of Events ! ”
Feeble and flattering is human speech,
Tho’ the words may witli passion burn —
But this is the truth the tale may teach,
This the lesson you may learn :
The infinite calm of the Infinite God,
In His infinite universe,
Is ne’er disturbed by a sentient clod,
Moved either to bless or to curse !
