SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “ Queen Elizabeth."
Royal “ The Manoeuvres of Jane.”
Lyceum “ The Irishman.”
Palace *■ Uncle Tom’s Cabin.*
Tivoli Rickards’ Co.
Criterion “ Robbery Under Arms.”
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ What Happened to Jones.”
Theatre Royal “The Ladder of Life.”
Bijou Rickards’ Variety Co.
Alexandra “ Hands Across the Sea.”
Jones’s comedy, the “Manoeuvres of
.Tane,” brought an unusually large audience to
Sydney Royal last Saturday. The plague had
practically left off for two days, and the d.c.
crowd, which has mostly poured Condy’s fluid over
itself and concealed itself in terror behind the
gas-meter with a prayer-book, came out in
strength, and could be heard stating to its friends
on the stairs that it wasn’t at all frightened. It
was very well repaid, too, for its valor in coming
out and defying the bearded germ in its lair.
“ Jane ” has the great advantage of being unusual.
It is in four acts, which is seldom the case with
comedies, and it contains 21 characters. Even in
the very last act the audience keeps making the
acquaintance of perfectly new characters, who
exist and draw their salary merely to come in and
cough in the drawing-room, and leave again. Jones
isn’t a short, crisp writer like Pinero. He unfolds
his story in a large, leisurely way—in fact, Jones’s
joke falls like the gentle drizzle from heaven,
and Jones’s spacious and unhurried style is refresh-
ing if you don’t get it too often. Jones’s Jane
is almost the best opening Miss Grace Noble has
had, and she makes excellent use of it. She is the
spitfire daughter of a rich and bilious man with a
bald head (Leslie Victor is the bilious man), and
having been fired out of various schools as a dis-
turbing element, and having fallen in love with a
disreputable party unknown, she is consigned to
the care of a lady in reduced circumstances to be
firmly treated. The reduced lady promises to be
firm, and to bring Jane up with an axe if neces-
sary, and to reduce her to such a state of subjec-
tion that she will enter the room humbly on all-
fours if so instructed. Miss Bessie Thompson
makes this promise with the dangerous suavity of
a large and bitter but polite cat. And Jane, on
her side, is just about to break everything by way
of asserting herself when she finds that her lover
is in the house applying for the billet of Lord
Bapchild’s steward, and that weak and pious
young peer is enquiring into his morals. Where-
upon Jane repents publicly of all her past wicked-
ness with tears, and hugs her lover in secret.
* • * •
Thereafter it is decided that as Jane is
rich and Bapchild is also rich, as well as weak,
and oily about the hair, and given to delivering
long sermons, they had better marry. And Jane’s
companion decides that she would rather marry
Bapchild herself. And that miserable peer is
kidnapped in a rowing boat by the companion,
and is out all night through various accidents, and
is so crushed by the penniless dependent’s shrieks
about her lost character that he agrees to marry
her. And Jane elopes to meet her steward, and
through more accidents is belated in a public-
house where a beery rustic hugs her, and the
landlord says he has no very high opinion of
ladies who are out alone in strange public-houses
late at night. And Jane’s lover can’t come to her
till she has been alone in the beer-shop till
near midnight, because an interfering small
girl who tells tales and listens at keyholes has
pursued him from inquisitive motives, and he
is spending his time trying to lose her. And at
home the mysterious disappearance of everybody,
including the listening child, has reduced the
family to tears. But everybody turns up in time,
including the weak, draggled peer in the custody of
his future wife ; and the future wife’s mother who
says she will live with them after marriage;
and Jones’s comedy ends pleasantly, though none
too definitely.
* * * *
A good many people get a show in this
play. Miss Noble’s Jane and Mrs. Brough as the
governess, and Brough as the weak peer, are all
food. Brough, in fact, is more than good. His
iord Bapchild is apparently meant to show the
awful results of a pious home-training on a weak
and bashful young man with red hair and
a tendency to blush uneasily on one leg.
Bapchild can almost rank with the Private
Secretary as a character study. Leslie Victor,
Mario Majeroni, Dartrey and Lovell fill the
parts of various ordinary gentlemen of the 19th
century in a pleasing fashion. Miss Bessie Thomp-
son furnishes an admirable study of the smooth,
affable, deadly person who is going to reform
Jane; and the dreadful child who listens at key-
holes and carries tales is represented by Evelyn
Martheze. That sour, precocious infant is next
best to Brough as a character study. She is a
horrible and clever example of the child who
ought to be taken bathing in deep water at a very
early age with a large dumb-bell in her pocket.
There is a heartless fascination about her
manner of looking sideways out of her hair.
Also, her crawling method of coming up be-
hind people who are busy hugging each other,
and leering at them in a hump-backed sort of way
when discovered, makes the public feel soft, cold-
footed things walking down its spine. Most of
the public has, at some time or other, suddenly
stopped hugging somebody to find itself being
looked at like that, or thereabouts.
The “ emergency man ” is still ejecting
the noble but impecunious Irish peasant at Sydney
Lyceum with the vigor indicated in these columns
last week. The “Irishman,” and almost every
other Hibernian drama of The Bulletin’s
acquaintance, tells a very remarkable story about
the alleged state of the green but distressful isle.
Details may vary, but these seven leading features
are almost always there:
(1) The Irish landlord, however rich he may be, never
gets his rent.
(2) If he is a good landlord he lives by forgiving his
tenant for non-payment.
(3) If he is a bad one he lives by tearing down his own
property In order to get Mulcahy or Hoolan out of the
place.
(4) The good and honest tenant is never able to pay his
rent. He works hard but he never has any money.
(5) The agent is always a wicked man.
(G) The average landlord has only one tenant—the one
who is behind with his rent—and he keeps six or eight
minions in his employ for the purpose of ejecting him and
his aged father. He never lets the house to anj'one else,
and no one ever lets a house to the ejected one. The
latter either dies, or disappears, or thereafter earns an ap-
parently comfortable living by waiting for the landlord
with a gun Why he didn’t give the gun to the landlord
to square the rent is a mystery.
(7) Occasionally there ‘is a wicked tenant. He is never
embarrassed, and he doesn’t work. He is never asked for
Tu- fl e hves and I)as his whisky by walking close
behind the agent, and giving him bad advice. The agent
keeps him out of his large salary, and the landlord pays
the large salary out of the rent he doesn’t get from Mul-
cahy, who was turned out.
There are some other points about Irish drama,
but these seven will do to go on with.

The heavy-going historical play of “Queen
Elizabeth is to be staged at Her Majesty’s,
Saturday. It is a long time since
Elizabeth unfolded itsdreary but richly-clothed
dimensions in Sydney, and the only imaginable
reason for its appearance now is that Nance
U Neil s stately figure will look well sitting on a
throne. Miss O Neil has become such a favorite
that the public will probably roll up strongly to
see her ruling England, and issuing crisp orders
for the obliteration of foreign Powers, and the
destruction of armadas, and the annexation of
places, and the arrest and execution of people in
general. In fact, it is quite likely that she will
look much better in that capacity than the real
Elizabeth did. Elizabeth’s majesty was apt to be
marred by a sudden suspicion that the Lord
High Treasurer had recklessly wasted 3d. or
even 4£d.

Robbery Under Arms,” in its prison-
scene, has toned down considerably since earlier
years, when, for the sake of the bloodthirsty boy
m the gallery, it was necessary to give plenty of
Repentance to antidote the gay wrong-doin 0 * of
the previous acts. Now the Gallery Boy has the
Iransvaal to romance about, so Dick merely hears
the birds singing as they did of yore for a few
minutes instead of for an hour or two as in former
The fact that the birds sing as of yore has
a depressing effect on most criminals ; evidently
most birds sing indifferently. The present render-
ing of the “Robbery” would be better if Dam-
pier could manage to do most of the parts himself
—especially the women parts and the Jim whom
everybody loves. Dick and the two policemen,
ana perhaps a few other personages, might be re-
tained, _ but especially the two policemen.
M Uinnis and O’Hara are the real props and sup-
ports of “Robbery.” The policeman who is played
by George Buffer (the writer doesn’t remember
whether he is M'Ginnis or O’Hara) leaves an
especially favorable impression in this scribe’s
mind, whichever he is.
** * *
A wise manager generally contrives to
have one or two poor voices in his entertainment
to show up the good ones in greater relief. Sydney
Palace manager is not wise. The voices that
are not golden are always silver—the best of their
kind. He gives no smaller change. The co. gives
a pantomime sketch, and Mr. Rickards, in his
odd moments, is asked to look in and see how it is
managed. The Human Frog contorts his boneless
being as usual. He must have an excellent
memory, for he never forgets how to untie himself
after he has secured his body and all his limbs in
iiyy 6 . The co. has determined to give
Uncle Tom later on—but it is no good meeting
trouble half-way. Plague and death and war and
Uncle Thomas are things to be borne manfully
when they arrive, but not to be anticipated.
** * *
Rickards new Tivoli is about the coolest
theatre in Sydney, and among its other attrac-
tions it has the smallest performer in Sydney—
little Roy Cushin. Wallace King has returned
£om Melbourne for the manyeth time to sing
111 tell her when we meet,” and one cannot help
wishing, for Wallace’s sake, that the meeting will
take place after dark, where she can only hear his
voice and not see him in the terrible rig that a
soulless Rickards has made him wear. The Mar-
tmettis have ceased to heave pillows, and now pour
powders over each other in immense quantities and
at great length. The Newsky Family, Browne,
and I rancie Adler do various things which help
to distract the mind from the horrible advertise-
ment curtain.
* « *- *
Charles Arnold’s luck, which has always
been good what time Australia has known him,
promises to be better than ever this journey.
There are no very obtrusive points of brilliancy
about What Happened to Jones,” at Melbourne
Princess’s, but the Easter rush to witness that
cheerful, easy-going farce was a more violent epi-
demic of boodle than the Firm could achieve with
any piece of the same class during its many
years experience in the same house. A catchy
title, perhaps, helps to explain the crowd at the
early door. It suggests strange happenings, and
invites enquiry. Anyhow, the story of the
Yankee bagman who sports the black cloth
gaiters of a blameless life and represents himself
as two different doctors of divinity, is in a fair
way to hustle along for some weeks. Besides,
there must be grit in the performance, seeing that
it improves on acquaintance. The farce fits the
co., and the co. gives it an air of human probability
and natural madness. Charles Arnold Jones is a
8> likeable live man—rather than a mummer
on the stage. Fred. B. Sharp, as the troubled
professor, George Willoughby, in the thankless
character of a lost identity on the Indian war-
path, likewise Frank Denton, the young man
who resembles George Majeroni, and E. W.
Thomas, as a fat, infuriated constable, are come-
dians of the kind that this country has nothing to
say against. Two bright visions from Sydney—
Miss Hope Mayne and Miss Bensusan—do duty
as Ebenezer’s daughters, and their duty is a plea-
sure to see. _ Miss Agnes Knights would be quite
satisfactory if she didn’t drop into Miss Dot
Frederic’s fault of laughing without provocation.
Two other ladies give excellent character sketches.
Little Miss Ada Lee makes the small part of the
housemaid look large, and Miss Sallie Booth, as
the spinster sister who hopes she hasn’t changed
much in the last 30 years, is an absolutely con-
vincing old girl. Her elderly playfulness inspires
the present writer with awe.
** * *
George Musgrove will take up the lease of
the Princess’s in about six weeks time, but his
first intentions appear to be shrouded in mystery.
Thus far the only business sign of his near arrival
is the serving of an injunction, or something, upon
ex-partner Williamson, and it is said that
Musgrove will stage a financial melodrama at the
Law Court Theatre before settling down to the
ordinary cares of theatrical management in Aus-
tralia.
*
At Melbourne Royal the career of “ When
the Lamps are Lighted ” will stop short to-morrow
(Friday), and in the meantime John Sheridan’s
advt. wants to know with considerable warmth
whether the drama aforesaid is not the most
laughable thing going. The Bulletin would say
that the “ comic relief ” succeeds in leavening the
whole lump, but the lump might be a lot lighter
if it were leavened a little more. Sheridan has put
in a good time —four months altogether—at the
Royal, so the exact dimensions of his humor-
ousness are not worth measuring now that he is
going away from Melbourne for a while. We can
run the tape over him when he comes back. On
Saturday another company will appear in “The
Ladder of Life,” which is quite a different matter
from Bland Holt’s “ Span of Life.” “The Ladder”
is a war-drama, dealing with British valor outside
of the interminable South African trouble. In
fact, it goes back to the storming of the Dargai
Heights, and should afford the tired public a
pleasant relief from the recent monotony of gal-
lant attacks on kopjes.
** # *
“For Queen and Country” is doing its
best to whet the dulled edge of patriotic appetite
(or something to that effect) at the Alexandra, but
for the present Melbourne refuses to bite greedily
at Briton v. Boer drama. Moreover, the “Aleck”
audiences who lately listened with some respect
to a fairly reasonable caricature of Kruger are not
impressed by the bloodthirsty conversation of an
Oom Paul whose mere make-up stamps him as a
cheap invention of the enemy. All the same, the
broadsword combat, and the plunge into the tank,
and the pretty general efficiency of the acting are
received with thanks.
* • • *
On Saturday the Bellewesque heroism of
Alfred Woods, supported by the full force of Miss
Maud Williamson and co., will get a better oppor-
tunity to score in “Hands Across the Sea.”
Pettitt’s drama was a rather powerful improba-
bility when Charles Warner staged it at the Royal
many years ago, and if the Union Jack incident is
worked for all it is worth the house will probably
get a violent attack of Rule Britannia—on Satur-
day, anyhow.
** # #
Ludwig Amann, the flexible foreigner who
Sves a wonderful impersonation of manager
ickards when he isn’t producing less faithful
likenesses of other notable individuals, is the lead-
ing item at the Bijou just now. Melbourne’s
recollection of Biondi is not shattered by Amann,
but the versatility of Ludwig’s face and the up-to-
date character of his disguises make him a popular
attraction. The present writer has a notion that
Amann’s awful Queen Victoria is rather rough on
our long - established figurehead of the Empire,
nevertheless he must admit that the public make
a point of standing up when the band plays, which
is truly loyal evidence of their inability to see the
joke.
* * *-
Now that Lalla Miranda has conquered
critical audiences at Amsterdam and Brussels
with her sweet, flexible voice, and is about to
make a first appearance in London as the new
Australian Nightingale, her early admirers must
be wondering more than ever why the graceful
little artist —yet another Jewess, of course—had
to wait so long for her success. The cognoscenti
who rave about the “remarkable purity and
range ” of Mile. Miranda’s notes, and praise her
“ sympathetic and attractive figure” as Ophelia,
in Ambrose Thomas’s “Hamlet,” are only
repeating The Bulletin’s wayback verdict that
to see Lalla Miranda sing is almost as delightful
as to hear her do it.” The Melbourne girl warbled
[ lk f a bird when she was fifteen years of age, and
had her voice been only a little heavier she might,
with her natural intelligence and pretty Spanish
face, have taken the position of “another Patti”
years ago. The Bulletin will expect to find the
cableman (who sometimes speaks truth) recording
a great debut for Lalla Miranda at the Covent
Garden house next month.

If all things were equal, the most prosperous
bank would be the one which earned the biggest per-
centage, and the least prosperous the one with the
smallest earnings. But as things are not equal there are
certain reservations to be made. The Queensland
National Bank gets its reconstructed deposits at the
cheapest rate of all ; if it paid the same rate as many of
the other banks it would be much nearer the bottom of
the list than it is. The Commercial of Australia, Colonial,
A.J.S., KS. and A., and Australian Deposit and Mortgage
Bank get their reconstructed deposits a little below the
market rate, though not so cheaply as the Queensland
National. The Commercial Bank of Australia is uncertain.
That part of it which is called the Commercial Bank of
Australia earned last half-year at the rate of £4 Is 7d per
cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds, and that part
of the Bank which is called the Assets Trust may possibly
have lost so much as to reduce the real, earnings to very
much less it is impossible to say, in the absence of the
information which the directors should give but don’t.
The Commercial of Australia is put in as a mere uncer-
tainty. As regards the Bank of New Zealand the profits
are calculated on the private shareholders’ funds only The
£2,000,000 provided by the State for 10 years at 4 per cent.,
and the £500,000 provided permanently by the State at 31
per cent, are rather in the nature of a 10 years’ fixed
deposit and a perpetual deposit than capital, for they
receive a fixed rate of interest just as ordinary deposits
do, and have no share in the earnings beyond that. So
the profits are taken to be the earnings after paying inte-
rest on these two sums. These reservations detract some-
what from the value of the table. Still, as regards the
other banks, it contains a good deal of information about
the relative prosperity of the various institutions. And
an important feature is that all the banks, save two, show
a great improvement as compared with last June, when a
similar list was published. The two exceptions are the
Bank of Victoria, the earnings of which show a decline
of 9d. per cent., and the West Australian Bank, which
shows a drop of £4 Is. sd. per cent.

The improvement in the last two years is certainly
remarkable, and the Colonial Bank’s earnings are now
equal to £5 12s. 7d. per cent, per annum on the share-
holders’ funds (Preference capital, £304,044; ordi-
nal capital, £98,392; reserve fund, £5000). A
dividend at 4 per cent, per annum on the pre-
ference capital uses up £6OBl ; an addition of £5OOO is
made to reserve fund raising it to £lO,OOO ; and the other
£391, added to previous balance, leaves £2OOB to carry
forward. The total reserves are therefore £12,008. This
isn’t very much certainly, and though the Colonial’s re-
serve before the smash was only £287,500 it will take a
long time at the present rate to get back to any-
thing like the old position. Still good progress is
being made, and if the dividends are only kept low as at
present, and the effort to rebuild the reserve fund is
carried on persistently, the prospects look very good.

In other respects the Colonial’s balance-sheet
wears a promising aspect. Note circulation has in-
creased largely. New deposits have gone up from
£1,292,041 six months ago to £1,634,221. The old recon-
structed deposits are now down to £857,087, a reduction
of £lBO,OOO during the half-year ; and as the amount still
unpaid is so small, it seems probable that the institution
will, in another year or two, get clear of its reconstruction
troubles altogether—that is to say, if the present pros-
perity continues. Cash and liquid securities stand at
£788,425 —an increase of £126,000. The one subject of
doubt is—are any of the profits, and, if so, how much of
them, earned, not in the ordinary course of business, but
by buying up the old deposits at a depreciated figure ?
This question applies to several reconstructed institu-
tions. It is only interesting as affecting the outlook ; if
much or any of the profits arise from this purely tempor-
ary source, the permanent prospect isn’t all that it seems.

Last year, therefore, Perkins and 00. Ltd., realised nearly
42 per cent, on its paid-up capital of £58,334. Curiously
enough this prosperous concern has never yet started a
reserve-fund, and the £58,308 which it has accumulated
is still described as “balance carried forward.” The co.
certainly ought to transfer the £58,000 to a reserve.
“ Reserve-fund ” has at least the merit of sounding fixed,
and solid, and permanent. “ Balance carried forward ”
sounds like a transitory sort of thing, that might be
“ blewed ” any day in paying a 100 per cent, bonus. Dur-
ing the seven years, Perkins and Co., in addition to pay-
ing large dividends, has come within £26 of doubling its
capital. Old “ Jonah ” Perkins might be an unlucky man
aboard ship, but his name seems to bring unlimited good
fortune to a brewery.

“Bayswater”: (1) Wouldn’t buy any more of
the shares mentioned. No special hurry to sell, and
Sou may possibly get a better price for them by waiting a
ttle. (2) The deposits of the first bank mentioned aren’t
reckoned quite safe for 20s. in the £, but as likely as not
they will pay that. Even if they only paid 16s. in the £
ultimately you couldn’t complain at the price. (3) E. S.

• * • •
British B.H. directors cable out that the year’s
profit has been £72,088, and the last half year’s £43,658 ;
which is by far the co.’s best in its 12 years’ eventful his-
tory. A div. of 2s. 6d. is announced ; of this, it is ex-
plained, Is. properly belongs to this year (the co.’s year
ends Dec. 31). The mine has big bodies of good milling
stuff—not rich, but better than most to handle. Com-
pare the results with those of its neighbor, the Junction.
British (figures are one week’s) : 2325 tons crude ore
milled for 313 tons first concentrates and 129 tons seconds.
That means about five tons of ore to make one of concen-
trates. Junction: 1314 tons ore milled for 183 tons product
(126 tons firsts, 57 tons seconds). That is, about 7J tons
of ore to make one of concentrates. The Junction makes
no cleaner extraction ; but all this extra treatment, all
this extra crushing and jigging, involving extra loss of
metal, has to be done in the case of the Junction ore in
order to get out the refractory garnet sandstone, rhodon-
ite, &c. The British ore is not rich ; it averages only
about silver and 17 per cent. lead. But, being a
comparatively easily-dressed ore, it is worth much more
than ore of nominally equal value from some other mines.
The Junction ore is worth 13 to 14 per cent, lead, and 9
to lOoz. silver—less than the British, it is true ; but the
difference in the metallic values is not nearly so important
as the difference in the composition of the ores. Moral:
Don’t place much dependence upon assay-values.
* m m m
Talking of assay-values, it is interesting to note
that the B.H. Central (Sulphide Corporation), which pays
very big divs., has an ore worth about 14oz. silver
and 16 per cent. lead. This is not high ; but the
great size of the lode and the excellence of all the arrange-
ments add to the value of the ore, which is not of par-
ticularly free-milling quality. Its neighbor, the South, is
producing ore worth 9’2oz. silver and 22 per cent, lead ;
and that is so good that shareholders might reasonably
have expected even better results, notwithstanding that
the ore is generally very hard. There certainly is room
for improvement.
* * * *
The jury which inquired more-or-less into the
Balmain colliery-shaft accident expressed the opinion
that miners using shafts ought to be medically examined
every six months ; and I have been getting practical
miners’ opinions about it (a Bulletin correspondent
writes). Most of those who don’t say “ Rubbish ! ” say
“ Rot! ” The older the miner, I observe, the more
emphatic is he inclined to be. The most representative
man put it this way : “Occasionally a captain goes off his
dot, no doubt; and then the ship may go to the bottom.
Therefore, examine captains every six months —and don’t
trouble about the ships 1 Run the tape over the captains,
if you like, but it’s a darn sight more necessary to look
after the hulls. So with mining. It isn’t the men who
are wrong so often as the appliances. Make them safe,
and we’ll take the risks of our own and even, our mates
sickness." _
The Balmain colliery aocident has had one effect
—a regulation (hanging fire for some time) has been
gazetted in Westralia limiting the number of men who
may be hauled-up or lowered in a cage at one time to four.
Miners, however, growl, because it means delay.
* * * *
At last there is definite news of systematic
work begun at Wertago copper claims, outside Wil-
cannia. Remarkably good assay returns have been had,
but the formation is very much disturbed. Shaft-sinking
is being done, with results that make the field well worth
watching. * * *
Dear Bulletin. —Are mines only inspected
after a serious accident has happened ? I know a mine
in Gundagai district in which the mining regulations are
utterly disregarded. It was inspected about nine months
ago, and a number of alterations were ordered, but no
attempt has been made to see if they have been effected.
I was below a few days ago. Entered workings by ladder-
way in main uncentered shaft, made an inspection, and
on arrival at surface again had a look round expecting
to find permanently located a doctor, coroner, and half-a-
dozen life-assurance agents.

Dear Bulletin. — Re “Mount Garnet’s” letter
(B. 17/3, ’00) let me point out a few errors. He says “ Mt.
Garnet is 28 miles from the C.R.M.’s branch”; real
distance is just under 20; also Mt. Cardwell is only 10
miles from the head of the C.R.M. branch. And the
route via Atherton is not 25 miles shorter ; at the outside
it is but ten miles less, and another thing, the railway
would be a second edition of the Cairns line —very severe
grades and enormous expense would be involved to build,-
it, as they have any amount of tunnels and very large
cuttings. The C.R.M. hasn’t a tunnel on its line.
Either “ Mt. Garnet ” is a chump or else he owns a cat
located somewhere on the Atherton route.— Flabby Paul.
• • * •
The Berry Consols does not long outlast the
Madame Berry, the parent of the famous Oreswick group.
The Consols ground is exhausted, and in what was for-
merly the No. 1 co.’s ground there is no more prospecting
to be done. For another six months divs. will be paid
from stuff exposed ; after that . But as £349,500
divs. have been paid on a called-up capital of £67,ooo(the
water and wash gave trouble at first) the concern has been
a.very good advt. for mining. To the end of the co.’s
half-year the output of gold was £886,642 ; so that practic-
ally 40 per cent, of earnings have gone to shareholders.
» * * •
Bendigo’s best recent development has been in
the Eureka—one of the mines which has been making
steady calls for years while it groped down from 2700 ft. to
3030 ft., and felt about in drives and crosscuts there. The
stone is big ; where the first crushing was got the cap of
the reef was about sft. high and 15ft. across. This first
crushing gave 88joz. from 77 tons. The co. deserves
something good. Though only a small concern, like most
at Bendigo, it has called up £57,750, while its divs. have
been under £lB,OOO. Its neighbor, the New Chum Rail-
way, which is down 200 ft. deeper, seems also to be taking
a deserved change for the better.
* * * *
Argo G.M. Co. —Veteran J. A. Wallace has re-
tired from this concern disappointed and after a loss of
£19,000. Last year he agreed to put a dredge on it, for
24,000 shares ; he has now withdrawn his dredge and sent
back the shares. Various excuses were given at the
meeting—one that with a bucket-dredge the ground
could have been worked at one-third of the actual cost.
Then there was an insinuation that all the gold had not
been got—the Directors did not attend the clean-ups ;
one nnan believed there was £150,000 worth of gold in the
ground still. Wallace replied that the people who talked
of working ground at 3d. per yard never had any pay-
sheets to meet, or other troubles of that kind. lie
originally thought the ground would pay and was sorry
for the shareholders and himself that it had been proved
otherwise ; he never attended the clean-ups of his own
dredging claims ; but trusted to his representative.
* * * *
The Vic. Govt, had, at time of writing, almost
come to terms with Mr. Shedden, the agent for the
mysterious English syndicate which proposes to open up
Mount Deddick with a narrow-gauge railway, 94 V miles
long. The draft contract stipulates for an expenditure of
£300,000 on the railway, and a further outlay of £50,000
on the 1000 acres of land granted to the hopeful specula-
tors for a term of 30 years, the Govt, reserving the right
to acquire the line upon a valuation at the end of 15
years. Shedden will have to plank down £lO,OOO in
guarantee of the syndicate’s existence, prior to his going
to London, on three months “ leave,” to get the contract
signed. The story of the far-off capitalists who paid
an English expert £3OOO to discover a rich copper field,
where Vic. experts had failed to_gce anything definite, will
continue to sound incredible until it is corroborated by
that £lO,OOO guarantee. Up to now, the people who have
talked about Mt. Deddick didn’t seem to dream of putting
up the boodle which speaketh so much louder than words.

It isn’t only surface reefs that give handsome
specimen stone at Bendigo. In the Unity at 2260 ft. the
other day a small show piece was broken, the gold form-
ing a slender ring about the stone, while between the
metal and the matrix is a hard shiny casing. Proposed
to send it to Paris, of course.
« • * *
Dear B.—Lonergan, of Mount Lyell, who knows
just as much of mining as any other new chum at the
game, signed bitter letters to the Arous opposing W. R.
Wilson’s scheme of Lyell amalgamation, and that
journal published, among other things, Lonergan’s glori-
fication of Lonergan. According to Lonergan, Lonergan
had always known the value of N. Lyell and the S6uth and
some others which, like the North and South, were most
hopeless shows until quite recently. Lonergan concluded
with some remarks to the effect that Lonergan had borne
nasty insinuations for some years from the Lyell crowd,
and now that his energy and foresight and faith and hope
and trust and other things had been rewarded, he didn’t
propose to cut the reward up. The amateur miner who
“ always knew the value of the mine ” is not a new person-
age. The Boulder by sheer accident struck a phenomenal
lode, and immediately the men who had traded off the
cat achieved fame as mining geniuses. The building of
the N. Lyell’s foolish railway, and the purchase of its
steeplechasing steamboat, and the project of its unneces-
sary smelters, are the result of the late lamented Orotty’s
recognition of the impossibility of ever getting a seat on
the Lyell board. Also, when the N. Lyell was floated in
London, the bush fires and the new track hadn’t found the
present lode, therefore the N. Lyell was nothing to talk
about at the time. The South Lyell’s first ore was got
very recently at some hundreds of feet below the surface,
and until that ore had been cut the South was simply a
cat. Therefore I rise again to protest against Mr. Lonergan
“ knowing the ore was always there,” and furthermore
beg leave to sniff disdainfully at Mr. Lonergan’s “ fore-
sight and faith and hope,” and all the rest of it, though I
don’t deny his energy.— Mainshaft.
» * • *
Said to be now over 300 dredging co.’s in M.L.,
and there will probably be 500 before the year is out. The
thin-skulled public has entered spiritedly into the allur-
ing gamble, and never since the golden days of old has
the market showed such a tendency to yellow fever. The
most remunerative of the gold-scooping dredges is Hart-
ley and Riley’s, whose shares have fluctuated between £l5
16s. and £lB 125., and publication of their splendid
returns only adds fuel to the gambling fire. Foundries
have been working day and night for the last nine months
vainly endeavoring to catch up with orders for machinery,
and a great many new co.’s will probably not get their
appliances for 12 months or more. But a little item of
this sort doesn’t apparently trouble rash investors.

Westralia’s gold output for March, 1900, was
126,0490 z. February’s output was 117,8490 z. ; so the
March return was really worse than that for February, the
fall being over lOOoz. per working day. Both February
and March were worse than January (143,8190 z.). The
1899 yield was 1,643,8760 z., and to keep up this average
the first three months of 1900 should have yielded about
410,0000 z. They only panned out for 387,7170 z. It
wouldn’t be much of a fall on a goldfield where shares
sold on their dividend value only, but they don’t do that
in W. A. They sell on the London Stock Exchange swindle
value.
* • * *
A deathly silence has, for the last month or two,
been creeping over the Boulder mines, says a Bulletin
correspondent. Formerly the sound of the stampers
could be heard like a Niagara for miles around, but now
there is not a sound. The reason is not far to seek. The
oxidised ore has almost given out, and there is no work
for the battery. The only mines which can boast of still
having any oxidised ore are the Boulder, Ivanhoe and
Horseshoe—especially the “ Shoe,” which has abundance
in sight and many lodes to open up. The managers of
these “ shows ” can sit by quietly and see the other mines
wasting their substance in trying to treat the sul-
phides economically. Even the Lake View has shut down
its stamps, and must in future stake its all on the sulphide.
And we are going to have as much difficulty in its profit-
able treatment as they did on Broken Hill, and whilst the
problem is unsolved the West will experience the dreariest
and dullest time it has yet gone through. They are
putting men off literally by the hundreds on the big
mines. Thousands are out of work, property is
almost unsaleable, and the gloom that bespeaks
depression hangs over the place like a pall. The
whole sulphide problem has to be faced and fought
out once and for all. What is the use of the sulphide if
it cannot be made to pay? After all, when the glamour
and romance have departed from gold-mining, it becomes
just such a business as selling tea and sugar. The mines
must pay or be abandoned. The Lake View and Asso-
ciated have been winning gold lately at about £6 per
ounce. This can never go on, and so we in the West are
at present in the Slough of Despond, and those Delectable
Hills (our champion gold getters) are common sul-
phide lodes, at present of no value. This is the dark side
of the picture, but the cloud will lift and once properly
treated the sulphide will make Westralia the richest
mining camp in the world (the Rand and Cripple Creek
given in). All our mines have sulphide ore, not in small
lodes like those in which we got the oxidised, but
in immense bodies beginning at the 200 ft. level and going
down indefinitely; and the ore will simply have only to
be stoped out to yield its enormous treasure. Some idea
of the vastness of the sulphide may be gathered from the
fact that they have it in the Hannan’s North and also at
Mt. Robinson, an almost unbroken lode of eight miles
long. The West is only in its infancy—we won’t see its
hey-day perhaps for ten years.

Kalgoorlie is going to try smelting its own rich
tellurides, hitherto shipped east. The Golden Horseshoe
makes the experiment; a water jacket and two roasting
furnaces are nearly ready there. Gen. manager Suther-
land is a furnace expert; he graduated at the B.H. Prop.
* * *
Though scarcely older in months than the Vic-
torian fields are in years, some Kalgoorlie cos. are already
planning for workings as deep as the average of even
Bendigo deep mines. Boulder Perseverance, for instance,
down so far only 500 feet, is building a plant to haul from
2,500 feet.
• * * *
South Australia has never had a good gold-
mine, but there have been many “ prospects,” and the
latest, the New Wilgena, was lately found away in the
sandy, waterless country 300 miles N.W. of Port Augusta.
Alleged that there is a quartz and ironstone reef of
“ about 18 inches,” and that there are numberless speci-
mens “ simply thick with gold.” The discoverers have
the sense, however, to deprecate a rush to the New
Eldorado.
• * • ■
South Australia takes its mining industry by
the hand in a paternal way. It keeps a mining inspector,
an old Bendigo and B. H. manager, perpetually on the move
inspectingminesand things, and advising owners about de-
velopment and treatment; also, it has erected batteries
and cyanide plants wherever there has been shown to be
need of them —even away up in the heart of the con-
tinent. Lately it agreed to erect concentrating works on
low-grade copper-fields ; and the plans for smelting
works at Pt. Augusta are in hand. And now it is an-
nounced that it has helped the Mt. Gunson copper co.,
owning a “show ” in the Far North, to buy a concentrat-
ing plant, lending £l5OO without interest on the security
of the product. This latter is partly with a view to keep-
ing the Pt. Augusta smelter constantly in blast. As to
this Mt. Gunson, general-manager Greenway, of B.H.
Block 14, who lately reported on it, said that the ore could
be dressed to 18 per cent., and sent even as far as Pt.
Adelaide for treatment (Greenway’s works are at Pt. Ade-
laide) at a profit of 3s. 7d. per ton crude with copper as
low as £6O per ton. The ore is all low-grade—plenty of
it, but apparently in an irregular formation; so that
there isn’t the usual peg on which to hang the comforting
old fable that “ the ore will no doubt improve with
depth.”
• • • •
One popular argument in favor of a through line
from Sydney to Broken Hill via Cobar and Wilcannia, has
been that by the low rates that would be possible on so
long and cheap a line, N.S.W. Western coal-fields would
be able to supply Barrier requirements. But Railway
Commissioner Kirkcaldie, ex-traffic manager, when lately
at Broken Hill, said that that traffic was impossible—an
overland line simply could not compete with the existing
service to Port Pirie, and thence 330 miles by rail through
S. Aus.

During the 12 months to the middle of March
the average price for tin was exactly double the average
price for the 12 months ending the second week in March,
’9B; the figures were £125 7s. and £62 13s. 6d. Yet the
deliveries in London, the Continent, and the United
States during the ’99-’OO twelve months were, in spite of
all efforts to increase the output, little more than 5000
tons greater—66,soo tons as against 61,000 tons.

joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,

cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, andall sorts of iron-work.*

The Great Boulder Co., Kalgoorlie, have just
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.,
Ltd., South Melbourne, to build a pair of Compound Con-
densing Engines of 700 h.p. to drive their extensive mill-
ing plant. These engines will be of the latest type with
Proell valve gear, the fly-wheel is 20ft. in diameter,
grooved for 16 ropes and weighs upwards of 22 tons, the
total weight of engine will be 85 tons ; the engines are to
be completed by the end of the year, and will be the
finest and largest plant on the field. Three engines
under construction at the Otis Works for the Kalgoorlie
Goldfields have an aggregate of 1800 horse power.*

* * * *
Aftermuch consideration, the Tasmanian Copper
00. (West Coast) is moving towards the erection of a
smelter on its mine. The co. (which is mostly J. Bull)
has an immense ore body containing gold, silver, copper
and zinc, the whole forming a most difficult smelting pro-
position. Generally believed that the co. will put up a
small water-jacket for experimental purposes on the spot,
as the co.’s metallurgist, is said to have a process of his
own to try. All the co’s mine officials are from Yankee-
land.

Victoria has a mine named “the Mother of
Millions,” which has a few tons of good ore on surface and
some left in the mine, if newspapers are to be believed.
But if the name does not send it into early liquidation
nothing else will.
Lord Methuen, thusfar, has scored a better
average against the Boers than any of the other
generals. At the outset he won three hard-fought
engagements in succession. Then came the much
criticised defeat at Magersfontein, where his plan
of attack was made known to the enemy before-
hand through the foolish talk of one or more of
his officers. After the Magersfontein affair
Methuen had no need to beat a humiliating retreat
as predicted by newspaper alarmists ; on the con-
trary he held his ground until Lord Roberts was
ready to work a surprise on Cronje. Doing
nothing in particular he did it very well. Lately
he has been going again and the cable, whilst
crediting him with two or three little successes, has
not recorded any blunders against him. It was
Methuen who managed to intercept and capture a
small force of valuable Boer ‘ ‘ mercenaries ” under
the command of the French colonel who seemingly
lost his life in an attempt to get through. The
unexpected is always happening in South African
warfare, so perhaps the lately,maligned Methuen
will rank as the most trustworthy general on the
job when all the accounts are made up. A British
commander able to roam around and casually in-
tercept somebody, instead of getting intercepted
himself, seems a decidedly promising person, even
though he is only prevailing over Europeans whose
knowledge of the country is no greater than his
own.

SOCIETY
The Bulletin hopes that some of the
N.S.W. “Imperial. Bushmen,” while camped on
Rookwood Estate, paid a visit to the Benevolent
Asylum across the road. In that institution are
some “Imperial pensioners” rewarded by their
grateful country for meritorious service, in the
Crimea and Indian Mutiny. These battle-scarred
veterans, unfitted for peaceful pursuits as a result
of their experiences by flood and field, are en-
joying the evening of their lives on the reward
they have so well earned—pensions of from four-
pence to ninepence a day, expended in procuring
luxuries in a poorhouSe.
* • • •
The members of the first S.A. contingent
were insured in the Citizens’ Life office against
death or disablement, and three of them are dead,
bo that £750 has already had to be paid out of a
premium not double the sum. Before the second
lot were despatched the war had begun to look
serious, and no co. would take the risk, so the Govt,
has had to pay in the case of the One trooper, so
far dead. The S. A. bushmen were insured against
death but the co. declined to take the risk of dis-
ablement. Evidently no attempt will be made to
insure the “ Imperial bushmon.”
SPORT IN SHEOL.
EGYPUS: “I say, what’s the fun, Nero?”
NERO: “I had a parson on my fork and he
melted.”
The services of a really smart officer be-
longing to a country mounted regiment were called
into requisition during the formation of the last
N.S.W. Bushmen’s Corps for S. Africa. He
speaks with considerable scorn of some of the
officers appointed to the Bushmen. On one occasion
he Says he was asked, “Capt. , can you tell
me where my company is situated ?” The com-
pany was standing at attention 50yds. from the
inquirer, yet he could not see it ! Another “Bush”
officer, who had spent all his life in a Sydney
office, was asked to point out the two men wearing
caps instead of the regulation helmets. After a
long stare through conspicuous eye-gear he pointed
to the extreme left of his company, where, the men
were all correctly dressed. It was the two staff-
officers by his side who wore the caps. Were
these men tested medically for sight ?
* * * *
In connection with the S. Aus. contingent
of Imperial “ bushmen,” Adelaide is talking of the
son of a Very Naice family who, thrown out at
the first riding test, jumps up now a non-com. !
And the smiles are all the broader because, at the
former throwing-out, a pretty little story was told
of somebody trying to influence the selectors and
being promptly sat upon, oh, so heavily !

A new evening daily, the Reporter, was
registered in Melbourne last week. It is said that
the Harmsworth crowd, probably stirred thereto
by David Syme’s whacking the cost of the Borch-
grevink picnic with opposition man Newnes, have
planked down £300,000 to collect the best men
from the Australian papers and run a sort of Mel-
bourne Daily Mail. But this gorgeous scheme
doesn’t harmonise with another rumor that the
new daily is to be syndicated among well-known
political aspirants, every one of whom will pro-
bably want to run it for himself.
* * * *
The daily journal that a few weeks ago
gave up to an aggrieved party to escape libel pro-
ceedings, the name of the contributor of an
offensive article, has numerous precedents in its
favour. Several N.S.W. country newspapers now
existing have saved themselves in that way; and
not a few of the city journals of Australia may
go into that wobbly boat. Sydney’s first news-
paper used at intervals to insert the notice: “I shall
publish nothing the authorship of which I cannot
give at once. Why should I run the risk of being
ruined ?” Very fair. But worthy pressman Bent
lived to learn that the giving-up of the name does
not, unless a compact to that effect has been
entered into, wipe out the offence. The aggrieved
party has stiff legal claim. Where, in important
matters the contributor has been mistaken he
should give up his own name ; where malicious, it
should be given up for him—perhaps; but then, as
in all walks, a mysterious thing called Etiquette
takes part in this play.
* * ♦ *
An awful state of affairs was revealed at
the trial of a youth of 21 in Victoria last
week. Accused was found guilty of criminally
assaulting his cousin’s daughter, a child of six.
The child’s mother is a lunatic. The grand-
mother, who acted as guardian, when told of the
assault, had seemingly regarded the circumstance
as a matter of no importance, and took no further
notice. Another member of the family was in-
criminated on a similar charge, and a third stands
committed for the murder of the girl’s aunt. Few
of the witnesses could give the dates of their birth,
and. many could neither read nor write ; while one
fearsome specimen—a boy of 14—couldn’t even

month. All these people came from a long-settled
district of Victoria.

In a recent N.S.W. criminal-assault case
alleged to have been committed on a child 8| years
old, evidence, disclosed that the child was illegiti-
mate, and that her grandmother, mother (it was
her mother’s temporary husband who was charged
with the assault) and aunt were “ living with men,”
and-her uncle had deserted his wife for the more
alluring charms of that lady’s- sister, said wife
being consoled by his brother. The child stated
that she had never been taught the difference
between the truth and a lie, that she had never
heard of God, and did not know what the Bible
was, And yet cheerful idiots of both sexes swarm
over to China and elsewhere to convert the “pore
heathen.”
* * * *
There is no telling from one meal to
another how some Judges will jump. For in-
stance, at a country N.S.W. sessions, the other
day, two men were charged with stealing soda-
water bottles, a manufacturer’s property. “Oh,
well-;” said his Honor, “ I suppose Mr. Softstuff
simply wants to have it shown that these bottles
are his, and that the practice of dealing in them
is illegal ”; and a nominal sentence followed.
Presently, in a case of stealing ore-specimens,
counsel urged that it was quite the custom for
miner’s to take a few specimens—it helped them
in their work to have some knowledge of
mineralogy, so the practice had been allowed to
continue. “What!” ’Sonah cried, “this larceny
of ore common ? It must be stopped.” And in
passing sentence of two years, he explained that
counsel’s admission of the commonness of the
crime forced him to pass a sentence that would
deter others from following the practice in future.
The vagaries of Justice. A man with numerous
aliases steals 2ioz. gold in specimens from Bay-
ley’s United, where he works. Coolgardie P.M.
Fmnerty sentences him to six months, and evi-
dently thinks he is being very severe, for he re-
marks that this sort of thing must be put down.
Another, found guilty of “ unlawful possession ”
of a big quantity of specimen gold, at Boulder,
is fined £lO or two months. But at Broken Hill
two miners, pleading guilty to stealing a few

most flattering kind, and Judge Gibson gives

It isn’t only the country J.P. who does extra-
ordinary things; the country P. M. is sometimes
full of capers, too. At Wentworth, in the last
week in March, according to a pretty full report
in the local paper, a man was charged with laying
poisoned baits. The P.M. heard the prosecution,
then announced that he didn’t want to hear the
defence—he couldn’t convict on the evidence.
But, according to this report, he added (though he
had not heard the defence, remember) that “he
had little doubt defendant did lay the poison;
when he could not get the dog from Dow he sought
other means to destroy it.” The accused, shut out
of the witness-box, has replied with a statutory
declaration categorically denying every charge
against him.

To all intents and purposes, Circuit
Courts, established to give
N.S.W. country dis
tricts the benefit of
the services of Su-
preme Court
Judges, have
been abolished
in all but the
most accessible
centres. Thus,
it is years since
a Supreme
Court Judge
was at Broken
Hill ; either
a barrister is
sent up with
a commission
or a District
Court Judge
is told off to
take the sit-

latter course
has just been
adopted
again,
though the

one of the
heaviest ever
put before a
country ses-
sions court
in N.S.W.
Judge
Gibson,
acting
lately
as Su-
p r e m e
Court
Judge,
is un -
derstood
t o take
the late
J ustice
Windeyer (to
whom he was
once associate)
as a pattern.
Downto datehe
hasn t demand-
ed to see witnesses’ feet, but he has manifested other
of the older and bigger man’s idiosyncrasies—includ-
ing the worst of them, a passion for long and late
sittings. At Broken Hill he has been sitting day
after day from 9 in the morning till past 10 o’clock
at night, winding up, after a bout of this, with a
murder case. He began this case at 2 p.m. ; and
the jury, which had been in attendance all day
and all the previous days and nights, was sent
out to consider its verdict just as midnight was
sounding. The court adjourned at 1.30 a.m., and
the same morning the sittings were resumed at 9 !
With his strong physique the Judge may have been
fit; but what about the frailer mortals defending
the accused and the often frailer ones in attend-
ance as jurors ?
* * * a
Now that Moore Park tip is abolished its
place is supplied by a punt moored at the foot of
Market-street, Pyrmont. Hither hundreds of
carts bring their rubbish and tip it promiscuously.
And strange are some of the deposits ; dead and
decomposing pigs, dogs, rats and cats, mingling
with the refuse from boarding-houses and the
waste paper and rubbish from warehouses. All
Sundays this choice collection is allowed to swelter
in the sun and the public passing the bridge are
assailed with fearful smells. From Good Friday
to Monday also this stinking cargo remained along-
side the wharf; children rummaging through it
and picking out bits of cardboard or broken boxes,
while a strong wind swept the stench all over the
lower portion of Pyrmont. And—“every effort
is being made,” we are told, “ to stop the plague.”
* * * *
Sydney’s new system of cleaning-up is
already bearing fruit. Instead of being dropped
in Moore Park, now the things which the city
would rather leave unsaid are taken about 15
miles out to sea in barges, and there dropped on
to the bosom of Abraham —that is to say, Nep-
tune. And the more buoyant among them
promptly wash up at Watson’s Bay, where the
council clerk is now fully occupied picking them
off the rocks by the tail and slinging them in-
dignantly into fires built on the beach. As the
refuse isn’t dated no one knows exactly how long
it takes to get back home again. The two dogs
that were found on the shore the other day may
have been yesterday’s dogs or last week’s dogs
anyhow, they weren’t last year’s dogs, for the
system wasn’t in vogue then. There is something
solemn in the idea of this procession of
dead animals and sundries heading, ghost-like,
over the quiet green sea back to its old haunts,
washing steadily along in the silver moonlight,
heaving up and down on Britannia’s special ele-
ment, and at last climbing spectrally on its native
shore again, to be found there by a suburban
council clerk in the morning. Fifteen dead ani-
mals per diem is the average at Watson’s Bay
now, and the Bay, with its own garbage tip at its
back-door and Sydney’s dead-dog washing up on
its front step, is unreasoningly angry. The fact
that it is acquiring much valuable information
about the set of the tides and currents appeases it
not in the least. It doesn’t even think of in-
quiring up in Sydney if any one can remember
what day any special animal was dropped over-
board, to see how long they take to get back ; and
it isn’t making notes on the winds and tides to
ascertain what particular set of circumstances
washes up most dogs, and which set leaves fewest
cats and gin-cases. It hasn’t even tried to find
out whether, on the whole, dogs or cats make the
best time back, or whether old infected hats get
ahead of both of them with condemned corned-
beef a good second. It is simply angry, and busy
shovelling valuable information into the fire.

Neutral Bay was the original quarantine
station for N.S.W. Typhus fever and small-pox
with occasional cholera outbreaks were the epi-
demics of 100 years ago. The “Alligator” as
hospital ship was moored in the bay, and on the
ridge to the right the huts for “contacts” and
convalescents were scattered about. Increase of
settlement in the district drove the station to the
North Head of Port Jackson.

Gormanston is an old Indian soldier, but he’s laid
up at present with wounds received in the fishing-
field. The 85-year-old R.C. Archbishop Murphy
is also an ex-Indian, and has started* an Indian
Famine Fund. Dr. Murphy was a bishop in
India during the Mutiny, and one day his native
banker came to him and said he’d be obliged if the
bishop would wipe out a little overdraft. '“But
why?” said the latter. “We’ve had business-
relations for years, and I’ve kept all my engage-
ments. Why are you afraid of me now?” The
banker was reticent, but, upon pressure, he con-
fessed that he wanted the account squared “ be-
cause,” said he, “I don’t know how long you may
have your head on your shoulders.” The bishop
paid up, and, when the Mutiny broke out, he ap-
plied to Government for arms, which he stacked
ostentatiously on his verandah, causing the local
natives to think the place was garrisoned. The
trouble, however, never came that way.

A larceny charge ran its peculiar length in
a Sydney court last week. A man accused of
stealing whisky from Toohey & Co., went for trial
to Quarter Sessions some time ago. In the course
of the trial a juror at lunch-hour went home for
his meal. Returning late, the Judge found ho
had left the precincts of the court and dissolved
the jury, remanding accused to a future court.
When next accused appeared and was tried the
jury disagreed. For the third time he came up
last week and was acquitted.

down the passage, and gazing meditatively a
towards the kitchen, dreamily observes. You ve f
got a sink there, haven’t you?” “Yes. Ah. g
—and, without going near said sink, the s.s.p. t
walks out into the yard, surveys, in turn, with t
lack-lustre eye, the fowls, the family cat, the gar- $.
ments fluttering on the clothes-line. lhen,
without so much as a look at drams, bath-
room, or stable, he observes, in a lost-chord land of
way, “ It’s all porfec’ly satisfac’ry ! ” and fades
slowly away.
«. * * *
N.S.W. District-Court Judge Rogers dealt
with a neat little domestic legality at Grenfell
the other day. A man stood forward charged
with entering and attempting to steal from the
dwelling of P. Curry. The evidence went to show
that accused entered the kitchen and there
attempted to steal. That seems to have gone
without comment, but it was pointed out that the
kitchen was not joined to Mr. Curry’s house by a
covered way and it was contended that there-
fore it was not part of Mr. Curry’s dwelling.
Judge Rogers, who rather relishes neat distinc-
tions, promptly concurred with this view and
ordered prisoner’s discharge.

Johann Andersen, a Russian Finn, sen-
tenced to 10 years for stabbing a British seaman
to death, in the sailors’ quarters at Port Adelaide,
was released, under mysterious circumstances,
after serving two years of the term. “Under-
stood that the request for a reconsideration of the
case emanated from a foreign source.” Mean-
while, Cecile Andersen, a Norwegian woman,
sentenced to a “lifer,” many years ago, for the
fatal shooting of her shabby ex-paramour, in the
back parlor of a Melbourne pub., is still eating
hominy at Government expense. Looks as though
a Russian consul in South Australia'carried more
guns of persuasion than a Norwegian consul m
Victoria. .

the caper with the ungodly. Thus: Ist ticket
19905, 2nd 1990 G, 3rd 19907, 4th 19908 sth 19909 ;
three nines would win at poker, while three tickets
would tie at cribbage. Some sm a rties are already
carrying old tickets such as 55550, 54655, 66696,
&c. However, it all helps the revenue and the
game is excusable in this season of war-drunk.

There is an amusing side even to the
plague epidemic. About six months before it
broke out a big firm entered into a two-years con-
tract with Govt, to supply carbolic acid at 2s. a
gallon the customary price to wholesale customers
at the time being 2s. 6d. ; but, like the grocers
who supply sugar cheap in order to sell tea, they
doubtless reckoned on averaging things. Unfor-
tunately, the plague stepped m to interfere, and
Govt, wanted thousands of gallons where ordi-
narily it would have ordered oniy, and
while the price to private people rose 200 or .
tier cent, the firmhad still to refrain from executing
most of’its orders from this source because the
demands of the health authorities were exhausting
all stocks as they arrived. A magnate of the co
cernone recent morning entered the establish
ment in a breathless and broken-up condition. As
soon as he could be “ restored to his personal com-

In re the notable frequency with which
plague has attacked employes of Sydney printing-

English PAit Mall Magazine for March
contains an “Australian story.” No real Aus-
tralian story is complete without either a bush fire
in Melbourne city.or a flood m Sydney harbor
This present yarn is a thrilling rescue from the
raging Murray, on a Queensland station.
At a South Coast (N.S.W.) pub., at which
the Judge on circuit was staying for a couple of
days, a man turned up in the bar early one morn-
ing and, producing a pair of handsome boots ex-
plained that he was “ dead-beat, ” and would the
landlord take the boots in exchange for keep and
sundry drinks. “Bung” took the canoes, and
gave the man a parting glass, but, later on* dis-
covered that the boots were His Honor s—picked
up from the bedroom door. An impression pre-
vails in the district that this amounted to sacrilege
or high treason, or something.

“ T.M.” : Talking of absent-
minded beggars: Was once on a
pleasure-trip round the world, and
one night, as our ship lay in
Naples harbor, several of us went
ashore and had a wild and woolly
time. Later on, when we ielt
shore on our return to the steamer,
I was very mixed and didn t re-
member much about it. But
directly my feet touched the lad-
der I knew where I was. I got on
deck, and felt pretty comfortable ;
not quite right, but knew what 1
was about. Presently there was
a commotion forward and some-
body yelled, “Man overboard!
I was wise enough to know that
I was not in a fit state to render
any assistance, and I said so most
distinctly for the benefit of all and
sundry. I stated that I couldn t
swim, and therefore I wasn’t going
over to rescue the capsized one,
whoever he was. Somebody
seemed to remonstrate with me
about my heartlessness, and 1
replied indignantly that when a
man made an inebriated beast of
himself and fell out of a ship he
couldn’t expect sympathy from the
well-conducted section of the com-
munity. I added that I was
going to bed, as exposure to the
night air was bad for my health.
Just then a boat-hook was stuck
in the back of my collar, and I
was hauled into a small boat.
The man overboard was Me.

There are many strange deaths to choose
from in Australia. Quite lately a boy of 16 was
suffocated with dust on the mam road close to the
large town of Kalgoorlie. He fell out of his cart
and was stunned—not .injured m any way, but
merely had his wits scattered for a minute or so.
But he fell f ace-do wnwards, and ere his wits
could come back he was choked m the six-mch-
deep dust.

The sooner the ordinary variety of Crown
Prosecutor opens his speech to the jury ™. 1
appear for the police, whose direct personal m-
terest it is to convict this prisoner,” the sooner
will he proclaim his true status. In the recent
Dunedin (M.L.) murder-case (already alluded to
in these columns) an old couple who had lived
happily all their lives were found by the police—
the Wife murdered, apparently, by a blow from a
tomahawk ; the husband bleeding and incoherent.
The latter’s tale was that a midnight intruder had
done the deed; but, on the theory of a drunken
quarrel, the husband was arrested and tried ; the
Crown-Prosecutor leading off dead against the
prisoner and finishing by calling the doctor who
examined the pair after the occurrence. This
witness described the prisoner’s injuries as
“trifling;” but the latter’s lawyer insisted on
the Crown-Prosecutor calling the gaol-surgeon
(subpoenaed by the Crown, but not called) who de-
posed that on prisoner’s arrival at the gaol lie had
stripped him and found him terribly bruised on
the body, evidently from a severe knocking-about.
Corroborative evidence of this —and acquittal of
prisoner. Police now looking for “a strange
dark man,” and public inquiring why the Crown
so strongly opposed the calling of the gaol-surgeon?

Letter headed “ How Can Men Live 1 ”
from a Sydney daily paper:—
To the Editor. Sir, —I wish to ask, through your
columns, if it is possible for a man to keep.a wife and
family (six), pay rent, and rail fare, and keep out of debt
on a weekly wage of 20s. This, Sir, is the state of things
in the city of Sydney, and in one of the largest wholesale
Houses, too. It is almost incredible that any man could
have the conscience to ask his brother-man to work for a
wage such as that mentioned above. It is no wonder that
men heap to themselves wealth when it is done at the
expense of their employes, who have to take it or starve.
It is time, Sir, I think,' that something was done in Par-
liament to pass an Act, so that those who have to bear
the "burden of life and are expected to pay their share to
the State, will be able to do it with more ease. I feel
that I cannot do any more than open the subject for
someone else who is more able with the pen ; but as one
who is receiving that wage 1 feel that it is nothing but
right, in the interest of my brother-workmen, that it is
time something was done. Trusting you will pardon the
liberty I have taken, and thanking you in anticipation.—
Yours, &c., Sufferer. Sydney, April 19.

A Sydney daily-paper item :
On Monday evening the police at No. 4 station, George-
Street N., received information that a man named George
Roberts, aged about 50, a ship’s steward, had (bed * lls |
lodgings, 5 George-street N. It appeared that deceased
had been in bad health for some time, and had not seen a
doctor. He was absolutely without means, and was sup-
posed to have had, for some time past, little or nothing to
eat. A few days ago he obtained some temporary work
of a light nature, and was supposed to have invested
what remained over and above his expenditure for
food and lodgings, in cod-liver oil. At 6 o’clock on Mon-
day evening Roberts called to a fellow-lodger, and asked
him to lift him up from the bed on which he was lying.
On his request being granted, Roberts said ‘ I am done.
and died. On the matter being reported to the coroner,
an inquest was dispensed with, but a post-mortem exami-
nation showed that death was due to consumption, accele-
rated by starvation. Roberts’ body was in a most emaci-
ated condition, his bones showing between them and tha
skin not a vestige of flesh.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ Sydney, April 24, 1900.
My Dear Myee,—
Vice-regal engagements consist in journeys
between Moss V ale and Sydney. Govt. House is
still swathed in chintz, and Cavanagh, the hall-
porter, enjoys a quiet life, unmolested by Potts
Point. A little sensation went through society
last week at the news that His Excellency
accompanied by Capt. Harbord, was going
to stay with the Coolangatta Hays. The
pleasant Miss Hays appear a little dangerous to
society. Capt. Harbord, by the way, is a naval
man, with a fair beard, who came into fashion last
year in a society-beauty’s train. It has never been
given out that he is any relation to Lady Caring-
ton, but there is a rumor to that effect, substan-
tiated, I suppose, by the fact that Lady Mary
Lygon so often accepted him as her partner at last
season’s balls.
At the A. J.C. on Thursday the sun shone, the
wind was still, and the lawn was more festive and
racey than at either of the two previous days. In
the V.R. box, Lady Darley wore a purple bonnet
and a black frock alongside Mrs. Pilcher, in more
black and purple bonnet. Miss Darley was in
black with a lace yoke and light straw hat. Mias
Sylvia wore a pink silk shirt with white skirt A
well-known lady cut an elegant figure in flowing
black chiffon, with a black satin swathing round a
wasp waist, and a big black hat. This lady affects
the newest thing in veils. Hers rested on her
upper lip, exposing the chin and mouth. When
we get used to it we may like it; but at present it
strikes us as about the most unbecoming way of
arranging a face-cover ever invented. Few, indeed,
are the women who can stand a black line across
the face under the nose; and besides, the upper
and lower parts of the complexion !
People continue to stare excitedly at Lady
Francis Hope. Her dressing on Thursday was a
little daring—a grey-green frock covered all over
with silk fringe, a white square parasol, and white
shoes. As for the future Duke of Newcastle, he
strolled about in a week-day suit, with his large
head in a little pot hat.
Anawful rumor went round the lawn. “Nance
ONen is eating peanuts, and throwing the
shells at men ! ” Society was horribly shocked
for Society is quite at Nance’s feet, you know.
She doesnt pick it up, but n’ importe. Her
lovely frocks, her exclusiveness—that is to say, her
talents—have reduced Potts Point to frantic
admiration and invitation galore. I strolled round
to have a peep at the stately Nance behavin' s, in
this early Darwinian way, and discovered that the
tall woman in black, with her black hat tilted over
her eyes, and she typical ’Murkan figure, was not
Nance, but somebody else altogether. Also that
it was the lady’s husband who was eating the
peanuts. The lady herself was merely sitting
still, and looking nice, and wondering why people
stared so.
Race week exposed several little society changes.
In the absence of the top layer of cream, two or
three pretty women, who never had a chance
before, took things into their own hands. They
now lead victoriously, rushing up and down
the lawn, trailing, looking over the shoulder,
bowing with arctic sweetness, and keeping for ever
on the move and in evidence, just as the genuine
leaders used to do. In fact, it looks as if the old
queens will return to their kingdoms, by-and-bye,
to find themselves dethroned.
I am always hearing now of people who are
going to live in the Mountains for the winter.
Mrs. Willie Mitchell is trying to let her new
house at Darling Point in order that she may
breathe the brisker air of Katoomba. J. C
Williamson is another citizen who will, pat-
ronise the Mountain resorts; he has just bought a
h ,° l use Wentworth Falls. But it will be prob-
ably. Mrs. Williamson and the golden-haired
Marjorie who will spend most of their time there.
Marjorie, by the way, is the pet of the Sydney
theatrical world. Her wealth of youthful jewellery
is made up of a bangle, from Maud Jeffries, a
locket from Mrs. Brown-Potter, and a brooch from
everybody you can think of. All the chorus-
girls and stage children are at her feet,
but her childish, affections remain childish, in
spite of all the spoiling. Marjorie, leaning out
or a box at her father’s theatre at a" Wednesday
matinee, is quite a feature. Apropos, this little
story is true of her. At church the other day the
parson announced special service on Wednesday
afternoon. _ “/can’t come on Wednesday,” cried
out Marjorie quickly.
A second visit to “Camille” showed several
changes. Edith Berry, for instance, has given
U P wearing a red wig. She donned it first
night so that she would not be recognised
™ dual characters. But friends remon-
strated against carroty hair round that thin
race, and Edith shed her wig promptly.
Also, Emily Hughes has a new dress. At first
she wore one frock all through the play; which
was scarcely fair to an actress so clever and kind
to her clothes. The new frock is worn in the ball-
room scene a peach-colored brocade, with two
deep flounces of lace on the skirt, and deep peach
flowers on the bodice. Miss Ricca Allen, the
American actress, who came over with Nance
U Neil, shows herself again, as in Mrs. Triplet, an
artist at wearing a cap over hair dressed round
the ears.
As for Nance, she is a wonderful study in atti-
tudes. Here are two favorite poses, both startling,
original, life-like, but opposed to all stage pro-
prieties. No. 1 sees her kneeling on the sofa,
talking to Armand. One foot is stuck out, and
taps on the ground behind her; her back is turned
to the house. I cannot tell how long she keeps
that up. It seems a very long time ; for much as
I admire and am fascinated by her personality,
her back is decidedly, emphatically, too aggres-
sive, too large too important to be so continually
turned on. Her second pose is the tailor’s squat.
One plumps down on the floor suddenly with her
legs doubled under her. Very artful is her
management of her big five-feet-ten in this trying
position. She sinks into it. The house holds its
breath. -But she steals up out of it, never tripping
over her sweeping muslin flounces, but getting on
her feet with a facility that suggests years of prac-
tice at that primeval pose.
. latest feature of the Sydney plague-scare
is the elusive suspect. Two of them turned up in
one day. A local doctor was called in to see a
patient, whom he promptly adjudged guilty of
plague, and reported. When somebody arrived
from the health office the patient had vanished,
and the police failed to find him. Another man
reported himself at one of the hospitals, was
suspected, told to wait. He thought better of it,
and left, saying that if they wanted him he would
be at a certain address. He was wanted, and was
finally found late at night at another address. He
died next morning before he could be removed,
thus gaining his end, which was clearly to escape
quarantine.
All sentimental nonsense, perhaps; but senti-
ment is just the lever that moves the world, and
it is neglect of that great truth that causes so
many administrative failures. It’s bad enough to
bump up against a physical law; but you may
survive it. An enduring popular sentiment, if
you run counter to it, will sooner or later fall on
you and crush you. Already it is openly said
that medical men in private practice are taking
advantage of the ambiguity of the symptoms to
treat plague patients at home. Many of them
even deny that it is plague, and are supported by
a large section of public opinion. Compulsory
removal to quarantine has aroused all the angry
passions of patients’ families, while the burial in
numbered graves has put the finishing touch to
the exasperation.
By the way, as the plague-scare is inducing
people to be more careful with their food, I feel
bound to recommend you to drink Atcherley and
Dawson’s G-lobe tea, a very special form of this
unmebriating beverage, I assure you.
I read, the other day, in Sir Evelyn Wood’s
book on the Crimea how, in that awful winter of
54 and ’55 before Sebastopol, there was nearly
being a mutiny in the British camp because the
authorities proposed, instead of burying the dead
in. their blankets, to diminish the sufferings of
the living by distributing those blankets among
them. The men who bore cold, hunger and toil
without a murmur were roused to fury by a fancied
insult to their dead comrades. It would be worth
something to the average politician or official to
learn tfm truth that men’s minds count for more
than their bodies.
Quarantine, is the latest honeymoon resort. A
country couple just married were carried there
last week among a, houseful of contacts. What a
situation for a writer with a French mind; the
young husband, the young wife, death hanging
over them by a single hair, as it were. Every day
the wonder as to who will be the next victim.
Will one be taken, and the other left ? But, so
far, nothing sensational has happened.
A society wedding last week was that of Miss
Alice M‘George and Mr. Wickham, from Welling-
ton. Bride and bridegroom have prepared to
spend part of their honeymoon in Quarantine at
Maoriland. They were married at Rand wick last
Wednesday. Miss M‘George has enjoyed for a
long time the reputation of being the best dancer
in Sydney. A particularly snappy dress was worn
by her one bridesmaid, Miss Finney, from Bris-
bane. It was a white chiffon frock with little
frills to the knees. Over this was a whole over-
dress of muslin-de-soie, patterned in huge nastur-
tiums, cut off the shoulders as if it were a low
bodice, and filled in with a transparent gympe
and sleeves of tucked chiffon. Worn with this
was a cream hat trimmed with nasturtium and
tomato mixed most fetchingly.
