A gilt-edged marriage eventuated in Adelaide
last week when H. A. Parsons espoused the
daughter of editor Bonython, of the Advertiser.
Young Parsons lately benefitted to the tune of
over £60,000 by the death of a wealthy aunt allied
to the Angas millions, and there are “tons more”
coming. Bonython pkre is reputedly a quarter-
millionaire ; his paper is supposed to turn him in
£15,000 a year. Parsons is a son of the scholarly
erstwhile N.T. Govt. Resident, and has political
aspirations.
By the way, Mark Hambourg isn’t coming out
here yet. He is said to be frightened of
Plague. There are reasons why some artists need
fear this dirt-disease, but Mark—the adored, fas-
cinating, nonchalant Mark—is it possible that he,
too, is a bit of a Svengali ?
Which reminds me that Sydney University has
no “Commem.” this year. No! The Senate
sat, and pondered. No reason was given aloud.
Then Commem. was mysteriously and suddenly
abolished for the present. I suppose there cannot
be any connection between this action and the
fact —it is a fact, is it not ?—that so many Univer-
sity women look as if they didn’t wash their faces ?
In these Jingo days the household flags may be
seen out at tne weekly wash along with little
Jimmy’s suit and pa’s six boiled shirts, but they
were all out again nicely ironed and laundried last
Monday, when the 750 nut-brown boys of the bull-
dog breed rode along George-street en route to
Africa. The scene presented little difference from
any of the preceding departures, save that there
was less crowd, less music, less enthusiasm—the
daily papers admit this—and more bushmen.
Bottles were as much in evidence as formerly,
and flower-garlands enwreathed many a manly
collar-bone. One Tommy carried an india-rubber
doll, possibly as a memento of one of the little
things he had left behind him. Another Achillas
with a heap of handkerchiefs on his saddle’
looked as if he were taking home the washing’.
Ihe girls did not hang on to the horses’ tails as
much as formerly, because they so often had to
ask these sagacious animals to take their feet off
their toes. One warrior kept the crowd at a dis-
tance by yelling out now and then—“ Tyke eyre
o my orse; ’e bites ! ” whereat the faithful com-
panion of man faced suddenly round and tried to
chew the contingent generally.
Sydney was in a state of barrier on Monday •
but whether it was an infected-area barrier or Im-
perial Contingent barrier was hard to ascertain
till one got inside. Then the envious looks of the
crowd were reassuring. Along Macquarie-street’s
lashionable boarding-houses, the leg; of Sydney’s
-Noblest adorned the various buildings, re-
minding one of a threepenny “hang-over”
at the Tivoli. At one corner a stout man
waved his arms and yelled—“ Mealies! me
boys; bring back mealies, mealies! ” Fur-
ther on a man of blood, who had loyally
colored his nose the sacred hue with which Africa
is to be painted, waved his hands frantically in
the atmosphere—“ Kill ’em all, boys, kill ’em all.
Kill the women, too ! Don’t— hic— forget the
women ! But now the end of the procession
was at hand, and the warriors began to ask each
other if their wreaths were on straight.
The proletariat took their seats on the hill
above Woolloomooloo and looked complacently
down at an all but invisible scene. They had been
jammed for an hour or so in a crowd that stood on
their shoes They had had to hold their top
1 one hand and guard Jimmy from
the biting ’orse with the other, and keep on their
hat by biting at it. They had run the gauntlet of
•hatpins and plague-germs, and men who carry
their sticks horizontally. Now they augmented
their thirst with bananas while gazing all the
afternoon at something they didn’t see. And yet
people say we are a pleasure-loving race !
At the embarkation: A glimpse of big iron
rails, some dishevelled creatures gazing through,
and a squad of policemen standing outside’.
Boor things! wot ’ave they done?” from the
crowd ; but it was only some of Sydney’s Noblest
who had succeeded in getting inside the enclosure.
Ihe soldiers were now being harangued as a final
test of their fitness to face hardships. What
tired, sleepy faces the majority had ! One fellow
looked martial in an array of medals, but, as he
modestly expianmd to the crowd that had cheered
flu r ey ain ’f mine - They’re father’s medals
tor the fattest sheep, and he gave ’em to me to
make a bit of a show with.” Then the cameras
started on their deadly work, and this scribe de-
parted.
The city was deserted, inhabited by nothing but
the swarm of microbes roused to activity by the
late shivoo. Then up a quiet side-street came a
lonely hansom, with a portly occupant, and we
realised that George Dibbs was having a little
procession all by himself.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The snappiest wedding for a long time past
was that of Miss Queenie Calvert (the Chirnsides’
niece) and Mr. Gordon Lyon, oelebrated at Colac
Saturday, April 21st. All the old Vic. hunting
families were to the fore. Aide Riohard Nevill
was best man. The presents, counted by hundreds,
included hunters (one froip the Brasseys), a pony
a gun, diamonds, and cheques galore. *
“Prominent in the present-show was a diamond
crescent, Mr. Cox’s gift to his liege-lady, who
responded with a hunter. Mr. and Mrs. Colles
the bride’s uncle and aunt, gave their niece an
impressive pearl pendant, and bestowed the most
desirable saddle and bridle extant on her new
proprietor. The travelling gown of beaver cloth,
with fixings of cream-point and black, was en-
livened by a dash of Mandarin panne. Janet
£ lark ?> Tiord Richard Nevill, Lady and
Miss Manning, etc., shed their smiles on the
heiress s wedding.
.“The leading lady’s white satin had train and
trimmings of tucked silk-muslin; sleeves and fix-
ings of real Honiton. The six maids in Gainsboro’
hats and white satin gowns, with plain skirts, wore
soft fichus and each had a breast-knot of a single
pink rose tied with sky-blue. They held their
posies of pink fairy-roses in quaint holders of
ivory-satin, with Honiton flounces and blue
streamers. The bride started on her travels in
dark-purple face-cloth, with intervals of apricot-
and-black spotted panne, real lace cravat, and
Pans hat.

raithful to orthodox white satin, orange-blossom
and myrtle, with flimsy flounces, &c., feather-
stitched with white silk, and her Court-train hung

Pretty Mrs. Crotty’s matrimonial luck seems

“As each contingent leaves these shores it trails
behind it a string of engagements. ‘ Strange they
are not reported till after the boat leaves,’ snorted
an angry damsel, to whom a good-looking bushman
had been making love on and off for years ; lie was
now claimed by another girl with the ineffable
simper and self-consciousness of the newly-engaged.
“After all, one can’t blame girls for making a
very little love-affair go a very long way. Men
are so scarce and so scared nowadays, and in the
usual ‘ strictly in confidence’ one must have some-
thing to go upon. However, an adroit Brighton
girl tied her admiring bushie with the marital
knot, and if he returns he must claim her whether
he likes it or not.
“ Some of the officers of the Australian Imperial
Regiment are presenting patches of khaki cloth
with military button attached to their girl-chums.
One has converted her patriotic patch into a
sachet which she sports at her side as a kind of
depository for button-hooks, tram-tickets, and
stray tags of enthusiasm. All brag ftf the khaki
patch as if it were a relic of an early-century
’scutcheon, or a chip of the True Cross. Colonel
Kelly, the best set-up officer in Melbourne, and a
bachelor to boot, is above patches ; autographed
photos, are more in his line. Melbourne girls
think thqy are excused for raving over the good-
looking Irishman, on the strength of his grey
hair. But under grey bushy eyebrows, twinkling
eyes look out with all the wag’s drollery at those
who would measure a man by the color of his
hair. Grey, he tells the gurrls, was given him to
match, his complexion.
“The Melb. girls are forming a rifle club, and
the uniform—which, of course, is the important
thing—-is to be khaki skirt and blouse, Lancer
hat cocks’ plumes, gauntlet gloves and
gaiters. There is no particular reason why women
should learn to shoot, beyond the facts that the
sex is imitative, and the men are busy forming
rifle-clubs. For a woman to be a craek rifle-shot is
surely as unnecessary as for a man to be an expert
in Mountmellick embroidery. Also, if women
would learn to do any one thing well, they would
benefit everybody. For instance, at the popular
sewing-bees, held to make garments for the re-
fugees and soldiers at the Cape, you will find,
perhaps, one woman in twenty who really knows
how to make garments. The quantity of ill-made
shirts, absurd petticoats, terrible dresses and bon-
nets should shame every woman in Melb. Why
can’t they buy patterns ; why don’t they learn to
sew and cut out ? Said one girl innocently:
‘ What is the size of a soldier’s neck ? This py-
jama coat is 26 inches. Do you think that is all
right ? ’ Said another : ‘ This petticoat is 60
inches—is that too long ? ’
“Collins-street now boasts a photographer
whose rooms are crowded all day long by every
beauty and society ornament desiring a dozen copies
of her features fora shilling. The inch-square repre-
sentations are by no means flattering, but they are
amusing, and the photographer is a bland, cheer-
ful creature with a wonderful aptitude for compli-
ments ; in this he could give a lesson to others of
his craft. At a certain shop in the same street
the fair sex subjected itself to all manner of
discourtesies—some of which were indeed unique.
One customer desired a profile picture. ‘ Profile ! ’
exclaimed the god of the camera, ‘ Profile with
that nose ! ’ Another desired to wear evening-
dress. ‘ I can hide bones by retouching, but fat’s
hopeless.’ Another wished for ‘ a pretty picture, ’
and received a contemptuous smile. ‘The camera
is not omnipotent, madam.’ And for all that he
had a large business.
“The practical owner of the Palace Hotel, the
Bijou Theatre, and the Victorian Arcade, Mel-
bourne, saw them for the first time the other day.
About the end of the ’3o’s, a young barrister
named Brewster was sent Iw the Sydney Govt, to
Melbourne as chairman of Quarter Sessions. He
bought land largely and cheaply, right on the site
of the coming city, and in time gave up the Law
and was elected member for Melbourne in the Syd-
ney Parliament. In the ’so’s he went to England
and took Anglican orders, but did not marry till
he was 74 years old. Meanwhile, gold had been
discovered in Victoria, and Brewster’s Melbourne
land had become worth pounds per inch. But he
never went back. In 1875 he let on building-lease
the land on which the Victorian Arcade, with
its Academy of Music, and the big Palace Hotel
were built by the late Joseph Aarons and his
successors. The old gentleman died at Capetown
a couple of years ago. The lease of the Bourke-
street and Little Collins-street land falls in five
years hence, and then the whole block—Bijou
Theatre, hotel, and all—will belong to Mrs.
Brewster (still a youngish woman), who, as has
been said, saw her property last week for the first
time.
“ Melbourne is to lose Mdlle. Dreyfus for a long
period. It may be for years and it may be for
ever. The ostensible reason of the French
teacher’s departure is to enjoy a sea-trip and a
change of air. But Mdlle. tells people in her
pretty confidential way that if she finds a good
opening and a sphere of usefulness in the gay
city she will abide there, for no Frenchwoman
willingly leaves Paris. 4 Things have changed—
comprenez vous ? since I left home. I cannot tell
eggsactly how I shall proceed,’ she explains with
a pretty Bernhardesque play of teeth and cheek
moles looking like chocolate patches. Mdlle
Dreyfus has taught all our ‘ best’ people French,
as-she-is-spoke. For several years past her hour-
glass figure, always garbed tastefully, has been
received with enthusiasm at the haunts of the
Upper Two Hundred.
“ IN °w that a well-known journalist is back in
Melbourne a few stone lighter for his war experi-
ence, one wonders (now that his position is prosaic
and pleasant again) how he relishes his own thril-
ling siege letters, which arrived before him, and
are published daily. Each epistle is more de-
pressed and downhearted than its predecessor,
and as the relief chapter is still pages ahead,
readers anticipate that, before then, the writer
wifi have unveiled a frame of mind likely to make
Job’s reflections seem frivolous by comparison.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Colonel Mackay, the much-feted, plain-
tively remarks that of the people who have ten-
dered him valedictory handshakes, one-half called
him Makky and the other half McKye.
Lieut. Oxley, who goes to S. Africa, is
grandson of the Lieut. Oxley, surveyor and
explorer, who named so many places in ancient
N.S.W. and Queensland. Grandfather Oxley be-
came M.L.C., and father Oxley, M.L.A.
The Soanes of Dubbo (N.S.W.) have four
boys serving in- South Africa. They are great-
grandsons of Col. Gibbs, who was Collector of
Customs (N.S.W.) in the early forties, their
grandfather, Dulhunty, being one of the pioneers
of the Dubbo country.
A writer to the Adelaide dailies asserts
that the of-the-period “ kissing-patch ” upon the
cheek of Frank Dicksee’s Lady Teazle (bought
for the Adelaide Art Gallery) “has been sand-
papered out by instructions of the gallery authori-
ties.” He suggests that they, perhaps, mistook it
for a fly-spot. Ye gods !
ArtistPiguenit, thehonest, careful, poetic,
“old-fashioned ” painter, at whom it is the fashion
of the local impressionists to sneer, has again gone
to England, where his work is in demand at high
prices. A leading London firm is photogravuring
two landscapes painted by him in England.
Piguenit could always sell in Australia and Tas-
mania (his native country) at substantial figures
all the pictures he could paint,, and his prices are
still rising.

vi. 12, 13): “A naughty
person, a wicked man,
walketh with afroward
mouth. He winketh
with his eyes, he

he teaoheth with his

Editor Bonython, of the Adelaide Adver-
tiser, who is slowly recovering from a severe
illness, sails for England by P. and O. s.s.
Australia. During the 35 years he has been con-
nected with the paper of which he is now sole
owner he has never previously taken a decent
holiday, but fate and the doctors have forced his
hand on this occasion. Mr. Bonython has Federal
ambitions r and will be back in time to run for the
Senate at'the first Commonwealth elections.

Dear Bulletin. —You let somebody say
that the Beau Standish, met by Disraeli at
Malta, was father of Capt. Standish, one-time
Victoria’s Chief Commissioner of Police. It may
be, but, all the same, the captain’s surname was

The death in England of Lady Louisa
Tighe, at 97, recalls the tragic death of her sister,
Lady Mary Fitzroy, in Parramatta over 50 years
ago. Gov. Fitzroy, generally in an over-dined con-
dition, would insist on driving, and ran his wife
and Private Secretary against a tree. To do him
justice, he maintained a limp, moist state of
features for over a month afterwards.
The vigor and healthiness of Queen
Victoria in her old age is lucidly explained by
Melb. Argus. Her Majesty is
Only another instance of the healthfulness and longevity
of brain workers, for Her Majesty does a good deal of
brain work in the 24 hours, and keeps it up all the year
round.
Why does anybody ever die ?
“Banjo” Patterson stands a chance of
being known in future as “The man fromModder
River.” He still appears to regard the war as a
kind of amateur steeplechase, and the racing
adventures of both Boer and Briton occupy a large
space in his letters. He is evidently very proud
of having been the first Britisher to enter Bloem-
fontein, but of course there was a horse at the
bottom of it: “Banjo’s” moke'was a Myles-na-
Coppaleen colt with more foot than the mounts
of his competitors.
That most popular pressman, Argus war-
correspondent, Donald Macdonald, returns thin,
shrunken, and with a weak stoop; but otherwise
well enough. The sea voyage revived him, but
the doctors in Capetown told him it would be
simply suicide to stay there. He doesn’t bring
back any confirmation of the tales of atrocity by
the Boers; in fact, he declares that the British
Tommy was quite as bad as the Boer in regard
to occasional offences against the accepted usages
of modern warfare.
Thompson Moore, the Melbourne mining
manager and politician, tells a story of the days
when he was storekeeping at Maldon in the Fifties
with an American named Tays. One day a cer-
tain Jimmy Wilson, an old “lag ” from Tas-
mania, dropped in and asked him to keep
a bag of gold safe for him, as he was on the
spree. Moore put it away safe, and next day
Wilson called and told him one of his drays
had broken down in the Black Forest, between
Gisborne and Woodend, and he wanted a loan of
£35 to make a deposit on another horse and con-
veyance. Moore, knowing he had the security
of the gold, advanced the money. Just
when Wilson left, however, it occurred
to Moore to look into the bag, and
there lie found only bits of lead.
He ran up the street, found Wil-
son in a store buying boots,
and throttled him. The lag
drew his pistol, but the shop-
man secured and discharged it,
and with the help of a policeman
Moore recovered his £35. That night when Moore
was lying awake in bed he had a thought,
and at daybreak he got up and walked 25
miles to Bendigo, where his ’Murkan
partner had charge of a branch. “Any
strangers here lately?” asked Moore.
“Only Jimmy Wilson,” said Tays. “Did
you give him anything?” “A shake-
down. ” * * Anything else ? ” “I lent him
£35,” said Tays. “You’re ‘had,’” said
Moore. “No fear,” returned Tays, with a
wink, “ I’ve got his gold.” “ Let’s see it,” said
Moore. “Here it is,” and Moore, on examining
the bag, found it contained lead, like the bag on
which he himself had lent £35 at Maldon the
day before. From that day to now Thomp-
son Moore has never seen or heard of Tas-
manian Jimmy or the £35 lent him by partner
Tays.
Including Col. Mackay, M.L.C. of N.S.
Wales, Australia will soon have three legislators
at the front in South Africa. Connolly, M.P. for
Dundas, went with the second W.A. contingent,
and Roberts, M.P. for Gladstone, goes with the
S.A. Imperial bushmen. Roberts, during the
two or three weeks he has been in camp, has been
promoted from trooper to lieutenant, so that he
ought soon to be a field-marshal. He has long
been known locally as “Bobs,” which may have
something to do with his preferment.
Baden Powell will, if he consents to
take the warlike gifts got up for him in Sydney by
Sunday press subscriptions, require to obtain leave
before he does so. The Duke of Wellington
declined in such circumstances; Napier also.
Marshal McMahon accepted a sword, but it was
after defeat. Rochefort accepted a sword from
Sydney admirers, but it was also after defeat and
while a fugitive ; and Cronje, for whom Rochefort
is now “ raising ” a sword, also occupies the posi-
tion of defeated warrior.
R. C. Patterson, one of the new members
of Assembly for Hobart, is a native of Victoria,
and was a schoolfellow of Judge Molesworth,
Carbine Wallace, and Col. Tom Price. He is a
civil engineer and contractor, and has built rail-
ways in various provinces—including the Sorell
(Tas.) line. He had command of the telegraph
party which, in 1871-2, made the line from Adelaide
to Port Darwin, from the Port end, and it was he
who fixed the last bit of wire connecting Australia
with the rest of the world.

The luck of pretty Mrs. Fraser with a Melb.
jury recalls the equally good fortune of Madeline
Smith (tried in ’56 for poisoning her lover) with
a Scotch judge and jury. The evidence for
the prosecution was clear, and the verdict “ not
proven.” The judge was well known to be
peculiarly susceptible to feminine charms. Just
before the summing-up, prisoner’s counsel, Inglis,
said to her : “ You have a pretty foot and ankle,
Miss Smith ;be careful to show them.” Several
inches of well-filled stocking were protruded, the
handsome girl assumed her most fetching expres-
sion, and the hypnotised judge charged dead in her
favor. It must also be remembered that the vic-
tim was a foreigner who had behaved meanly.
Among the rumors as to what happened to the
fair Madeline after her acquittal, none were so
persistent as the one that she married a medical
man who resided in a Melb. suburb
until a few years ago.
The death of Lieut.-Col. Phil-
lips, Commissioner of Westralian
Police, removes one of the very last
vestiges of old-time convict regula-
tions. Born in Westralia, reared
and educated in it, Phillips declined
to believe there was a superior spot
on earth, or better Government in
creation. He was the protege of
Gov. Weld, and pampered Broome.
Thriving under such conditions, he
was taken by surprise when the gold-
fields broke out. The “ Tothersider ”
invasion was too much for him. He
persisted in the old local custom of
a policeman after 7 p.m. challenging
belated citizens with the call “Bond
or Free?” To this day, the watch-
house book containing the entries of
drunks, &c., has a column with the
heading “B. or. F.” The Colonel
refused to march with civilization
until absolutely pushed along. On
high days and holidays he used to
swagger about with sword and sabre-
tache, in a glittersome uniform, re-
sembling that of some of the Imperial
Hussar Regiments. The new Wes-
tralian Commissioner of Police,
Warden Hare, of Kalgoorlie, is the
man who, when John Forrest in-
sulted the Kalgoorlie people and got
hustled, read the Riot Act, and called
upon 50 constables to charge over
10,000 people. Fortunately, Sub-In-
spector Holmes declined to carry out
the order, and thus serious riot was
averted. For his “loyalty” to
Forrest, Hare now gets his reward.
Ex-District Court Judge Dowling, of
Woollahra (N.S.W.), now in his 81st year, was
Attorney-General of the Port Curtis district in
1849. He spent nearly ten years there, and wit-
nessed most of the exciting scenes of early gold-
fields discoveries. It was mere chance which
caused his return to Sydney, as, had he stayed,
the highest judicial position was open to him in
the new province of Queensland.

new Commissioner of Police in Westralia, ex-
Warden Hare, graduated at Govt. House. Phil-
lips was Private Sec. to Gov. Hampton ; Hare to
Gov. Broome. But whereas the elder man had no
police experience, the younger was for some time
in the force as inspector : and his experience as
P.M., Govt. Resident, and Warden ought to help
him. The late Commissioner was the new one’s
brother-in-law —but, then, being both good
’Gropers it was difficult to escape some relation-
ship.
Australian Star (Sydney), in its issue of
4th April, reprinted Victor Daley’s verses “ His
Conqueror” which originally appeared in The
Bulletin of 13th Jan. this year. But it carefully
abstained from acknowledging this paper as the
source of its steal, and credited the verses to the
Times of Natal (South Africa). This paper is
quite accustomed to be stolen from, sometimes
with acknowledgment and oftener without, but
when the matter that it has paid for is stolen
(by a paper printed in Sydney) and credited to
other papers it is time to say things.

certainty for one of the new N.S.W. Upper House
appointments. Meagher was a Dibbs’ nominee in
’94, when Gov. Duff’s obstinacy put a stop to the
appointments. Apropos —during a recent visit to
Dublin “John” was taken round by an Irish
guide to see the sights, and visited a certain church
which had been restored at great expense by the
late Guinness, of stout-brewing fame. Looking
up at a statue which had been erected to the de-
parted Bung, “John’’asked the guide, “I sup-
pose Mr. Guinness was a very popular man, eh ? ”
The guide looked mysteriously round, and then
whispered confidentially, “Shure, sor, it’s glad
he’d be, where he is now, to have a dhropof hisown
porther ! ”
Dr. Hall-Owen, who has just been taken
off by enteric fever at Chieveley Hospital, Natal,
was an amusing identity in South Melbourne. In
orating he was afflicted with the er—er —ah
malady to which Gov. Brassey was so pronounced
a victim. The doctor had a knack of turning up
to South Melb. Council meetings when the pro-
ceedings were half over, sometimes attired in a
blazing uniform or in evening-dress. During his
term of office as Mayor he made things hum
socially. When he announced his departure to
the front as a surgeon to the British forces, he
said he intended, after seeing the campaign
through, to proceed to England to complete his
studies of military surgery. But, unfortunately,
he found that in Africa a through ticket to the
Great Beyond can be easily obtained without ven-
turing into the firing-line. In addition to his many
other offices, the late surgeon-captain was a J.P.,
but seldom sat on the Bench. Yet the ostentatious
exercise of his office of honorary beak once got
him into a hot comer at the police station. One
Sunday afternoon, a grass widow of good position
was brought to the lock-up in a state of d.t’s.
The void made by hubby’s absence she had en-
deavored to fill by tanglefoot, with disastrous
results—-her delusion being that she was the centre
of attraction of a number of telephones, which
had all been mixed up, and were jarring her
brain most agonisingly. The bobby in charge
knew who she was, and instead of putting
her into a cell, he told her to sit on a
stool in the passage, and sent a man for the
medico J.P. in order to have her committed
direct to the gaol hospital. The doctor duly
arrived in a condition of stately fussiness.
“ What’s this ?” he exclaimed, as he spotted the
victim. “ Constable, I know this—ah—lady. She
should—er—er— neve? have been brought here. I
will look into this Now, madam, I will dis-
charge you at once.’ 1 And the doctor seated him-
self at the office-table and indicated that the
prisoner was to be brought in. But the watch-
nouse-keeper at once determined that the watch-
house prestige should be upheld. Stepping into
the passage he tapped the victim of electrical dis-
turbances on the shoulder. The poor woman put
up her trembling hands and said, “ What’s he
going to do?” With a cast-iron chuckle the
bobby whispered, “ He’s going to put another tele-
phone on to yez.” That settled it. Springing up
with a steam-siren yell the woman swooped upon

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 17~>
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

Aboriginalities.
A close call 1 Two miners were sitting
outside a Broken Hill mine-drive, waiting for a
charge to explode. They brought out the explo-
sives they had not used, and let a lighted candle
fall into the box. Instead of mincing up both,
though, the explosion mangled one and simply
tore off the other’s trousers and one of his boots 1
Compare that luck with another’s—his call came
two or three days after. He was standing on a
platform, which gave way ; and he was beginning
a fall of 50ft. among rocks when his hands touched
a man-rope, and clutched it. It was a marvellous
escape ; and the man’s mates were just reckoning
that he had the luck of a dozen, when a solitary
rock bounded down from somewhere, struck him
on the chest, and killed him almost instantly.
* * * *
“ Gobung ” tells a fish-story :
I had gone up the Fitzroy from Rockhampton to fish
for barramundi, having only with me Bobby, a retriever.
I landed at the mouth of a creek, and fishing all day
caught three fish, averaging 121 b. As it was getting
dusk I hooked what must have been a 30-pounder, at
least, but after a great struggle the line parted, and I
lost him. It was then all but dark, and as I was winding
up the remnants of the line, I suddenly caught sight of
something black swimming straight for where I stood.
I gazed for a moment, and then there flashed into my
mind the memory of an alligator which was said to have
killed a calf just about that spot. Then as the thing
drew near enough for me to catch the gleam of two
ferocious eyes, I threw down the rod and ran for my life.
I had gone barely 20 yards when I heard the creature
throw itself out of the water and rush up the bank. I
strained myself to the utmost, but I knew the thing was
gaining on me—then something seemed to rise from the
ground and hit me a violent thump on the forehead, and
I knew no more. When I came-to it was moonlight, and
I was lying at the foot of a tree—which I must have
crashed into in my blind haste --- with a pain in
the head ; Bobby standing over me, licking the blood
from a wound in my forehead. I reached out my hand
and found his coat wet. Then I knew. My monster had
been only the dog, who had, unobserved, gone in for a
swim on his “ own.”

“ Cecil P.” : Recently read a stiff yarn
about an orchid in Cuba whioh dropped its ten-
drils at night and drew up and strangled some
Yankee sailors, yet in N.S.W. coastal range are
J. W. HECKENBERG, a Champion Australian Axeman.
vines which, in order to feed their roots, drop
down and pick up all bits of stick, bark, &c., near
the tree upon which they are parasites. In face
of this, of the existence of carnivorous plants and
the growth of creepers to a possible support, why
talk of “ missing links ” ? Practically, there are
none. This oldest of lands revealed the missing-
link between mammal and reptile, and some of
the vegetables show characteristics usually sup-
posed to be purely animal. Also, if there is any
meaning in words, plants have certainly a nervous
system. Re carnivorous plants. Q resident of
Woodville, near Binda, once showed me grass on
his run which he believed was carnivorous—the
flies becoming stuck to its glutinous tips.

“ D. 8.” : Out vvest lately I came across a
hale old man who had been through the Crimean
war —showed me his medals. He was nearly
penniless, but declined to accept anything he had
not earned. Had tramped hundreds of miles
since he parted with his last job as “private
tutor” to a selector’s family at 7s. a week. He
told me he lost the billet owing to a governess
from Sydney engaging to fill the post for 6s. a
week, with “light household duties” thrown in !

“J.A.W.”: Have not seen anywhere
noted the tenacity with which the Australian
aboriginal clings to the literal terms of a bargain.
In one case where a black was asked to procure a
kangaroo for a visitor going back to Sydney, he
was promised a shilling and a flask of powder
(value also a shilling) ; when the darkie returned
with the ’roo he indignantly refused 2s. 6d. ten-
dered him because it was too late to buy the pow-
der. At the same place it was the custom for the
lady of the house when she wanted game to give a
certain black a gun and ammunition, and send him
forth, the condition being that she was to have all
he shot and pay him one shilling. One day when
Billy was returning with a fine lot of pigeons he
was waylaid by another housewife, who offered
him half-a-crown for the lot ; but Billy was
mightily insulted at the idea of being expected to
take more than his regular customer’s price.

“A.T.S.” : He “ the luck of drunks and
children.” Twenty odd years ago James Crocker,
who was bookkeeper for Henry Simpson and
Son, coal merchants of Adelaide, was driving a
waggonette containing his family across the rail-
way line near the N. Adelaide station when the
waggonette was run into by a train. Mrs. Crocker
was killed, and the baby she was nursing was
found after the accident on the front part of the
engine, unhurt, and is, I believe) alive at present.

“Broke” Billy, a boundary-rider from
Bulga Brush, never refused a request for a pipeful
or a chew of tobacco, yet he hardly ever parted
with any. When asked for some he would put
his hand into his pocket and pull out two or three
plugs of tobacco mixed with many soda-crystals.
“Oh, dash it 1 ” he would say, “the stncknin’
musta got loose in me pockit summow ; but, bless
yer, it won’t hurt th’ tobacca.” And, of course,
the’reply was, “ Oh, never mind, thanks.”

“ G. 8.” (B. 31/3/’00) and
leaning trees. Trees don't
lean towards the sun, though
they do lean towards the
light. In the forest-clad
ranges of S. Gippsland the
timber is remarkably sym-
metrical except on sloping
ground where fully 99 per
cent, of the trees show well-
developed branches facing
the gorge, while the side
fronting the hill is notice-
ably sparse of foliage. The
mountain ash and sassafras,
for instance, in the bottom
of the gorges shoot up
straight and even, while on
either side of the ravine
trees of the same kind are
conspicuously lop - sided.
The statement that trees
twist from east to north is
mere bunkum. In the same
forests, where a few years
ago the bush roads looked
like so many timber-yards,
the paling-splitters had a
superstitious dislike to “left
hand twists,” though trees
winding to the right were in
freat favor for palings.
'he majority of trees dis-
carded by these bushmen
twisted from east to north.
—Bo-peep.

» * * *
Work on the Uganda (Africa) railway line
laid actually to be stopped for three weeks, lately,
on account of the ravages of a pair of huge man-eat-
ing lions, which had terrorised the workers (5000 of
them !) from March, ’99, to December —when they
were killed by Mr. Patterson, a Scotch engineer
having during that period slain and eaten over 80
Indian and native laborers, besides severely injur-
ing others. The fearful shrieks of the victims
rang in the ears night after night, till no one knew
whose turn would come next. Sound men lay and
listened to the cracking of bones and the tearing
of limbs within 50 yards of the place where they
were, and sick men in hospital expired from sheer
terror as they listened to the monsters quarrelling
over their feast. Twenty shots were fired in the
dark at the spot where the lions were eating a man,
and yet they finished him to the last bone. They
would spring over the high thorn “boma,”pick
up a man, and trot round with him, looking for
the best way out, as a cat carries a rat. . . . One
of the brutes was a huge maneless lion Oft. Bin.
long and 3ft. 9in. high. Its last meal had been an
African native. The other was 9ft. 6in. long and
3ft. llin. high. Both beasts killed men solely
for food, though the country round swarms with
every description of game.
» • • *
The sub-protector of aborigines in Far
North 9. A. has reported that births have exceeded
deaths up there for some months past, and that
the natives are living well, in spite of severe and
continuous drought. Which seems to support a
recent Bulletin contention that Centralia was
built to resist drought, and that the deterioration
of the occupied country is due to overstocking
during drought-time.
ONE TOUCH OF NATURE.
In wayback N.S. W. recently a litter of puppies lost their mother, and were taken
in maternal charge by a hen—which brooded over the infant dogs as if they
were so many chicks.
“ H.S.” : The little white tail-tips of the
recently much-discussed bilby are in great
request amongst the aboriginals of Central
Australia as ornaments. Occasionally a big
darkie will be seen with one wee white tip
absurdly displayed, though sometimes this adorn-
ment consists of hundreds of these little tips.
Sometimes a particularly stylish young “ buck ”
wears a tail or two dangling down his spine sus-
pended from a “chignon” on the head, made of
emu-feathers, hair, grease, blood and ochre. Neck
ornaments of human hair twine are often also
finished off with bilby tail-tips; whioh, again, are
sometimes worn gummed on to the nose by one
end, the white snowing-up strongly against the
black-bearded face. These bilby-tails are in great
demand by tribes in whose territory the animal is
not found, consequently on the Georgina and
Herbert Downs and elsewhere they are bartered
for such weapons and implements as are not
possessed of good quality by the local natives.
Note: The bilby is really a species (Lagotis) of
bandicoot.

“ Potts ” : Afghans are not good to camels,
though they are not perhaps intentionally cruel;
but their one remedy for disease, sores, or broken
bones is a red-hot iron or burning stick. Have
seen them tie an unfortunate beast down (his
injury being a dislocated shoulder) and then score
him with a red-hot wire.
“ Chas. F.”: (“ E.K.,” 8.,
24/2/’OO, p. 14) Rabbit-proof netting is
not any good. If the rabbits don’t
climb over, crawl under, or break
through, the swaggie who wants work
heaves them over. Rabbits dumped
here in W.A. won’t live —too much
poison. But if allowed to come of
their own accord (and border netting
can’t stop them) they will doubtless
acclimatise and avoid the poison. An
imported horse or cow turned out here
won’t live more than a few days, but
wild cattle bred here never get poi-
soned. Know a “ remittance ” farming
drunk who wired in alarm to his
wealthy English father that the rabbits
were approaching, and please wire £l4O
for netting round selection. Money
sent in hot haste; and it was. then
explained that, as the rabbits were on
an island 20 miles from the shore, and
had been there for several years, there
was no immediate danger.
“Y. Kotze " : “ When the price
of tin was low ” —that ought to make a
good heading for a thousand Northern
reminiscences. Once struck the camp
of an old hatter who lived precariously
out of very attenuated tailings. His
tucker-bill was low. And no wonder !
One of his devices consisted in tying
up a pinch of tea in a piece of mosquito-
curtain (wherever he got that) and
dipping it into the boiling billy. The
time of immersion depended on the
result of the day’s labor, or the pros-
pects of the tin-market. When I called
he paid me the compliment of an extra
2-seconds’ dip. At the week’s end a
fresh pinch would be inserted, and the
anaemic old charge converted into
tobacco-substitute. There are always
men about who deride thrift and try
to ridicule that sober, saving instinct.
Improbable persons have told me that
the ashes of their unday smoke werSe
used as tooth-powder afterwards. But

nearly as fast as

smart man can
run him down on
a bot day on foot,
and nine dogs
out of ten can
catch him any
day providing
he is not too
near his burrow.
To the average
dingo that can

matter. No dog,
however, is fond
of rabbit, and
those who eat it
quickly lose con-
dition. Also,
“ Eller” may not
know that rabbit
coursing is done
with fox-terriers,
which have no difficulty in running-up to bunny
after the first spurt.

Dear Bulletin. —I know a man who is
prepared to swear that he has known a dog to re-
cover after having his stomach opened to extract
a bait from it; the stomach then being washed
out with soap-and-water and sewn up again. Is
such a thing possible ?—W.G.C.
* * * •
The blacks in parts of N.Q. believe firmly
in re-incamation. If a blackfellow dies they say
his soul goes into a hole in a mountain and re-
mains there until a child is about to be born. The
soul then passes into the body of the newly-born
child. As this child grows up he claims two sots
of relatives. Besides his present brothers and
sisters he claims also the brothers and sisters of
the defunct blackfellow whose soul he appro-
priated. At the same time no kinship is recog-
nised between the two sets of brothers and sisters
—barring their joint relationship to this indivi-
dual.

“ Gobung ” : Not generally known that
the frog is a cannibal. Writer placed a large
brown one in a tin with a number of smaller ones,
and subsequently found him “surrounding” his
mates.

Dear Bulletin. — Re “X.’s” par. (B.
25/11/’99) I have also seen worms in the intestines
Miss ALICE HOLLANDER, the rising Contralto.
Photo., The Falk Studios.
of rabbits, and have found peculiar cysts consist-
ing of white specks and a watery substance im-
bedded in the flesh of one of the hind legs or in
the back, close to the kidneys. These cysts are
about an inch through and go off with a fizz when
punctured. Diseased bunnies are usually fat, and
trappers bundle them off to be frozen every time.
HIS MISTAKE.
Came a woolly man to the chapel small,
And he sat in the foremost pew ;
He was spare of limb, he was tanned and tall,
And he came from the bush, we knew ;
For his looks were strange, and his clothes unfit,
And his manners were rather rude.
When the sermon flagged on his plug he bit,
Then he settled him down and chewed.
When the plate appeared at the stranger’s side
For a moment it puzzled his wits—
Then, “ Oh, thanks,” says he, with a touch of pride,
“ But it’a seldom I ever spits.”
S.S.

“ I want Warner’s Safe Cure
NOT SOMETHING BETTER.”
I know just what I want. We have used
Warner’s Safe Cure in our family for biliousness
and for attacks of kidney disease, and know all
about it. I don’t know anything about a cheaper
substitute “just as good.” Warner’s Safe. Cure
cures, and that’s what I want. It cured a friend
of mine, whose companionship I value as dearly
as life, of Bright’s Disease, and my son’s wife used
to always suffer from bilious headaches until she
took Warner’s “Safe ” Cure ; and now you want to
put me off with some new-fangled, high-soundin’
medicine. Just give me a bottle of Warner’s
“Safe’’Cure, or I will go elsewhere. I know
what I want; I’m buying it for myself for liver
trouble, and I want the best —the tried and proven,
never failing, and that’s Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure.
You don’t make as much profit on Warner’s
“Safe” Cure as on “something just as good.”
Well, I’m sorry, but then I didn’t fail in health
just to make you a millionaire. You can get
cheap things of all descriptions, but when you’re ill
you want the best,*
• •
Kuppers Elberfeld Lager.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
“ For England and All ” : You, like the rest ot
the Jingo crew, quote the and Daily Mail and
Standard ; but you take care not to quote London
Chronicle, or Echo, or Manchester Guardian Makoro:
No interest. The old gentleman may be included in the
600 whom Henry Parkes, with a lofty hand-wave, said he
had seen pass through Parliament and leave no trace
behind Mab. :If all the other rhymesters were dead,
and it was the last bit of verse in the world, we’d extin-
guish the breed in you Orara : You leave us in the
dark, because you send no news-clipping as to the prelimi.
nary proceedings at the coronial enquiry or at the police-
court. Broadly speaking, the Crown in N.S.W. takes no
trouble at all as to defending a prisoner on trial for his
life. Formerly, £5 ss. was supposed to be available for
the defence of a moneyless prisoner capitally arraigned
(though it was Bar etiquette to take no money for such
service) ; but through the good offices of Mr. G. B. (now
Mr. Justice) Simpson when Attorney-General, the sum
placed at the disposal of a prisoner so situated was in-
creased to 15 guineas, which no doubt is always
claimed. The Crown Law officer, or the Judge at
the trial, calls upon any barrister available to defend a
pauper capitally arraigned, and, generally, the choice
falls upon some otherwise briefless junior who happens to
be present. We cannot comment on the present case
as the officially-stated facts are not before us

It is not always wise to be a civil hero.
A certain latitude is allowed to the military hero,
but a Melbourne larrikin has just discovered the
folly of the theory that virtue is its own reward.
He saved two boys from drowning in the Yarra,
after a long run and a hard battle necessitating
medical assistance to preserve the hero from the
effects of exhaustion and a barrel-full of Yarra
juices. This was all very fine, and the papers said
pretty things about the valiant young man, but it
reminded a soulless police that a youth of his
name, age, weight and general outline and charac-
teristics was wanted for various minor offences,
not to mention such frivolities as hitting police-
men with building material. So the law seized on
the absent-minded beggar, and he was sentenced
to ten days without the option. Result —another
Victorian Cross. No Victorian was ever crosser.
“ Puyyaka ” on the plague :
The prevailing popular ignorance on this subject would
be ludicrous were it not in connection with so serious a
matter. The oracles of business-trains discourse learnedly
upon typhus and immunised horses, while the Solomon
Eagle of the bar-floor adjures great uncleansed Australia
to avoid the injection of “ stuff out of niggers in Injer.”
It was to one of these latter a medico said: “Look at
the color, you fool; if it was got out of a nigger wouldn’t
it be black ? ” The “ logic ” was accepted and the ob-
jecfcor bared his arm for the needle. Of the two chief
prophylactic agents Haffkine’s has the Australian floor
at present. The other, Yersin’s, is mainly curative,
though it also possesses certain prophylactic powers. No
others have been tried on a sufficiently large scale to
give trustworthy conclusions, though the work of
Galcotti and Lustig, of Florence University, seem to trend
in the direction of the ideal. Iliffkine, bacteriologist,
in Allahabad, India, worked long at the problem before he
solved it. Two difficulties arose with the medium—for a
fluid medium is necessary for the suspension of the actual
inoculative material. It had to be of such a composition
that the. religious sensibilities of natives would not be
offended, and it had to provide sufficient aeration for the
growth of the bacilli. The first difficulty was got over by
the use of goat-flesh digested with hydrochloric acid and
neutralised, the second by the addition of oil drops, from
the lower surface of which the bacilli could suspend them-
selves. In later brews, wheat pulp has been used instead
of flesh, to overcome Hindu prejudice against the medium.
Inoculated with a culture of plague, this is gently
shaken daily for from 2 to 6 weeks, brought to a certain
pitch of translucency discernible by the practised eye,
sterilised of the bacteria of inflammation, and
bottled in heat - sealed sterile glass tubes. Then
it forms a brown fluid, slightly turbid and of a pecu-
liar heavy scent. A dose of 5 cubic centimetres (1£
drachms, English) injected under the skin of an adult—
generally on the outside of the left arm—will usually pro-
tect. against death from plague for a period variously
estimated at from 6 to 12 months. Graduated doses are
used for women and children. Certain constitutional
effects Comm'inly follow, and in the Indian service a rise
of temperature to 102° is looked upon as essential for
efficient inoculation. This disturbance varies with con-
stitution, but probably it is caused by other constituents
than the actual immunising agent ; in some cases,
doubtless by insufficient sterilization measures. But
this is not an adequate explanation, and until further
experiment is made we can only accept the Indian
method. The protection conferred is, estimated at the
best, by no means absolute, but it has a very
great effect on the death-rate. It is necessary in any
case that the constitutional effect shall have passed-off
before any protection can be expected. In popular lan-
guage, the protective agent must have got completely
“ into the system.” Indian statistics show encouraging
returns. Of a Parsee colony of 304 souls, 277 were inocu-
lated at the beginning of an epidemic, with a return of 6
cases and no deaths. Of the 27 who refused inoculation,
6 took plague and 4 died. In Byculla Gaol last year, of
the uninoculated 4 per cent, took it, and of the inocu-
lated only - 6 per cent. In Lanauli Hospital the ratio of
attacks was 6.8 to 1, of deaths 67 to lof these. Similar
results could be quoted indefinitely obtained, too,
from a native population whose bare limbs, exposed
to slight injury of various kinds, present opening for
infection such as does not occur with European-clad
people. For, in the great majority of cases, plague is an
inoculated disease. A tiny scratch, a pimple, a scarcely
perceptible abrasion may furnish a door for the bacillus.
Except in epidemics, the inhalative form—plague-
pneumonia—seldom occurs. When it does, it means
death—a ghastly death where the lungs struggle against
a poison turning their delicate cells to putridity. Turn
up Defoe on the Great Plague of 300 years ago if you want
to know what plague-pneumonia is. Those who assert
that the Sydney outbreak is one of typhus can never have
witnessed either typhus or plague. A typhus case can be
diagnosed before the door is opened. The mortality of
typhus equals that of plague, and the characteristic rapid
onset, spotty eruption and ever-haunting smell make it
unmistakeable. But in typhus the short bacillus of the
shape of a Manilla Victoria cigar is never seen, though it
is visible in many microscopes in Sydney and Melbourne
at present—in Sydney specimens. There is no mistake.
The people of Sydney have among them as ghastly a pro-
duct of Eastern filth as ever swept a town off the map.
They have provided by their negligence a culture-ground
for an epidemic which may yet devastate the world.
Dr. G-resswell, President of Victorian
Board of Health : “ There is no danger in coming
to Sydney at the present time. Of course, it
depends a great deal on where the visitors go.
To come to a place like The Australia Hotel is
to run no risk at all.”*
Master-puncher Lawrence Foley, in colla-
boration with a well-known Sydney sporting jour-
nalist, is writing a book on the Art of Boxing—
which will, no doubt, be called “My ‘Scrap’
Book,” with the accent on the “scrap.” Larry
is still a man of excellent physique, and could,
with a few weeks’ training, get through a respect-
able battle—though it might not compare in vigor
with that he fought 30 years ago against old-time
champion, Sandy Ross —“For Foley and the
Green,” as the almost-forgotten ballad had it.
Foley’s comfortable residence at Waverley, Syd-
ney, is frequently visited by sporting cognoscenti
from England and America, where the retired
champion’s name is very well known indeed, and
it was to an American visitor who pilgrimaged to
the shrine of St. Laurence, in company with a
Bulletin man, that Foley unburdened himself
recently—somewhat to the following effect: —
“ You were askin’ me just now why I haven’t sent
you any champions lately like Peter Jackson find
Fitzsimmons. Now, listen a’me ! It ain’t because
I can’t. They’re here, undeveloped ! Yes,
mister (are you listenin’) I tell you this : 1 could
pick out a hundred men in N.S. Wales to-day,
keep my eye on ’em, try ’em out, and in 12 months
I’d stake a’most anything but my ’mortal sold
that I’d have three world-beaters from out of the
crowd. It’s the country and the feedin’. I’ve
thought it out (see what I mean) an’ I’m sure of
it! Look at the best fightin’ animals, and the
gamest; listen ’a me—the gamest. What are
they ? —why, the lion and the tiger ! What are
they fed on?—meat! —see what I mean ? That’s
the secret. The Australian eats meat with a
big big whatever letter meat commences
with. It doesn’t matter what their occupa-
tion is, if they’re reared op meat they are
natural fighters, and game. Of course, it’s
better if they’ve got an -Irish rather —see what
I mean —but a meat diet’s the principal thing.
They used to tell a yarn about me trainin’ two
holy bishops of the Church to spar, and that they
had a habit of scrappin’ at my place. Well, it
aint quite true ; but couldn’t Archbishop Vaughan
use ’em ! See what I mean ! Down on my knees
I went to his lordship on the wharf, when he was
foin’ away, and, says he, ‘My blessin’ on you,
iawrence.’ An’ then he added, ‘ It’s one cham-
pion seein’ another off.’ Rest his soul ! An’ one
of my relations; he’s a priest, you know—a meat-
fed priest. He’s up in Queensland now, an’, of
course, out of respect for his holy office, he doesn’t
go ‘ lookin’ for it,’ but, for a priest, he has a very
dirty left and a good right—eye, toe, an’ hand all
in a line. We sent him to Ireland to make a
priest of him. Jim Punch an’ me took out his
passage. The si lippin’-clerk looks at Tom an’ looks
at me, an’ says, ‘ Rowin’ man or pug ? ’ —meanin’ my
nephew, for there’s a discount of 20 per cent, on
professionals. ‘No,’ says I, ‘ the young feller’s
a-goin’ into the church.’ ‘True bill,’ says Jim
Punch —for we could see that that shippin’-clerk
found it hard to swaller that Larry Foley and Jim
Punch tuck such a real interest in the true reli-
gion ! But about meat-eatin’ for fighters—shall I
tell you a yarn ? See what I mean ! There once
was a rich old English lady, who came to Ireland
and saw what fine children an Irish working
woman had. So she ups and says, ‘Bridget,
what will I have to do to get childer like youm ? ’
‘Feed ’em on praties an’ buttermilk,’ says
Bridget. So the lady was delighted, and gev
Bridget lialf-a-crown and says, ‘ I’ll take back
with me some real Irish potatoes and buttermilk,
MASTER-PUNCHER LARRY FOLEY .
Photo., Brand.
and then I’ll have fine childer ! ’ ‘But,’ says
Bridget, with a sly wink, ‘to have really fine
childer, I’m thinkin’ that you’d have to take Pat
along wid you, too.’ What, ho ! she bumps !
Was anyone listening? Well, anyway, there
you are,’ added Lawrence, ‘Australian fighters
are made with meat —if ye have Pat atony with it,
too. Coin’to put it into print ?—well, be careful
of the grammar ! See what I mean ? ”
On veldt and round the kopjes
They’ll shed their life-blood there,
And their graves in memorandum
Be wet by England’s tear !
A GOOD MAN DAMNED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I stand beside his grave alone,
A nd, spite of tears, I laugh
To read upon the milk-white stone
A stranger's epitaph.
T understood, him more than wife
Or mother old and grey,
They saw the good side of his life;
I—where the evil lay!
“ A Christian gone to his reward!"
“A h ushand kind and true! ”
Lies! Lies! He “gathered to the Lord
The proud, wild man 1 knew!
And yet for him I weep to-day,
A sinner past Jits span,
A nd long to wipe those lies away.
And unite: “A d good man! ”
E.D.

MELBOURNIANA.


These are the houses that Jerry built.
This is the filth that lay in the houses that Jerry built.
These are the Inspectors who didn’t see the filth that
lay in the houses that J errf built.
This is the Mayor who said you could eat your dinner
off the floors of the houses that contained the filth that
the Inspectors didn’t see, and that fed the rat that,
spread the plague germinated in the houses that Jerry
built.
And this is the money saved by the
City Council by not cleaning the
streets where stood the houses that
contained the filth that, etc., etc.
And these are the graves of the victims of the plague
that was spread by the rat that lived on the filth that
lay in the houses that Jerry built.

Heaven’s sake, kill him, Syd.”
He will not, be happy till he gets that oven.

One woman in Bruce (M.L.) district is the
personification of modesty. She describes herself
on the electoral roll as ‘ 1 nothing.
“ Carpe ” : I once asked a man who knew
what it felt like to be hanged. He was a young
Spanish sailor, who had murdered the first-mate of
his ship, at Levuka, in 1873. It was before the
annexation of the islands, and the Sheriff, feeling
funky about his authority to put a man to death,
got 20 or 30 townspeople to haul on a rope attached
to a high pole on the beach. As the night-dews
made the rope stiff and prevented the noose from
running properly, the whole thing was a. fiasco, and
the culprit was returned to gaol, from which he
got away after a term of imprisonment. Said the
victim of this weird experience: ‘‘When I was
being hauled up I was half-strangled, and the pain
caused by my neck having to sustain the weight
of my body was so great that I became unconscious
before I reached the top, of the pole. When I
opened my eyes, a h w minutes afterwards, my face
was turned upwards to the sky, and I could see
nothing but the vast expanse of blue. I thought
I was dead, and I said to myself ‘ Am I in Heaven
or in Hell ?’ After a while I wriggled about until
I got my face turned downwards. Then, seeing
the people on the beach I realised my position and
sang out to them to shoot me quickly, as I could
not die that way. But no one would do it, and
after a brief conference I was lowered to the
Sound and sent back to gaol. No, I don’t like
ing hanged, though the stiffness in my neck
soon wore off. The worst part of the business was
the stifling sensation when I was first suspended.
I would rather be shot than killed that way.”

A SHATTERED NERVOUS SYSTEM
Neuralgia, Indigestion, AATeak and Hopeless.
COMPI iICATION S YIELD TO CLEMENTS lONIC
An Influential Cure Worthy of Careful Perusal.
The Oam of
Mrs, JOHN GEDDES.
{Reported for the Newtown Chronicle.)
When the tram cars pass by Enmore they
goeed with a rush down a gentle incline,
j drfcipg a park, over which the traveller
(arches glimpses of the distant sea. Pre-
the tram gives a sudden swerve round
a diSftd, apd the guard shouts, “Victoria
fiiladT” It was here our reporter descended,
almost at the door of Mr. Geddes, a well-
known storekeeper of the Marrickville
district. He inquired for Mrs. Geddes, who
wag, fortunately, at-home, yet who answered
bhS.reporter’s enquiries with some, little
Imitation, as she does not like publicity ; at
tfie same time her fueling of thankfulness for
unremarkable recovery from dire sickness
was so great that she thought it only, right
td; others, placed as she had been, to put
her-hesitation on one side, and make known
die true facts of the case.
“We were living at Bundanoon at that
iine,” said Mrs. Geddes, “and owing to
iome unknown cause I got into a bad state
of .health, continued to get worse and
worse, till my plight was quite pitiful.
This lasted for two years.”
“ How did your , sickuess affect you, Mrs.
Geddes ? ” asked the reporter.
“My most serious trouble was indiges-
tion, which at last came to such a stage
that I could not keep any food on my
stomach. Even when I tried to drink a
little pure water I should retch it up at
once ; and the water that came up when I
Vomited would be quite black to look at.
showing, I think, that my digestive organs
were in a shocking and deplorable state.
And yet all the tin e I had a strong desire
and craving to eat, and I dared not. . There
was ever present a feeling of oppression and
weight upon my chest, and a sharp pain
between the shoulders that pierced right
through my body, so that it filled me with
apprehensions that my lungs were affected,
and that I should soon be a victim to con-
sumption. My limbs were in a constant
tremor, my heart palpitating with distress-
ing throbs. Neuralgia came in my face
and forehead, and was so bad at times I
thought 1 should go out of my mind. And,
with my general collapse, my eyesight
suffered ; clouds of mist hid my sight when
I tried to use my eyes for any length of
time. The natural colour of my skin
changed to a deep yellow, like one suffering
from jaundice, and I would almost have
passed for a mulatto. I am sure that my
nervous system was shattered. I was
weak, hopeless, and out of heart, and came
finally (o that helpless condition that I
could not crawl as far as the back garden.
As it was, I utterly despaired of getting
better. I became very frightened, the least
noise upsetting me. I even dreaded, from
sheer nervousness, to go into the next
room.”
“ Did you not see a doctor or try remedies
of any kind ? ” enquired the reporter.
“ No ; Bundanoon, as perhaps you know,
is nine miles from Moss Vale, and the
charge made by a doctor coming that
distance in the country is quite beyond the
means of all but rich people. For my
indigestion I tried a number of the remedies
so much advertised as cures for that com-
plaint, but they did me no good whatever.
And In the same way I tried the local
remedies for the neuralgic pains in my face
and forehead without benefit, for I did
not understand then, as I do now, that my
neuralgia was only one of the symptoms of
a deeply seated malady and impoverished
blood.” • ■ t
“ But you found relief at last, did you
not, Mrs. Geddes ? ” . -t
“Yes; when I had got fairly tired of
trying one thing and then another, it
happened that we were talking to a friend
of ours at Bundanoon, and who, having
some medical knowledge and experience,
was much consulted in the district. He
advised me very strongly to try Clements
Tonic; and it was a happy day for me
when I did so. When I had bought a
bottle at the store, and commenced to take
it according to the directions, I found a
marvellous change for the better after the
first few doses ; and as I continued to take
Clements Tonic, all my troubles and pains
grew lighter and more easy, till mV good
health returned. Before I had finished
taking the first bottle I could eat freely
without vomiting or retching my food up.
The neuralgic pain, the heart trouble, the
impaired vision, the pain between the
shoulders, and the wretched, despondent
feeling, together with my nervous weakness,
all yielded and faded away before the
recuperative influence of Clements Tonic ;
and when I found what the remedy was
doing for me, I stuck to it, and persevered
with it, and long after I had recovered, and
felt quite well, I continued to take Clements
Tonic, to make my recovery doubly sure,
for, being well again, nothing was so dread-
ful to me as the thought of a relapse. So I
made sure to clinch the cure.”
“ And you found your cure genuine, f*i,
Geddes, and not a mere flash fn the pan -
enquired the reporter. • ,
“Yes ; and after that I had the best ot
health for several years, so I think my cure
was as genuine as a cure could be ; and you
may be sure I have recommended Clements
Tonic whenever I had the opportunity, and
this often for, as you see, my
husband and I are in business.”
“Are you willing that this testimony
should be made public ? ”
“ Yes ; I feel that it is only right for me
to do so, for it may be the means of telling
some sufferer, with symptoms like my own,
that a sure cure is waiting in Clement*
Tonic.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Maggie Geddes, of Cook-road. MarriCkville, in
the Colony of New South Wales, do solemnly and
sincerely deolare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of four folios and con
secutively numbered from one to four, and that 1
contains and is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my lull
permission to publish the same in any way ; and 1
make this solemn declaration voluntarily, and without
receiving any payment, conscientiously believing
the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
an Act made and passed in the ninth year of the
reign of her present Majesty, intituled An Act 10.
the more effectual abolition of Oaths and Affirmations
taken and made in the various Departments of the
Government of New South Wales, and to substitute
Declarations in lieu thereof, and for the suppression
of voluntary and-extra judicial Oaths and Affidavits.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Daniel O’Connor’s win in Phillip Division
followed five successive defeats in various elector-
ates of N.S.W. Spout, spout, spout again !
Again : An Australian has practically no
appeal to Caesar over the water where his life is
concerned. And if he cannot appeal to Caesar
against a death-sentence, why should he be allowed
to appeal where mere money is involved ?
Hawkesbury electors are excited about
‘the strong man” from N.S.W. Leg. Council
who threatens to demand their votes at next elec-
tion. A “strong man” wouldn’t think of the
Hawkesbury, with Federation and an attenuated
local Parliament imminent—unless he were lazy.
• S.A. Attorney-General Gordon claims an
Australian record in having represented an elec-
tive district for 12 years without once being called
upon to take the platform in defence of his seat.
In the interim Gordon has held office in four
Ministries, and was four times leader of S.A.
Leg. Council.
A perusal of British news paper-files shows
that the cable-news sent to Australia from the
London agency is mostly a copy of the newspaper-
headlines, which do not often represent the con-
text, but more frequently represent the vivid
imagination of the sub-editor. As witness the
utter divergence between local newspaper head-
lines and the facts narrated in the body of the
news.
Holder Govt.’s policy for coming session
was announced by Attorney-General Gordon last
week. Its main items are : —Household Suffrage,
Companies Law Amendment, Factories Reform,
Workmen’s Compensation, Early Closing, and
Land Values Assessment. This comprises most
of the reforms Kingston ineffectually tried to get
through the Tory Council for the last three years
of his term.
Some have thought that Sargood, the one-
eyed man who is king among the blind of Vic.
Leg. Council, has his eye upon Gippsland district
to make him Senator Sargood, but it is not so.
He will stand for the constituency which will in-
clude his present Leg. Council province, South
Yarra. But his finicking, small detail, comma-
and-semi-colon kind of ability will be lost in an
able Senate.
A batch ot eight Labor Js.P., all thorough-
going party men, and admitted nominees, were
sworn in the other day at
Broken Hill ; and it fell tr
Judge Gibson to do the
job—he of all the judges
rightly or wrongly credited
with the most utter de-
testation of Laborite poli-
tics. This is the third
batch of Js.P. practically
nominated by the Broken
Hill Trades Hall—a strik-
mg illustration of the
power of combination ; for
the Trades Hall there does
not directly represent more
than one-eighth of the male
adults of the district. As
to the members of the first two
lots, there has only once been a
serious allegation of bench-
packing against any of them, in
spite of gloomy forebodings when
the first was gazetted.
Of the eight members who
retire from S.A. Council this month
only one (W. A. Robinson) is a
Laborist, but three others—Bice, Gor-
don, and Russell—belong to the
Liberal party; the other four—Basedow.
Fuller, Stirling, and W ard—are
hard-shell Conservatives. Ful-
ler and Ward, it is hoped,
are marked for destruction,
but the Tories are spending
so much money on their men
that the other side looks for-
ward with fear to the elee-
tiors, which take place May 19.
Kingston's battle is being fought in his absence
by members of the Holder Ministry and the
Labor-party. His battle flag is Household Suffrage
for the Council as a step from the present £25 an-
nual rental franchise towards ultimate adult
suffrage, but, of course, the election has to be
fought on the present exclusive suffrage.
This should be a good time for reconsider-
ing the Sydney City Railway idea. A line along
the west side of George-street to Dawes Point,
with a view to an ultimate extension to North
Sydney, would involve the tearing-down of a good
part of the infected area. Surely the investigations
of the last few weeks have showji that no part of
Sydney could be better spared !
London Daily Mail (circulation a million
or so) re Windsor’s goodbye to the Queen, on the
eve of her visit to Ireland :
The day was gray and sombre, the sun which is wont to
shme upon Her Majesty was hid by the heavy clouds, but
there was a sense of dignified forbearance in the very
atmosphere. One felt that but for the presence of the
Queen the heavens had rained.
Steward ! Quick !!
The abolition of Bellamy’s (the official
refreshment-room) is to be once again attempted
when M.L. Parliament next meets. The osten-
sible reason urged for closing Bellamy’s is that it
doesn t pay, but were the House Committee to
order the abolition of the “ slate ” it would pay
well enough. Many members and ex-members
owe fine healthy scores, and carry about a strong
whisky aroma that isn’t paid for.
The delicate question of re-modelling
S. A. Parliament and reducing the number of mem-
bers was referred to a Parliamentary commission
last year, but the Holder Govt., when it in-
vited politicians to become members, met with re-
fusal m nearly every instance, and it is now pro-
posed to refer the problem to a popularly-elected
convention. The S.A. politicians who preach re-
trenchment in the public service are only human
when it comes to dispensing with their own ser-
vices.
From a Sergeant-Major of Rhodesia
Police :
Well, after the war is over, and the British flag is flying
in Pretoria, all the mechanics from Australia will have to
look for fresh fields ; there will be no more £3O and £4O a
month—it will ruin the country for ever.
1 hat’s it: Australia is fighting in the interests of
cheap labor. The very pick of her bone-and-
muscle is hiring itself out to do the sort of work
done by the fashionable N.S.W. amateur mounted
police of the big-strike time.
Already British Govt.’s offer to pay the
“ Imperial Bushmen ” ss. per day has aroused
British protest. When the £35,000,000 war-loan
came from committee in the Commons, on March
8, Mr. Healy gave notice of his intention to move :
That the principle of the loan be so extended as to en-
able the colonies to participate in the responsibility, so
that all the self-governing colonies who were so keen to
send troops to the war should bear the burden of paying
for them, and so that the Bushmen should not receive 6s.
a day while Englishmen, Scotchmen, and Irishmen were
paid only Is. 3d. a day.
Ashburton (W.A.), Seppy Burt’s old con-
stituency, for which Oom John Forrest’s little
brother David is a candidate, is the emptiest
electorate in Australia. There are seven resident
electors ; the 35 absentees on the roll mostly live
hundreds of miles away. It is practically im-
possible to get up a contested election in Ashburton
for want of enough inhabitants to nominate the
second candidate, and if there was an election
there would probably be only one vote altogether
—the returning officer’s. It is alleged that once
when Burt visited his electorate in the course of
an exciting and bitterly-contested walk-over there
wasn’t one elector in the constituency—the whole
seven had gone away on business or cow-shaking
or something. Ashburton has never had an elec-
tion. Neither have eight other W.A. constitu-
encies—-Bunbury, De Grey, Gascoyne, Irwin,
East Kimberley, Nelson, Roebourne, and Wei
lington!
SAME TRADES-DIFFERENT JARGON.
Volunteer Captain (the leading local butcher,
after receiving news of Cronje’s surrender ):
“ Sq —’ t’tion, shoulder—hie — mutton; ’t’tion,
:pork arms; ’t’tion—what yet — hie—laughing
at? Sq—right — hie—tripe, ’smish!”
The 27th has been a momentous day in
the history of the Transvaal Boers. On Feb. 27th
they won the battle of Majuba Hill. On the
same date three years later Great Britain signed
the second Convention which omitted all mention
of the word suzerainty, to the great joy of the
Boers. On 27th Feb., 1900, the battered remnant
of Cronje’s army came out of its burrows and
surrendered to Lord Roberts, and on 27th March
Joubert died.

NEW BOOK-KEEPER : “ Fancy! Well, really the world is getting
smaller every year, sir, isn’t it?”
Old, forgotten, back-number Strangways,
ex-Premier of S.A., rises up in England to urge
that Chamberlain will retain the Privy Council
appeal, and that he will “safeguard the State
creditor ” before introducing the Commonwealth
Bill. Now, how is Birmingham Joseph to “safe-
guard the State creditor?” All that can be said
is that he will be paid as heretofore if this country
has the money. Does Henry Bull Templer
Strangways want Barton deposited in the Tower
of London as a hostage for due payment of the
half-year’s interest ?
Ex-Premier Reid’s Advisory Unemployed
Board was swept away, and Premier Lyne’s Un-
employed Board now takes its place. The chief
difference, so far, between the Boards appears to
be that Reid appointed his Board nominally with-
out pay and afterwards surreptitiously put a sum
on the Estimates to pay the members, whereas
Lyne starts out by saying he will pay his Board.
Each Premier took care to have hangers-on of his
own on his Board, and neither sought Parlia-
ment’s approval either of the personnel of the
Board or of the emoluments of office.
Sydney Truth, in a leading article of
April 22, says :
But the existence of the Privy Council as a final appeal
court in Australasian matters is quite another affair. It
represents a real overlordship; and, to be frank, it is an
overlordship which, in the interests of equity and natural
justice, we should be sorry to see abolished.
John Norton’s first participation in active Federal
matters was at the Bathurst Convention, and his
qualification for a seat among the delegates there
was a document labelling him as the accredited
representative of N.S.W. Republicans.
Sydney Telegraph on the evils of Pro-
tection :
All our leading industries have something to lose, hot
gain, by protection. But Mr. Rose says, “ Each new in-
dustry enriches the country with another wealth-pro-
ducing asset.” That might be true, if it did not with-
draw labor from pursuits which were more profitable to
he community as a whole.
It is a very beautiful theory—but where are these
industries from which labor was “withdrawn?”
What is the name of that occupation which col-
lapsed or was crippled for want of labor because
Protection drew the labor away to new occupa-
tions ?
What treason, sedition, and sacrilege is
this ! Here is J. H. Symon, Q.C., the S.A. Fede-
ral Conventioner, _ protesting against the deifica-
tion of Chamberlain. “Why,” this old fellow
asks: “Why are we to assume that Mr. Cham-
berlain will not give way, and that Australia must ?
Why is he to be elevated into a modern Alex-
ander, who
Assumes the nod
Affects the God
And seems to shake the spheres ? ”
Nothing but disrobing and disbarring should meet
that case.
With a view to next Assem-
bly election the reform party on
the Westralian Goldfields is en-
thusiastically putting the names
of new voters on the roll, and the
Oom John party is enthusiasti-
cally shunting them off. The
reform party isn’t always too
particular about the qualification
of its voters if it can only get
them on, and the Oom John
party is ready and willing to
strike men off as “ dead ” though
they rise up personally to swear
to their own existence. A lot
of voters get off again so sud-
denly after they arrive that they
can hardly be said to get on the
roll at all. It would be more ac-
curate to say they jumped across
it.
“Arist”: Despite the pre-
sent Imperial hysteria the prac-
tical movement for an Empire
Federation is not even obscure
—it is not visible at all. The
Victorian Imperial Federation
League has an actual member-
ship of about half the number
of letters to its title, although
leaving the matter of subscrip-
tions entirely to the discretion
and memory of its members. At
the annual meeting the other day
there was an attendance of 16,
including press and public, des-
pite the well-advertised fact that
justices Holroyd and Hod gas
were to speak. Writer has seen
few funeral services that were
more inanimate and felt as he
stepped into the street that he
had left a corpse behind.
The Argus is in a rather
tight place over the Federal
tariff. It is against Protection
—in fact, it is against customs
duties altogether because they
hamper its importing friends.
It is still more against the extra
direct taxation that must come
if the duties are abolished. In
Victoria it could advocate the
abolition of the duties, and
thereby please the importers,
without saying anything about
the alternative direct taxation—
because there was no chance of
the duties being abolished. But
it is afraid to advocate absolute Freetrade in the
Commonwealth lest it should get it. So the Argus
proposes a “ moderately protective” Federal tariff.
N.S.W. Works Minister O’Sullivan sug-
gests that a few prominent Irish-Australians
should be commissioned to England to explain to
the British the sort of Constitutions Australians
possess, and to claim the same sort of Home Rule
for Ireland. First, there already are Irish-Aus-
tralians in the House of Commons and elsewhere
in England. Second, the Home Rule Irishmen
claim is not identical with any Australian Consti-
tution. _ Third, if such a commission were called
into being it would be rushed for picnic and self-
advertisement purposes by the Dan O’Connors
and O’Sullivans.
It is cabled that ex-Premier Kingston, of
S.A., says, in London, that “Australian Federa-
tion will help the grander Federation of all Aus-
tralia with the Motherland.” They all begin to
talk like this in London, worse luck. When the
titles are handed round on a soup-plate as usual,
the Federal delegates will presumably fall, also as
usual. The fall of the Australian politician in
London is like the story of a Woman with a Past.
It is always the same kind of Past and the same
kind of Fall. It has been the same Fall ever since
Genesis, when the serpent offered Adam a
K.C.M.G.-ship over the fence.
Be the awful risks that some of the anti-
Billites saw in Federation. S.A. Premier Holder,
when Treasurer in Kingston’s Administration,
used to tell of two men on a Westralian goldfield
who, having only a shilling between them (drinks
Is. each), intended buying one drink and sharing
it. A stranger entering the bar, one of them
asked him if he would have a drink ; he said
he and his thirsty mate had just had theirs.
The stranger accepted, and, after disposing of
their bob’s-worth, asked if they would join him in
a drink. They did, and when they were alone
again the man who had asked the stranger said—
“ Wasn’t that a clever thought of mine ! ” “ Ay,
’twas,” said his Caledonian mate, “ but, losh!
mon—what an awfu ’ resk ! ”
In Westralia you are not supposed to be
on the municipal roll unless your rates are paid in
advance, and the electoral roll is made up from
the municipal roll. Therefore you can lose your
parliamentary vote for not having paid your
municipal rates in advance. It is fair enough but
for one thing—the gang in power has ways of
getting at the rate-books, and of preventing the
gang out of power getting at them. Old George
Shenton is going up for re-election, and a very
large number of electors were invited to sign his
nomination paper. Many refused, and every one
of them whose rates were in arrear was objected
to within 48 hours. The gang-in-power says it
was only a coincidence. Westralia has more coin-
cidence than all the other Australias put together.
