The Bulletin acknowledges receipt of the
“ Newspapeb Press Directory for 1900.” This is
the 55th. issue of Messrs. C. Mitchells useful
annual, which is a complete directory of the whole
of the newspapers on the planet. Not only does
it give the startling information that the Bunga-
bidda Prokasseeka is published and distributed
free daily in Calcutta, but also that the Diabio de
Cundinmarca and Soerabayaasch Handelsblad
circulate in Begota in S. America and Sourabaya
in Java respectively, but it gives a terse account
of every town and hamlet in the empire that issues
a sheet and describes its surroundings and re-
sources. The publishers receive _ advts. for all
foreign papers, and to the advertiser the story-
writer, the artist, and all the great army of people
who do business with those who deal in printer s
ink the directory is worth a vast amount more
than the modest price of two shillings. Every-
thing, including the list of weekly, monthly and
quarterly illustrated and un-illustrated magazines,
reviews and papers, is in alphabetical order, so the
searcher can put his finger on the right spot as
soon as he opens the volume.
The Khedive of Egypt is a famous hater —
he never forgets an enemy, and when he conceives
a sudden and passionate dislike for anybody and
cannot get at that party, he rolls around the floor
biting to fragments the amber-and-gold mouth-
piece of his pipe. Kitchener’s calm assurance was
the curse of Abbas’s life, and when the machine-
like warrior started for Omdurman his nominal
ruler prayed long and earnestly that Khalifa
Abdulla might get his head and place it in a
casket before the Mahdi’s tomb. When the man
in Cairo heard of the victory there was a terrible
wreck among the chibouks and nargilehs and other
oriental smoking machines. When he heard that
the victorious general was to be dubbed Lord
Kitchener of Khartoum, he tore the stuffing out of
the upholstery with his teeth. Hardly had he re-
covered before an incautious Armenian secretary
informed him that the Sirdar was going to drop
the Kitchener, and be known simply as Lord
Khartoum. Then the Khedive flew at the secre-
tary and bit him—so goes the story, anyhow.
It was all very well for Mr. Mat Steyn to
speak contemptuously of his brother, the Orange
Free State President, but the fact remains that a
large majority of Free Staters were (and are) pre-
pared to follow Kruger’s warlike lead. President
Steyn’s comparative weakness is a small personal
matter in a big uprising. Apparently, he lacks
the moral support of his wife, in which respect?
has good cause to envy old Paul Kruger. Mrs.
Steyn, at latest dates, was visiting some relatives
in Aberdeen.

Constance Clyde has some vague and pro-
miscuous ideas about dress:
It is probable that many women will be driven to Matri-
mony this season owing to the buttoned sleeves in which
the ‘fashionable are beginning to encase and, as it
were tight-lace, to sausage-like proportions their well-
nourished arms. The spinster will be known by the
elongation of her neck due to repeated attempts to see if
the hundred-and-third button-the one at the north-west
corner of the left shoulder —is all that it ought to be.
The grasping sex’s hatred of appearing empty-handed is
shown by the fact that since reticules went “out ” woman
has taken to holding her lowest back skirt-frill in her
hand, the dress being evidently so built that only a
reasonable amount of lace underskirt is shown ; but that
does not prevent the frill-holder from looking somehow
or other like the kitten that has caught the long-chased
tail at last. If her blue or green frock evolves into white
at the shoulders and neck-band, giving her an appearance
as if her outer robe had slipped its moorings and was dis-
playing the chemisette underneath, then she is really
chic ; though why a woman should be chic because she
looks as if her clothes are falling off passes my compre-
hension.

A weird story is reported in the papers
from Newcastle, N.S.W.’s city of coal-dust. A
lady walking home about 11 p.m. had her attention
drawn to a female in a back lane surrounded by
about 10 or 15 men. The lady’s husband arrived
shortly afterwards, and, accordingtothelocal paper,
took in the situation at a glance, and instead of
rushing for immediate aid proceeded to watch
events. He watched the toughs leave the group
round the woman in twos and threes, and as they
left, others, apparently on guard, took their places.
A i ter a while the gentleman’s blood boiled over
and rushing, down the lane he threatened the
offenders that, if they didn’t go away at once (and
thereby get out of the way of arrest and dodge the
law) and allow the unfortunate woman to depart
at once “he would send for the police. ” The gang
wanted to fight, but finding that this very strange
and lenient gent could use his fists it fled. When
the larrikins had departed with impunity into the
darkness, the woman tremblingly told how she
had come from Maitland with a female friend
how a strange man had taken her to a wine-shop
where her memory failed, and she only awoke to
find herself being cruelly and brutally outraged by
the gang in the lane. The policeman who came in
response to a telephone message promptly arrested
her and locked her up for being drunk and dis-
orderly, and next day she was fined 5/- and sent
home to the Chinaman with whom she lived.
And nothing more has been heard about the
alleged 15 or more ruffians who are said to have
committed the assault. It is a curious tale all
round.
The cheek of the British Government in claiming
to alter the Commonwealth Bill in comparatively
unimportant respects is the more marked when we
reflect on the immensely more important regards
in which it might be altered for our own sakes,
and would be altered if the operation could be
performed quickly and effectually. For instance,
it would appear that, in the haste of the last day
or two of the Convention, and in a laudable desire
for conciseness, the clause relating to taxation in
the section enumerating the powers of the Fede-
ral Parliament was finally framed in such a way
as to appear to give to the Federal Parliament the
rather startling power of passing a comprehensive
law prohibiting any particular kind of taxation,
e.g., a land-tax, from being imposed in the various
states. This has not hitherto been publicly
noticed, and, of course, was far from the intention
of the framers or the wish of the people. Yet,
when the people of Australia would not, even
for the sake of removing a blot like that, tolerate
any further delay in the passing of the Bill, Cham-
berlain, in spite of all his flapdoodle about the
relations of the colonies and England, must come
forward with a wrecking proposition on infinitely
more trivial blots, if they are blots at all, and in
the interests of a few practically alien bank-part-
ners in London.
At the recent Melb. send-off to Capt. Wollaston
and Lieutenant Mann, of the Vic. Bushmen’s Con-
tingent, Best, one of the Jonahs of the Turner
Govt., let fly a balloonful of extra-effervescent
flapdoodle about the Fag—the heir of all the ages,
the Pax Brutallica, the glowing loyalty of their
young guests, the wealth and crimson threaderie
of the dear old mother countree, &c. With a re-
freshing disregard for the fitness of the “things”
• PJ e ? ent > young Mann gave an interesting analysis
of the feelings that prompted him to volunteer.
He frankly repudiated Best’s right to speak for
his motives in the matter, said that it was mainly
the spirit of adventure that was sending him to
Troy or Syracuse, coupled with the desire to see
and take part in important events rare of occur-
rence, and after hinting that Capt. Wollaston’s
opinions were not unlike his own in the matter,
concluded by saying that he would never forget
that his word of command as an officer might re-
sult in sending to annihilation better men than
himself. Needless to state, Mann’s speech was
not reported in the full accounts of the shivoo
given in the daily press.
Going to the share-list of the African
Chartered Co., John Burns shows how many
highly-placed Englishmen were concerned to drag
Britain into the Transvaal war for the sake of
dividends. He finds that eighty “peers, com-
moners, Imperial and Consular officers, equerries,
and society bankers ” have held since 1892 180 000
shares, with a top price of £1,500,600 and lowest
of £050,000. Among the names he selects are the
following:—

Lord Roberts’s report on the Spion Kop
bungle is all in accordance with news wired to
London at the time by Bonnet Burleigh, Winston
Churchill and other correspondents. It is, in fact,
strong testimony to the smartness of the smartest
pressmen who watch the game in South Africa.
h rom first to last Sir Charles Warren was chiefly
■to blame. Given a certain plan of advance by the
commanding Buffer he preferred to go “on his
own ” in a great measure, and it was his departure
from the plan that necessitated the so-called
capture of Spion Kop. This proved disastrous
for the simple reason that he put far too many
men on the job. The top of the mountain was
crowded with unfortunates who could find no
cover. A third of the number might possibly
have held the position with comparatively little
loss of life, but as it was the Boers blazed away at
the mob of unprotected ones and killed about 250
of them outright. The slaughter didn’t mean that
the Kop was untenable. It meant that the
large majority of the dead and wounded had been
foolishly sacrificed. Buffer knew nothing of what
was happening. Warren took no measures to
maintain communication with Colonel Thorney-
croft, the man in command of the Kop holders
after General Woodgate had fallen. At nightfall
when Warren was trying to make up his mind as
, what *0 do next he sent a newspaper man
> Winston Churchill) instead of going himself, to
discover Thomeycroft’s “ views * on the situation
and Churchill found that Thomeycroft had already
made up his mind to get the troops away from the
place where they had been overwhelmed by their
own numbers, so to speak. Warren disobeyed hi 3
general s instructions. Thomeycroft was to blame
for what Roberts caffs a “wholly unauthorised
assumption of responsibility.” Buffer—who ap-
pears to be a wise but slow-thinking commander—
al *owed Warren to overrule him for a while.
When he came along and saw the
mess that Warren had made Buffer practically ad-
mitted his fault by stating that he would take
personal control of the next attempt to relieve
Ladysmith. And seeing that Buffer eventually
got right through, without losing as many men as
Warrens failure had cost, he might have been let
down more lightly in Roberts’s report, many
people will think. Nevertheless it is a hopeful
sign for the British cause that Roberts has begun
to make unpleasant remarks concerning important
people in South Africa, for there are some strange
stones told of how sundry colonels and majahs
have been treating the gory business in a pick-
nickian spirit.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
i A u ,^ umn m Qet, just over, shows a heavy
loss through the almost total absence of sporting men
from the country and the other provinces. The plague
scared them—it scared even Ike.v Mo of Victoria, who is
very much afraid of Death—and no wonder. Local odds-
vendors consequently did great business. All through
the sport reached a high standard. Clever handicapping
hid winners in a remarkable manner. Finishes, as a rule,
were close and exciting. Parthian topped the three-year-
° a ' va -' khat left no room for question but both he
and Mernwee could have been better. La Carabine was
the best of the older nags and appropriated the big
*v, ni \ 6S , e '[ en f 'host convincingly. Ex-galloway Bega was
the best hurdler of the lot. Malster and Haulette stood
out among the two-year-olds, with Hautesse, Kinglike
and Isa running them very close.
• • • •
Second Steeplechase at Randwick, Saturday,
sprung a surprise on punters generally. M.L. crack Nor’-
\Yest went out a 2 to 1 favorite, with his stable-chum,
Crusade, at 7s. S.A. nag Arctic fell, breaking both
shoulders and was destroyed. Nor’-West had 12 12 up,
and looked fit to carry a lot more, but came to his knees
in second round and parted with his rider. Orusado, at
this time, was far behind, but he subsequently moved up
and got to the front 200yds. from home. Winner’s fencing
and pace, in view of his display on Easter Monday, proved
an eye-opener. F
H.S.”: The recent A.J.C. meeting seems
clearly to have demonstrated that Parthian was Merri-
wee s superior a.t all distances. But to the writer it was
apparent that the Derby and Cup winner was stale, and
void of all dash. The ideal of training is to have done
sufficient work and yet have the recuperative powers
unimpaired—a reserve of natural vigor to draw upon.
But Mernwee was obviously overdone, probably not
having recovered from the severe preparation he under-
went m the spring. The writer, however, expects the
horse soon to regain his old brilliancy if he is allowed a
proper rest. Merriwee’s defeat in the 3 miles was owing
doubtless to there being nothing in the race to make
pace enough to extend the brilliant La Carabine, so that
she was able to come at the finish with her meteoric rush
and score a win before Merriwee could be got all out
Anyhow La Carabine was, prior to that win, by far the
best handicap mare in Australia. A kind goer, honest, a
great stayer, and the most brilliant finisher in the land.
Of the two-year-olds, Malster, Haulette, and Kinglike
stand out prominently; the one of the three most likely
to improve with age being the strapping son of Wallace—
Kinglike, a horse singularly reminiscent of the prints of
the famous Eclipse—his head, neck, and body being
carried, as it were, all in a horizontal line h
* • • •
Striking instances of reversals of form were
urmshed at recent A.J.CJ. Autumn meeting by Hautesse
wi A on Mosfc - vn - o Th , e former, who ran a bad second
last in Champagne Stakes on second day, simply spread-
eagled almost the same field in Easter Stakes on third
£?ir A v. ,ICe - Mostyn who was served up specially warm
for Highweight Handicap on St. Legerday, ran absolutely
nowhere ; but, on concluding day of the meeting, created
much surprise by her foremost position in Rous Handicap,
suffering defeat only by a head. Surely the slight
difference in the weights could not be wholly responsible
for this in-and-out running. Cyanide’s double win, too
was unlooked for, owing to her recent inglorious display
at Hawkesbury. It is, therefore, a matter of no small
wonder, as per usual, that Ikey Mo came out on top.
It looks now as though La Carabine might have
won last Caulfield Cup but for being treated as the
stable s second string. W. R. Wilson made the same
nustake in 96 when Resolute started for the big Caulfield
at 7to 1, and Merman went out at a long price The
fancied one got a fine show to win, but he failed miser-
ably at the finish, whereas Merman, whose jockey had
been looking after Resolute all the time, beat his stable
companion handsomely. For last year’s Caulfield Cup
the impostor, Scorn, was backed down to 5 to 1 whilst La
Carabine stood at three times the odds. Burn rode
Scorn and took him to the front what time La Carabine
was shut in among the middle division. Scorn proved to
be a coward when asked to struggle, biit La Oarahine
came along with a late rush and finished fourth, as Mer-
man had done three years previously.

Plague scare materially affected Autumn sale of
yearlings at Randwick last week. The absence of
prominent Vic. buyers was much felt. 79 lots were put
up and 77 sold, at first sale, for 5297 gs.—an average of

The famous Chipping-Norton (Liverpool, N.S.
Wales) stud has been scattered. Best of the lot were
brought under the hammer in Sydney on Friday. There
was a big crowd, but few bidders. Nineteen brood mares
4 fillies, and 3 stallions were submitted, and realised
5555g5. Mares fetched 4360g5. of this-an average of
229£gs Stallions brought 720g5., and the fillies 4?sgs
Highest figure (650g5.) was given for Trenton mare
Archeress, dam of Parthian, and in foal to Grand Flaneur
Mr. Sam Hordern was the buyer. Dovecote (dam of
Lotos), with colt by Grand Flaneur and again served by
that nag, went for 600 gs.—the—the bargain of the week Her
yearling colt, also by Grand Flaneur, realised 450 gs 0 n
previous Tuesday. Another Trenton mare, Gloria was
sold for 450 gs. Queen Agnes (dam of Ace of Diamonds)
by Grand Flaneur by Dame Agnes (imp.) by Hermit went
for 310 gs. Patron’s sister—Patrona—and a filly foal by
Seneschal cost 275g5. Eight Trenton mareswere tliusparted
withfor276sgs. 15gs. secured old Terara. Stallion Seneschal
foaled in 1892, brought 350 gs.— one of the cheap things of
the sale; and Gentle Hermit (by Bill of Portland)and
Be uilieu (by Grand Flaneur) were knocked down to an
Indian buyer.
* * * *
Grand Flaneur died at Chipping Norton
N.S.W., stud farm, Monday week of rheumatism. No
more successful nag has ever been known in Australia
Grand Flaneur was never thoroughly extended, hence it
is impossible to say how good he really was. Sprint or
distance, or weight within reason, made no difference
to him. His wins included A.J.O. and V.R.O. Derbies
St. Leger, Melbourne Cup, Normanby Stakes and Cham-
pion Stakes. Stakes gathered in by him totalled £7930
He has sired winners of most big events, among them
two Melb. Cups, Sydney Cup, V.R.O. Oaks, Ascot Vale
Stakes, Epsom Handicap, V.R.O. Sires’ Produce Stakes
A.J.O. Derby, Caulfield Guineas and Cup and V.R.C St’
Leger. Bravo, Oobham, ’Frisco, Dainty, Ohesham, Dare
Devil, Ruenalf, Straightfire, Patron, Patroness
Parthian, and many others owe their existence to
him. Grand Flaneur was bred by the late Mr. E. K. Oox
at Fernhill, 22 years ago, and was bought cheap as a
yearling by Mr. Long. He was by the mighty Vattendon
from First Lady (imp.) by St. Albans from Lady
Patroness by Touchstone’s son Orlando.

* * * *
Silver-hue, driven by Dr. Slate, showed a fine
piece of trotting in the two-mile trot at Forest Lodge,
Sydney, last Monday. Starting 30sec. behind, the mare,
moving in beautiful style, fairly mowed down the field,
and ere a mile had been covered took command. Silver-
hue paced her two miles at a 2.331 gait, although the
doctor eased her down for the last mile. Huon’s daughter
seems to be about the best mare now trotting on Sydney
tracks.

* * * *
A queer piece of judging at Sydney Agricul-
tural Show was the awarding of the trotting-in-saddle
blue ribbon to the Moree horse Chieftain. Chieftain
broke, skipped, and all but went round on three legs for
the last part of the journey, and was distanced by Judo-e
Huon, who trotted in perfect style, but, to the surprise of
the onlookers, Chieftain got the prize all the same For
the prize given for trotters which had not won at the
meeting Prince Harold was the only horse entered to
oppose Chieftain, other owners, no doubt, standing out
owing to the erratic judging in the saddle event As
might have been expected champion Prince Harold made
a " exhibition of the Moree horse, which was called off
after the first round.

Otto Cnbb of M.L. is the biggest pug draw-card
south of the equator Metrop. A.O. (Sydney) Friday last
he i n Very ', na ",?L 1500 afc 10s -’ 5s ’’ an( * 2s - 6d- to see him
and Bananaland “Snowy” Sturgeon fight. Excitement was
at fever-pitch from end to end of the go, which was the
toughest and bitterest fought in Sydney for years. It was
&"«?• b : Vr h a “ , the wa y- Cribb’s weight was given as
10.2 and Sturgeon’s at J-lb more. Cribb had Snowy on
the boards twice in the initial 3min. for 9 and 7sec. as the
result of a savage rush and a right cross. Betting opened
at 5 to 4 on Cribb, but 2 to 1 was offered while they rested
Hurricane work characterised every round. Several times
Cribb sagged at the knees, rocked in his tracks and looked
on the verge of collapsing, but an iron jaw kept him up.
oturg’eon sent in left and like piston-rods with pounds
of powder behind them, but still Cribb stood up Thrice
he went down before irresistible mishaps, but was on his
feet instanter. Then it was Cribb’s say, and the Queens-
lander got a parlous time until he in turn flew signals of
distress and appeared a “ moral ” for the Maorllander.
Such unflinching work has rarely been seen in a boxing-
ring They would now and again stand toe to toe and
punch as if their very lives depended upon the speed
number, and weight of the blows delivered. Cribb caught
a very wicked one on the mouth once and his head went
back as if knocked off his shoulders. Neither man’s face
would have taken the prize at a beauty-show after it was
all over ; Sturgeon s was in much the worse plight. The
end came in the eighth round when, during a wild rally
Cribb stood off and swinging the left promiscuously (as it
appeared) reached Sturgeon’s chin heavily, strildngit with
the side of the hand and dropping him like a rock The
failing man s head struck the boards very hard thus
assisting the effect of the knock-out StJrgeon
lay there many seconds after the referee declared Cribb
the winner. Up to the end it was either man’s light.
Cribb was lucky to get that punch home, particularly as
almost at the same instant Sturgeon landed a paralVser
over the heart which brought up the whites of the Maori-
lander s eyes and caused him to reel across the ring

“ Old Timer ” : Saw the Cribb-Sturgeon fight
Friday night last, and marvelled at the capacity each
evidenced for taking and giving. Sturgeon is the best-
built and quickest man we’ve had in Sydney for many a
day Two pluckier fighters never doffed a shirt. Each
had bad times in turn, and takers of 2 to 1 on Cribb could
have laid-off at evens. Had Oribb’s knock-out swing
been the smallest fraction of a second later, Sturgeon’s
punch over the heart would have left him (Cribb) at the
mercy of the Bananalander ; in fact, for a moment or two
it looked as if both were going out. It was as much as
Cribb could do to stay on his feet; nothing but the ropes
kept him up while the enemy was being counted out. If
the same two met again Sturgeon’s chance would be good-
he hits very clean and fights more cleverly than Cribb’
whose extra strength proved too much. Had Sturgeon
used his right less frequently in the earlier stages he
might have done better. Cribb’s corner thought it was
all over on three different occasions, but a gainer man
never stood than Otto. He had £5O to £2O on himself
before operations began. Said that Sturgeon didn’t vet a
fair chance in training through lack of funds ; butt all
the same, his party tipped him as a cert., and Charlie
Dunn, who knows what’s what, planked the “pony” side-
wager down. Herb. M’Kell challenged the winner before
the fight began. Cribb must be able to foot a fair
cheque now.

Ebon-hued Ike Stewart and half-caste Cape
Malay Charlie Weiner had their third tussle at Golden
Gate A.O. (Sydney) Wednesday night last week and drew
a big house. Ike won on both previous occasions, but
only after a bitter struggle ; this time he won again in
four hot rounds. Coal-skinned Stewart never fought
better. Fact that Peter Jackson looked on was perhaps
responsible. * 1

Latest colored comer m the pug. game (Joe
Thomal) went under at Sydney Golden Gate club on Sat-
urday night to Melbourne “Young” Nathan, who
brought the darkey down in the 18th round. Up to the
10th things were extremely lively, and Thomal seemed a
sure winner, as Nathan’s condition was none too good •
but the black’s heart failed him a bit—and ultimately he
went out to a solid thump on the point.

Editor Bulletin. I have only just been shown
a par in Bulletin, 27/l/’OO, in which “Crank” says—
Herb. McKell is not taking any chances at the ‘ willing ’
game, if there’s a possible get out. ... We saw that here
when challenges from Ned Burden and others were
ignored.” As this is only one of numerous similar
insinuations which somebody has been making against me
in your columns, I hope you’ll let me say a word in reply
First, as to the “willing” business, what about my
numerous severe battles, including my 15-round defeat of
Bill Jenning—described at the time as one of the goriest
fights ever seen ? Then, as to the Burden affair, I enclose
you a cutting from Sydney Referee, 8/8/’9B, by which you
will see that, though I declined to let the Golden Gate
people make use of me for a £2O purse, I put up £25 with
the Referee for a match with Burden ; and that mv
backer further offered, if necessary, to put up a purse
sufficient to bring us together. But the other side made
no more sign. These are hard facts, all recorded in the
sporting press ; and, as my record shows me to be a
fighter all out, when required, I think it is hard to have
insinuations to the contrary levelled at me merely
because, as a man of business, I decline to go for what will
not pay me if I win.— Herb. McKell.

Once a year Providence expresses a dis-
taste for militarism in Yic. by dropping heavy
rain upon the Easter Encampment. No matter
when Easter may fall, the rain always comes down
at the same time, and this year, owing to the extra
warlike arrangements, the Bushmen’s camp at
Langwarrin was positively flooded out, and the
muddy, disconsolate Contingent had to trek to
the Agricultural Show Ground at Elemington on
Easter Monday.
As an illuminant, Acetylene gas has no equal.
Carriage and buggy lamps are being sent in to Messrs.
Brandt Bros., lamp manufacturers, of Sydney and Mel-
bourne, for alteration, to show this powerful light, in
shoals. The firm obtained four first prizes at the Royal
Show, and represent the Imperial Acetylene Gas Coy’s
patents, of London, for house lighting. Primus Kerosene
Bath Heaters, which instantly heat a flow of water up to
110 degrees Fahr., are working on their premises.*
One who is well acquainted with the
northern Transvaal Boers suggests that General
Roberts will be presently in such a tight place that
Buller may have to go to his relief. Apart from
the capture of Cronje’s crowd, achieved with a
loss of many hundreds of men and 10,000 horses,
Roberts’ “successes” have been more apparent
than real. Anyway, his cocksure condemnation
of Buller, received by the British War Office a
month before it was published, looks ridiculous in '
the fight of the fact that two big disasters hap-
pened to his (Roberts’) army soon after he had
posted his dispatch, the first disaster being a loss
of 7 guns and three or four hundred men within a
few miles of Bloemfontein, his headquarters. A
report from Buller on the recent mistakes of the
Commander-in-Chief would be interesting.
An anti-war Canadian quotes from a letter
written by a Captain of the first Canadian Trans-
vaal contingent, and rightly says that its shameless
brutality should make honest Canadians blush.
This is the extract —
We had a pleasant little fight on New Year’s day about
36 miles from here, which distance we covered between
2 p.m. on Sunday, Dec. 31st, and Jan. Ist, at 11 a.m., and
though we killed 14 Boers (of which one went to my
credit, through the neck) and wounded as many more,
my little force of 100 men lost none. Some got away,
others fell with pleasing little holes in them, and when
“ Fix bayonets ! ” brought out seven or eight white flags,
the Boers who remained, 42 in all, threw down their arms
and quit like dogs. I would have let the beggars have the
bayonet at that, only Col. Richards, of the Queenslanders,
stopped us.
“ Honor,” says the writer, “to Col. Richards, of
the Queenslanders, who arrested the Canadian
Captain and his men in their bloody work, mis-
taken though he may be in the cause in which he
is engaged. Colonel Richards at least had not the
head of a fool and the heart of a murderer.”
The rising star of English money-lenders
has been snuffed out at the early age of “ dirty -
five.” Isaac Gordon is dead. Gordon —bom
Bradski, or some such name—was an impecunious
Russian Jew when he came to England a dozen
srears5 rears ago. He got a billet as clerk to a money -
ender m a Midland county, and finding the busi-
ness ridiculously easy for any man with a hook
nose and a hard heart, “Gordon” started on his
own account. Of late years he traded under
various aliases in all the large towns, but his head-
quarters were at Birmingham, where he suddenly
expired last month. Many of Gordon’s grandest
transactions came to light in the London Bank-
ruptcy Court, notably the case of a long-suffering
person who produced the usurer’s receipts for
£14,000 odd, paid on account of an original loan
of £5OOO. There was a small but growing balance
still outstanding when the victim filed his schedule.
Isaac Gordon appeared as a principal witness
before the committee appointed a while ago by
the British Parliament to enquire into the
Shylockian system, and his bright, candid manner
of owning up to the ruin he had wrought stamped
him as one who might have lived to be truly great.
Arthur Griffith (Fellow Aust. Instit
Patent Agents, and Member Patent Law Assoc.,
Washington, IJ.S.A.), 169 Phillip-street, Sydney.
Patents secured and Trade Marks registered
throughout the world. Tel. 2898.*
Brisbane is having a special anti-bubonic
cleaning-up, and the usual telegram states :
The amount of rubbish, old clothing, bedding, &c., dis-

came as a revelation to the authorities.

seem to know.

It is estimated by a House of Commons
Committee that to pension the deserving poor
aged 65 and upwards would cost, in the United
Kingdom, about £12,000,000 a year. This is the
deserving poor only. Truly there must be an
appalling amount of poverty in Britain, for on
this basis about one person in every 40 must be a
deserving pauper over 65 years. Britain spends
about £24,000,000 a year in poor-rates.
“ Oxygen and its Administration ; A few
facts for Physicians ” (received from Messrs.
Felton, Grim wade and Co., of Melbourne) is a
neatly illustrated little pamphlet dealing with the
subject of oxygen gas treatment of many pulmon-
ary affections —pneumonia, asthma, &c. Also it
has something to say about diseases of the blood
and circulation, and emergency cases, such as
asphyxia, morphia poisoning, &c. Messrs. Felton,
Grimwade and Co. supply the booklet free upon
application, and it should appeal to the feelings of
all medical men, not to mention the more advanced
section of their diseased patients.* *

“ON OUR SELECTION.”
“By Steele Rude.”


“ A.G.”: Went once to a new-steamer
shivoo, and after the _ usual refreshments we
adjourned to the engine-room to inspect. Here
one man indulged his curiosity to such an extent
that soon he was dancing in agony at one end of
the room, while at the other his most useful finger
was lying on the floor. In endeavoring to explain
how it happened, another of the party excitedly
grabbed tne captain by the sleeve: “Look,” he
said, “he was standing here like this, and he
poked his finger in like this” . Then there
was a shriek and the excitable one bolted up the
ladder, leaving his mashed finger waltzing round
amongst the cogs.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on pave
27 of this issue.*
W. O. Hodgkinson is to edit the new
Queensland official mining journal; and he
will really be an editor who knows all about
it. The veteran has had hard luck and
long illness, but The Bulletin is glad to know
he’s still going strong. No Australian of his age
(52), or of any age, has a fuller life behind him.
Coming here as a middy in 1851, he was in charge
of Tarnagulla (Vic.) goldfield a year later; was
sub-editor of Melb. Are in 1859, with the Burke
and Wills expedition in ’6O, second in command o.
M ‘Kiniey’s across-Australia expedition in ’62, and
led the Q. expedition to the Diamantina in ’75. In
his busy life he was alsoQ. rfoldfields-warden, M.P.,
Cabinet Minister, and a dozen other things; but,
personally, always “a white man.”

CHEAP AND FASHIONABLE TAILORING.
CG. HATTE, of King-street, Newtown (near Sydney,
• N.S.W.), has recently made great additions and im-
provements to his Tailoring Department, which is now one
of the features of Iris extensive business. He receives regu-
lar shipments, from his Home buyers, of all the newest and
most fashionable goods, and has one of the smartest cutters
in the colony. Everything is made to measure on the pre-
mises; a perfect fit is guaranteed, and patrons may rely on
every satisfaction being given. Self-measurement forms
and patterns sent free bypost. Hatte’s suits of Indigoserge,
all-wool tweeds, and sergerettes (with extra pair of. trousers
given in) for 42/- are the marvel of tailoring and the talk of
Australasia. For 50/- Hatte makes a suit of tweed or serge
fit for a prince. Inspect or send for patterns of his new
woollens, comprising vicunas, West of England, Scotch and
Blarney tweeds, at 57/6. and 65/- ; the great value offered.
Hatte makes a smart Chesterfield overcoat from meltons,
vicunas, and Venetians from 45/-, and the new paddock
coat, with full back, side vents, crescent pockets, silk-faced
and velvet collar for 55/-. Hatte’s speciality is in dress-
suits, starting at £3 3s, made of purple dye elastic coating
with silk-facing. For excellence of material, perfect fit,
latest fashion, and superior workmanship, Hatte is un-
equalled, whilst his charges are at least 30 per cent, under
those of ordinary city tailors.

Cope Bros, and Co., of Liverpool, donated
(through their Svdney agents, M. Rutty and Co.)
1600 tins of tobacco to the recently-departed Bush
Contingent. It was Cope’s, by the way, who
recently tackled a hasty order for 2 tons of Caven-
dish (to be in 3oz. cakes, packed in kegs of 351 b.
each) for the British troops in Africa, and had the
order executed and dispatched in 8 hours !
Wool, —The N.Z. Loan and Mercantile
Agency Co. Ltd. reports that, owing to the inci-
dence of the Easter holidays, business during the
past week has been at a standstill, and there is
absolutely nothing new to report. It has been
decided not to hold any public auctions this
month, as in the present unsatisfactory state of
the market it is thought inadvisable. The sales,
therefore, which were arranged for the 25th and
26th inst., will be deferred until 16th and 17th
May, and this co’s catalogue will be submitted on
the former date. The next London sales will
commence on Bth May.*

At Poverty Point.
Mre.Patrick Campbell’s Pat, whom the cables report
killed in a late Safrican engagement, owed his sergeant’s
stripes (and perhaps, therefore, his death) to the fact that
he had a society actress for a wife. When Wales in-
spected the Imperial Yeomanry just before it embarked
Mrs. Patrick’s husband had to be brought out for the
Prince’s closer observation-Kind, of course, that meant
promotion on the spot. Campbell, a fine, genial fellow
besides being Mrs. Pat’s husband, was a little of “ some-
thing in the city ; ” he was sec. of the Miners’ Dream Ex-
tension Co. until that Westralian wild cat went the wav
of most miners’ dreams, extended or otherwise.
“ Yowler”: Melbourne has in Miss Bertha Bird a vocal
gymnast who is likely to eclipse even Amy Castles as an
advertising medium. The claims of America that its Miss
Beach Yaw has the highest register on earth, and can sin™
2v full notes rising to F in altissimo, reminds people that
Melbourne s Bird can go to Aflat beyond the F in altissimo
a vocal feat that leaves the ground-and-lofty champion
of America practically out of it, and secures to Australia
another world’s championship. Bertha follows the other
song-birds to Europe at an early date.
At a mass meeting at Syracuse, U.S., on Feb. 25,
Emehe Melville, whom old-timers remember as the most
sparkling opera-bouffe artiste Australia has ever seen
made a speech of red-hot sympathy with the Boers, among
whom she Once lived—with Billgreeno Verdi.
Miss Dot Frederics, as the most vivacious and flighty of
damsels who happen to Jones in Arnold’s farce
at Melb. Princess, .has so much laughing to do in the
course of the comedy, or does it of her own volition, that
during the whole evening she never goes ten seconds
without a burst of hilarity. If there were good and
sufficient reason for half the laughing Miss Frederics
laughs, Jones’s adventures would he the funniest on
earth, but the glad laughter is often uncalled for, and as
an effort on the part of the actress to convince the
audience that the farce is funnier than it looks it is always
ft mistake.
‘‘What Happened to Jones” is excessively American,
and much of the patter wants editing down to the com-
prehension of a community that does not plav poker to
excess. The American musical burlesque creates the be-
lief that the average American person devotes his spare
time and the minutes he can snatch out of business to the
pursuit of the illusive cocktail, but the American farce
convinces you that the ordinary Yank’s whole energies
and aspirations are devoted to poker, and the Yank
language is based on poker expressions.
‘‘M.E.P.” : Miss O’Neil’s Oamille—or Camel, as they
called it on the stage—didn’t remind us of Bernhardt or
Potter, or of anyone we have seen. Miss O’Neil made the
part a study in tones. Her adieu to Armand, for instance,
i" ~c tv l lAlj.:~^dieu I A I j. : ~^ dieu (™ ezz °). Adieu (mezzo). Adieu (con-
tralto), Adieu (contralto), and then again, Adieu (soprano *
—aH called from the passage while Armand sat like a good'
little boy in his chair, and did what he was bid (one could
almost.magine him reading from his book “ A-b-ab e-b-
-eb. ) Camille would rise from her chair when she wanted
to say anything impressive, stand straight, chin sunk in
neck, and work those deep notes of hers. They are cer-
tainly fine, but they rather take her mind off her part so
intent is she on giving them play. It is certainly graphic,
and rather pathetic, when Oamille suddenly sits on the
floor and exclaims “Once a woman has fallen it is no
difficult to rise.” Papa Duval let the hint pass unheeded
and never offered to help her up. As she sat there, and
swayed from side to side emitting groans and cries of
My God, or something to that effect, in deep, pathetic
tones, it did look as if it were difficult to rise. Camille
cried and sobbed, and brushed her hair back, and paced
the room a good deal before she sat on the floor and said
that about the fallen woman, and Papa Duval listened and
looked nice. He flung his pretty cloak around him,
turned his classic profile to the audience, and, the arm
extended and gracefully curved, might have posed for a
statue of some leading light. In accents truly noble he
told Car (\-7nul of a let -tor he had received, and spoke to
her of credit-ers, and ended by giving her, coin-passion-
ate-Iy, a paternal kiss of silent blessing. But, not content
with robbing her of her Armand, on his way to the door,
on Saturday night, Pere Duval whipped up her shawl
with his hat and carried it away also. I was reminded of
poor Girard and his jerky style when Oamille told Armand
that she had given her word “to one! who has ! the
nght! to ask. Yes ! I love ! the Count! de Varville 1 ”
Kingston, in his frock-coat, rather shocked the modest
in the stalls, for he strongly suggested Louise Hepner as
the leader of the Purity Brigade. He wore no petticoats,
and his green-trousered legs had a rather comical effect
on one. Gustave looked at first as if he had dressed in a
hurry, to take his Nichette to see Camille, for he had the
appearance of having rushed out of his home in the midst
of shaving and forgotten to wash the lather off his jaws.
This couldn’t have been the fact, though, for he still wore
the lather in acts 4 and 5. The audience was much im-
pressed with all these things, and cheered a good deal
and clapped a good deal, and altogether was well pleased.
Especially did. it watch with breathless and wet-eyed
attention the sick woman as she tried to rise from her
sofa and walk across the room. To the callous there was
a suggestion in this of a very inebriated gentleman trying
to pick up his hat, but Saturday’s audience was inclined
to be maudlin and did not see it. It wept, and went
home feeling happy and good. Miss O’Neil’s “ Strange,” in
the last act, reminded me irresistibly of a very bored
society woman’s remark to the man who bored her.
Colonel Bell has been on the flute again in Sydney, in
mellifluous accents, advising his brother Anglo-Saxon re-
garding his duties and responsibilities. Colonel Bell is a
gentleman with a curious delivery; his voice goes into
retreat the first half of the sentence, and then swells
fortissimo with a suddenness which causes the audience
to look up and wonder how the other fellow got on to the
platform ; but it is the one and only Bell all the time
smiling ecstatically while a blob of reflected light circles
round his pale pink head. Bell’s humor is of the dry
American variety, hard to be understood at first, of a
people who like their fun of the rich moist beer-and-curse
order—as when the Colonel affirms that to do wrong is
sometimes better than to do right as thereby you learn
not to do wrong again, whereat his audience' inwardly
resolves to take many lessons of that sort. The lecturer’s
idea of the duties and responsibilities of the Anglo-Saxon
seem to be that as John has grabbed much of the earth
his duty is to grab the rest, so that the children of God
may rejoice. Bell does not say who are the children of
God —or if he does it is in one of his diminuendo moments
—but everybody understands that he is himself the
children in question—which is a comforting assurance and
good for the digestion.
Mrs. Maesmore Morris, cast for a two-line part in the
recent London revival of “ Prisoner of Zenda,” had the
luck to step into the shoes of her “ overstudy,” the lead-
ing lady, for a night or two. The theatrical papers seem
to have been chiefly struck by the fact that the new-
comer looked sweet and self-composed in the character of
Princess Flavia, for they said little or nothing about the
pretty little woman’s attempts at emotional acting, which
were probably not impressive. The Maesmore Morris
played Berenis in “ Sign of the Cross ” and other strong
parts before she left Melbourne, but her passion was mila
as milk. All the same, she ought to do very nicely as a
sentimental girl in light comedy, or a graceful minor
figure in classic drama. A part requiring warmth and
dash is out of her line.
There is a hideous taste about that cablegram of
hst Monday which stated that, according to
British military doctors, many of the Boer dead
prove to be women in male attire. The said cable
came just on the day that N.S.W.’s severalth
Contingent was going away to further reinforce
the 200,000 or 250,000 men, who are busy crush-
ing two little nations, which don’t muster more
than half these numbers—men, women and
children all counted. It wasn’t nice to think that
that latest contingent was going even to shoot
and bayonet men under these circumstances,
—the reinforcement which goes to help seven
men to down one isn’t a reinforcement
that one can be deliriously proud of.
But to think that a contingent from a’ country
which numbered among its citizens the men of the
Eureka Stockade is possibly going out with its
bayonets to disembowel women and girls is still
less - agreeable. Of course, if they are there—if
the wives and daughters have come in to fill up
the gaps in the scanty ranks of the republic for a
last effort, and they are dressed like men, the
chances are that no Contingenter, when the fury
of ripping up complete strangers for five shillings
a day, the glory of the empire, and the
dividends of Wernher, Eckstein, Rhodes & co.,
is °n him, will recognise their sex in time.
When seven Britons and one Australian have
climbed on the top, of one Dutchman with fatal
results it is possibly unpleasant; when they find
afterwards that the seven have been ripping up a
Dutch woman in their hurry, even the fervor of
loyalty ” can’t disguise the fact that it is cer-
tainly unpleasant. This sort of news is calculated
to much damp the enthusiasm over the departure
of Australia’s 117th contingent, or whatever the
correct figure may be.

Regina Dubberke, the candid young
heroine of the great Boot-box Tragedy, was mar-
ried at Melbourne last week to an Englishman of
some means.
A lady writes to Sydney D.T. re the
plague scare:—
There is a curious inconsistency in the actions of the
authorities. No case of infection from one human being
to another has yet taken place, no “ contact ” has taken
the disease or given it to another, no nurse or doctor has
taken it, yet the cruel dragging of patients from comfort-
able beds goes on relentlessly. And side by side with
this, in deference to notions that are admitted to be
purely sentimental, the dead are not allowed to be
cremated, but are buried, in the face of the fact that
infection has been known to occur from the re-opening of
a burial-ground 1 The dead are to be tenderly considered,
but the suffering living are to be endangered at will 1
That able veteran war - correspondent,
Bennett Burleigh, in London Daily Telegraph :
Let us wait till we hear the absolute truth about firing
on the white flag. I believe these stories to be exagger-
ated, but possible to explain if true. I profess myself to
believe the Boer to be a brave man, and as such a fitting
antagonist for English soldiers.

“ Food for Thought/’
A NYTHING which causes a drain on the nerves helps to
make a SUSPENSORY necessary. _ Excessive mental

The Great London Plague.
A GERMAN SCIENTIST’S VIEW OF IT’S
CAUSE.
“ Hundreds of years ago, as everyone knows,
London was visited by a plague that carried away
half its population, notwithstanding the fact that
physicians labored night and day to overcome the
black spectre that overhung the city. For a long
time the work was in vain. Death continued to
claim its victims, and in a spirit of terror many
people fled from the city. A well-known German
scientist, who was in London at the time, dis-
covered that all who left the city escaped the
disease ; or, at worse, had it in a mild form. This
gave him an idea, and, after thorough investiga-
tion, he found that the sewerage system was im-
perfect, that many of the principle sewers were
partly clogged up, and that in these districts the
disease was particularly fatal. He communicated
his discovery to the authorities, and a general
cleaning up was inaugurated at once. The bene-
ficial results were noticeable immediately, and it
was only a short time until the dreadful disease
was entirely stamped out, and the great plague
was no more.”
The importance of a perfect system of sewerage
in a city cannot be over-estimated, but it is not a
whit more important than that the sewerage of
the human body—the bowels and the kidneys —be
kept in perfect condition. It is just as easy for
them to become clogged or impaired in action as
it is for sewers in the streets, and the results are
just as disastrous. The bowels become costive,
and no attention is paid to it; this goes on, and
constipation follows, which weakens the actions
of the bowels, and they do not carry off all the
waste matter from the body. The sewer is
clogged, as it were, and the circulation of the
blood carries the germs of disease into all parts of
the body.
It is very easy to purge the bowels now and
then; but that only weakens the system. What
is wanted is something to tone and strengthen the
stomach, liver and bowels, so they will work of
their own free will, and we get what we call health.
Now, Bile Beans for Biliousness, for all
stomach, liver and bowel troubles, are almost an
undoubted specific; they act very gently on the
bowels and strengthen the liver’s action, and in this
way the sewerage system of the human body is
thoroughly cleansed, and instead of foul breaths,
sour stomachs, dizziness, etc., caused by the stop-
page of the bowels, health is regained.
The painful fact cannot be denied that the
Bubonic Plague is in our midst at present. Its
total obliteration depends in a large measure upon
the precaution the public itself takes.
The liver and other organs of the body should
be kept pure by taking a course of Bile Beans,
and thus the public will be better enabled to fight
against the dreaded disease.
A Laborer’s Testimony.
INDIGESTION, HEARTBURN, AND
CONSTIPATION.
CURED BY BILE BEANS.
Mr. John Partridge, of Parkside, Ade-
laide, has a pitiful tale to relate. He suf-
fered from Indigestion, Heartburn, and
Constipation, and, being a laborer, it can
be easily imagined how he was handicapped
in his business. Mr. Partridge was almost
desperate with theinconvenience his malady
caused him, and he tried many patent medi-
cines without effect. The trouble a laborer
would experience with such a complication
of disorders might easily be imagined, and
when he tells us that, when he stooped,
he experienced a horrible belching, we
can readily believe his tale. Mr. Part-
ridge is now completely cured, and he has
been good enough to forward us the follow-
ing testimony :—“ lam only too glad and
willing to add my testimony to the value
of Bile Beans for Biliousness. My liver
was quite out of order, and for over six
months I was troubled with Indigestion,
Heartburn, and Constipation. I tried
many* patent medicines and pills without

Bile Beans are the result of a discovery

The enormous success of this preparation the

" Heart-Broke! ”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a hawker, and his van bore the name of
John Price, but “Daddy” he was always called.
A little gray man he was, with an old gray horse
and a strange, shuffling way of getting about, like
a man who fears to move alertly. Beyond the
conversation incidental to trade he was never
known to utter a word, so that to some he was
known as ‘ ‘ Daddy Silence.” The children always
called him plain “Daddy.” And the childien
knew him.
Once, after a few weeks’ absence from the dis-
trict, Daddy returned with a crippled boy in his
van. He, too, was of the prevailing color gray,
but his was an ashen gray. What he was to the
old hawker no one ever knew, not even the chil-
dren. All they could learn was that he was eight
years old, a oripple from his birth, and in some
way connected with Daddy.
During Daddy’s absence a Syrian hawker had
started a van on his beat, and now times were
hard for Daddy. He decided to strike out for
fresh fields. There was a back track he knew
leading over to another district. Syrians and
Chinamen were not game to face that track.
Daddy tackled it. All day he travelled over the
blue-grass plains, hot with the heat of a scorching
summer sun. He camped for the night on the
bank of a dry creek five miles from the township
for which he was heading. The shade of the gum-
trees was refreshing even in the sunset. The boy,
who had been ailing all day, burdened with the
heat, sat up and laughed merrily as Daddy boiled
the billy. Daddy smiled in response. The old
horse was cropping away down along the creek.
Daddy wondered vaguely—would he try to make
back to the old beat. They had worked that beat
a long while together, and now, as he looked first
at the horse, then away back along the track they
had come, Daddy’s face grew sad and wistful.
The blue-grass waved softly, the stars began to
peep through, and night fell.
In the morning the old horse was gone. He
had drifted back towards the old beat. Without
troubling to look which way the tracks headed,
Daddy started after him. It was almost a plea-
sure to him, heading back. He felt inclined to
drift along slowly, carelessly, lost in recollections,
but there was the boy to think of. He would be
wondering what had happened to Daddy. The
old 'man plodded on.
When Daddy found the horse it was dying. He
sat down on a log and stared at it stupidly.
“ Heart-broke, I suppose,” he said slowly. He
went over and raised the horse’s head. It fell
back wearily, heavily. “Heart-broke!” said
Daddy; “ we shouldn’t ha’ left the old beat.” His
voice was tender as a woman’s. He choked back
a sob ; went and sat down again. By-and-by he
got up and looked at the horse. It was dead.
Mournfully Daddy faced back towards the cart.
The waving blue-grass looked sad and lonely. The
sun was scorching hot.
When Daddy reached the van the boy was cry-
ing for water. Daddy gave him a drink and went
and sat down in the shade. He needed time to
think.
The sun began to slope westward. The boy
grew uneasy, “Daddy,” he called. The old
mail rose and climbed the cart. The boy was hot
and feverish. “I’m cooked, Daddy,” he said.
Daddy did not contradict him. He turned to get
down again. “ Don’t go, Daddy, said the bov,
“ sit there and hold my hand. Daddy sat as he
was told. He was trying to think, and somehow
his brain felt heavy and useless. . ,
The shadows were lengthening; the shade of
the big gum-tree passed off the van. Daddy
looked towards the setting sun, “We must shove
on !” he said. The boy did not reply; Daddy did
not expect him to. From force of habit he picked
up the winkers and started off. After he had
gone a little way he stopped, passed his hand over
his forehead and turned back. Leaning on the
shafts of the van he looked at the empty harness.
Then 'he began to fix a chain across the shafts.
Shuffling in, he took the chain on his shoulders
and started to drag the van. The boy awoke and
began to cry feebly. Daddy took no notice. The
boy ceased crying. The old man struggled on.
Sunset. Daddy stopped for a spell. He had
got about half-a-mile, and the track was getting
heavy. A horseman came jogging along a cattle-
pad that crossed the track. Pulling up, he looked
at Daddy. “Where’s your horse?” he asked.
“ Dead,” replied Daddy. “ Anyone with you ?”
asked the man. “ Yes,” replied Daddy without
looking up. Thinking Daddy was daft, the horse-
man rode on. When he had disappeared, Daddy
clambered up into the van and looked at the boy.
There was a fleck of foam on his lips, his eyes were
dull and glazed. Getting down, Daddy went and
seated himself in his former position. “Dead,”
he muttered. “ Dead—heart-broke, too ! ”
Next day a passing squatter looked in at the
van and saw the dead boy. Daddy had disap-
peared. A week later they found him, back on
the old beat.
He, too, was “heart-broke.”
WILL M. FLEMING.
Some N.Q. Impressions.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I visited Ingham, the other day, and I have
absorbed and inwardly digested the manifold
beauties thereof. Ingham lies on the Herbert
River, and sometimes under it. Also, Ingham
grows sugar. A huge semi-circle of black soil,
about l|ft. above sea-level, and fenced in by a
towering chain of mountains —the average tem-
perature is, according to the festive sugar expert’s
report, “ eminently suitable for and conducive to
a profitable cultivation of the cane plant.” He
forgot to mention that it is also eminently con-
ducive to language.
• * * *
The township consists of a wide street, which is
named, according to the state of the weather,
‘ ‘ The Desert ” or “ The Canal. ” There are three
hotels, but they don’t flourish. The inhabitants
are too tired to drink. They mainly live on
damper and treacle, the latter luxury mostly beet
molasses imported from Europe. The arms of
the town are a cookie couchant and a C.S.R. Co.
manager rampant with the inscription : “ Knox et
prcuter ca, nihil / ”
* * * *
At kingtide a considerable area of the cane-
fields is under salt water, and when tide and flood
happen together the coastal steamers call at the
main pub. and make fast to the second-storey
balcony. When the tide recedes it deposits a
large sediment of dead fish and other vegetable
matter which quickly decay under the fierce sun.
This operation vastly enriches the soil, and in-
cidentally, the air. In fact, at times the latter
becomes so rich that the local cookies grow
pumpkins in it.
* * * *
There is a numerous population of assorted nig-
gers, the most ferocious of which is yclept the
“ walk-about kanaka.” This gentleman indulges
mainly in Chinese gin and equestrianism. The
latter is particularly stimulating, especially to the
stranger. The local resident that might be on
the road simply lies down under a culvert when he
hears a sort of Sioux war-yell in the distance and
observes a turgid cloud of dust travelling rapidly
his way. The working, or indented, variety of
kanaka amuses himself by chasing the white
ganger with a cane-knife, and much blood curdling
imprecation, round a 50-acre field on a hot day.
Then the ganger is fined £5 and costs by the local
beak.
* ■’■>* * * *
A large settlement of Japanese holds itself aloof
from the common rabble and communicates with
the outer world by means of a very dignified
official with a linotype-smashing prefix. On Sun-
days and other high church festivals they visit in
batches of a dozen, and walking solemnly in single
file, the local pub, where they retire into a parlor
while the spokesman politely requests from the
noble barmaid the distinguished honor of a
superior bottle of brandy. After the superior
snake-juice aforesaid has duly disappeared they
file silently out again and retire within their
domestic privacy. It’s for all the world like a
procession of ghosts taking refreshment. But
there’s nothing mean about the Jap.
* * * *
Leaving to catch the Townsville boat at Lucinda
Point, I travelled per steam-train. Half-way
down the train derailed and fell into a swamp. I
complained. The driver simply looked contemp-
tuous: “Yous fellows are too dashed particular
for this country,” he remarked. As the steamer
cast off the sun went down over the distant hills,
and the green, pink-tipped delta, studded here
and there with weird, purple clumps of tortured
pandanus, seemed beautiful beyond description.
“How sweet!” exclaimed the ecstatic little
school-teacher, leaning over the railing by my
side. “How could it be otherwise, m’am,” I
answered, dreamily, “ where they grow so much
sugar!”
VON KOTZE.
The story of a Southerner who married in
haste while beery, and is now repenting at leisure.
He simply remembers putting up at an hotel with
the unworthy idea of having a wild carousal, and
when he sobered up he was married to a woman
whom he had never met before, and who was plain
and had large feet. Also, she swore in a harsh,
wild brogue and hit him on the head with a
bucket as a reminder of her legal and social
status. The young man had now recovered from
his consternation, and he put himself in the water-
butt to cool whilst he reviewed the evidence. He
speedily saw that he had indeed united himself to
a female incubus weighing lGst., and he decided
that his only course was to bolt. Accordingly
when night came he stole out and disappeared
over the hills. But his bride was on his track
armed with the law, and in two days the happy
man was run down and locked up for wife-deser-
tion. When last heard of, he had been remanded
for seven days to allow of an “amicable settle-
ment.’
His “Alphabbet.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Mick Malone returned to the “farrum”
after five years of Hunter’s Hill College, it took
him all his time to understand his mother’s brogue.
“ Micky.” said she, before he’d been at home an
hour, “I hope ye’ve larned to shpell well.”
“ Spell ! —oh, I say, mater, hang it all, I’ve been
going in for Latin and Greek —almost everything
except Irish.”
“Well, Mickey, there’s wan thing do be bother-
in’ me ; an’ maybe it’s Latin, but divil a wan o’
me can unthershtand it.”
“ What is it, mater ? ”
“Just this, avick : ‘Th’ blue—th’ blow—th’
blell—th’ blow—em th’ blow —ell th’ blow.’ ” The
old woman rattled it off like a feu de joie.
“Jumping Caesar ! Say it again.”
“Th’ blue —th’ blow —th’ blell —th ”
“Hold on, mater; say it slow. I don’t seem
to get the hang of it when you fire it off like that.
Steady, now, and I’ll catch ’em as they come out,
and write ’em down.”
“ Yis, Michael. ‘ Th’ blue —th’ blow.’ ”
“The blue ! b-l-u-e, mater, or b-l-e-w?”
“Naither, ye amadhaun. Just * th’blue.’ Wid
all yer college, don’t ye know ‘ th’ blue ’ yet ? ”
“Yes, of course, but how do you spell it ? ”
“Tare an ages ! Amn’t I wantin’ you to shpell
it!”
“Oh, all right, mater; got that —the bloo the
blow. What’s next ? ”
“ Th’ blell—th’ blow.”
“ Right; but are you sure it isn’t Irish ?”
“ Irish, me fut! ” snorted the old woman.
“ What’d Irish be doin’ in the paper?”
“ Oh, in the paper, was it ? Got that
paper? No? Now, if I had the context- —
Wasn’t there anything else bssidos—what is it—
the bloo the blow, the blell the blow ? ”
“Av coorse there was. There was em th’ blow,
ell the blow.”
Mick made the addition, and surveyed the whole
with a wrinkled forehead. “ ‘ The bloo the blow;
the blell the blow; em the blow; ell the blow.’
The ‘ blow ’ seems to be in difficulties. Oh, con-
found the blow ! Here, I say, mater, I give it up.
Too much blow about it for me.”
“ There’s four ‘ the blows ’ in it,” answered his
mother, ticking them off on her fingers; “but,
never mind, Michael. The paper is losht some
place, and mebbe I’ll find it agin ; but I’m disap-
pin ted in you, Micky.”
Mick turned to his notes again. “Just come
here, mater, and see if it was spelt like this.”
“You know, Michael, I can only read print.”
Mick carefully printed the whole puzzle, and
showed it to her.
“ Fwhat the divil do ye mane by this ? T-h-e,
the ; b-l-o-o, bloo / Why, divil whip you, I said
'th' bloo! Thub-dthub — dhubloo /’ Oh, Mike,
don’t you aven know yer alphabbet yet ? Didn’t
they tache ye the .letther 'thubloo'l An’didn’t
they tell ye that two ‘ o’s ’is ‘ thublo ’ ? An’ me
thinkin’ it’s killin’ a pig I was whin I sint ye to
college ! Gimme the pin ! ” And the old woman
scrawled ‘ ‘ WOOLLOOMOOLOO. ’ ’
BENDEE.


For more Than One Hundred years
SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could not he
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com-
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears’ Soap in the form of a
Shaving Stick wants no other, he takes it with him on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until
her burning, smarting skin inexorably demands the
“ matchless for the complexion.”

THE GREATEST REMEDY OF THE AGE.
IF YOU ARE IN POOR HEALTH GIVE VITADATIO A FAIR TRIAL, AND YOU WILL
FIND, AS OTHERS HAVE, THAT IT DOES ALL THAT IS CLAIMED FOR IT.
Hopkins-street, Footscray, Feb. 7, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —It is with much gratitude I send you this testimonial regarding the exeat benefit I
have received by taking the great remedy, “W. WEBBER’S VITADATIO,” I am positive I
could not have existed much longer but for it. For 16 years I had been suffering from some un-
known disease. Having a good knowledge of herbs and their uses, I never consulted a doctor, in
fact, although I have a large family and have had the usual amount of sickness among them, by
constantly relying on herbal treatment I have never been obliged to call in medical aid. Yet in my
own case I was completely nonplussed and helpless. I may say I have been treated by a noted her-
balist, from whom I got but passing relief. He said “my lungs were filling up.” I have found by
taking VITADATIO that what he said was true, for it has brought from my lungs quantities of hyda-
tids, and also from other organs of my body ; they even came from my eyes and head. On account of
the stabbing pains in my eyes when I would stoop I became afraid of losing my sight, as it was then
very defective. I had lost all sense of feeling in some parts of my body and in both my index
fingers and thumbs, but since taking the Vitadatio my feeling has been restored. I have been in-
formed since my recovery that hydatids are a cause of paralysis, which has made me still more
thankful to you for bringing this truly efficacious medicine to Australia, and I have much reason
to thank God that I was led to try it. A good Samaritan, who told me he thought I was in a de-
cline, brought me a ss. 6d. bottle of Vitadatio, and after I had taken the third bottle the hydatids
began to appear, and then I knew what my disease really was. I felt alarmed, but kept on taking
the medicine, as I recognised there was no other hope for me. After taking 10 bottles I found
that I had increased two stone in weight; previous to this I only weighed 6st. 41b. The effect it
has had on my nerves is marvellous. I look back on the state I was in with horror, and I pity any
poor sufferers who are in a like condition, and can only recommend them to try Webber’s
Vitadatio, for it has cured me, and mine was an extreme case. If I was to mention the quantity
of hydatids removed from me by Vitadatio it would be looked upon as an exaggeration. Before
taking the Vitadatio my household duties were a burden to me, but now I can fulfil them with
pleasure, and everything wears a bright aspect. Thinking, perhaps, this testimony may induce
other sufferers to try this far-famed remedy, I give you my full permission to publish it, if you
think fit.— I am, dear sir, yours gratefully, (Mrs.) M. COXHEAD.
The price of this wonderful Remedy is ss. 6d. and 3s. 6d. per bottle. Obtainable from all

The “Troopers” of the Empire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Young Australia’s highest ambition is to be a mounted
policeman.”
From far-out tracks and out-back runs
Where all with drought was drooping,
They heard the call, Australia’s sons,
And valiantly oame trooping.
They came by road, they came by train,
From deserts grey and dusty;
From North among the sugar-cane,
From South all cold and gusty ;
From Mulga plain, from mountain-height,
From back of Never-Never,
From Carpentaria to the Bight,
From th’ Swan to Richmond River.
And as they rode o’er hill and plain,
With eager hope pulsating,
By horse or rail, this one refrain
Their hearts kept animating :
Five “ bob ” a day ! five “ bob ” a day *
A decent horse for riding ;
A uniform all bright and gay—
Who’d let the chance go sliding ?
The men who pull the strings whereby
They keep the crowd in blindness—
The hucksters and the “ statesmen ” sly,
They showed their loving kindness ;
They praised them for their “ answer grand
To Queen and country’s calling ” ;
“The Empire’s saviours,” “noble band”—
Their language was enthralling.
But we, who’re also Austral’s sons,
We know a Gov’ment billet
Has charms that no Australian shuns,
Give him the chance to fill it!
And this is what the bridles sing,
And this the hoofs are beating—
The lilt to which the stirrups ring
And ever keep repeating :
“ Five 1 bob ’ a day ! five' - bob ’ a day !
A decent horse for riding ;
A uniform all bright and gay—
Who’d let the chance go sliding ? ”
ULOOLA.
Some weeks ago a call of fire was made
at Mudgee (N.S.W.). Nearly an hour elapsed
before the call was answered, then the engine
appeared, headed by a minister, an editor, a
policeman, and a chimney sweep. The members
of the fire brigade had hurried off to get into
helmets and other millinery, and arrived on the
scene when the fire was extinguished.
What, so far as cabled information indi-
cates, are Australian volunteers to be used in
Rhodesia for ? Is it not exactly the same mission
many of them filled at Broken Hill in strike days ?
Special constables to preserve law and order when
the inevitable row begins between reduced-wages
men and the capitalistic companies. And for
this Australians are to be paid ss. a day when on
duty and Is. 2d. when off.
Milligan’s Rooster.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ike Burgess was giving it best. He had worked
the claim for six months and it did not yield him
tucker and beer, let alone clothes. His moles and
shirts were a mosaic of patches, and as for his best
coat, it had been “done in” long ago for repairs
to the rest of his wardrobe. He owed Milligan a
big score in bar and store, and Pat was turning
“ snake-headed.” Only the Saturday night before,
when he wanted to stick up a few drinks, the
landlord had ordered him out. When he tried to
argue the point, Pat slung a bottle at him, which
grazed his ear and drew blood.
Playing cunning, Ike dropped on the floor. Pat
felt frightened, and thinking bluff the best policy,
he ran from behind the bar in a terrible rage, and
prodded the prostrate man with his foot. “ Out
o’ me house, ye loafin’ blaggard ; and if I catch ye
inside me dhure anny time from Monday till
Saturday, I’ll lave no bone in ye that ain’t sausage-
meat, so I will.” Ike never stirred ; the bar
'offers flocked in, attracted by the prospect of a
fight. Pat stooped down and felt him all over,
but Ike was limp as wet string.
“ Come an, boys, and have a drink. Come an !
all of yez, free drinks all round. Poor Ike have
bumped his head agin the dhure, so he have,
an’knocked the wind out ov himself entirely,”
said Pat, trying to square the witnesses in case
of an inquiry, for he thought Ike was dead. The
boys gathered round the bar, and Ike, thinking he
was forgotten and would be out of the shout,
spoke too soon. “Mine’s a beer,” he gurgled
from his lowly resting-place.
Everybody started, and Pat’s rage, on finding
him alive and thirsty, burst out fresh. “ Och, ye
schamin devil! Ye’re not dead at all. Out o’
me house ye gang o’ robbers ; no free drinks for
annybody. Ye’d think a man had naught to do
but fill ye drunk from mornin’ till night, free
gratis for nothin’.”
The boys all dispersed, and Ike went thirsty
away, and, moreover, minus his weakly supply of
groceries. Going out of the yard a sudden thought
struck him, and, as he passed the fowl-house, he
stopped and felt about amongst the feathered
occupants. The first one he gripped he lifted
carefully off the perch, and, tucking it quietly
under his coat, struck out for his tent.
On Sunday morning, what wa3 his disgust to
find that, instead of a dinner, he had got hold of
Milligan’s champion fighting rooster, Rory
O’More, a veteran who could lay-out anything
that wore feathers. Pat’s one weakness was cock-
fighting, and he collected game-fowls with the
same enthusiasm as other men accumulate old
china. Rory was the pride of his life. He had
others of various grades of ferocity, and could
accommodate almost any man that wanted a fight
with a bird just good enough to win; but Rory
was so utterly invincible that his billet had becom
almost a sinecure. Nobody would tackle him, but
•p,nj i , , ,
Pat s judgment was usually good enough to enable
him to pick a representative from his second
string that would do the trick.
As Ike felt the bird’s powerful legs and muscles
tough as green-hide, he sadly realised that, hungry
as he was, he could no more chew Rory than he
could chew a railway-buffer. Then another idea
struck him, for hunger is the son of necessity and
the father of invention. Knowing Pat’s love for
a cock-fight, he determined to give him one that
he’d never forget, and baste him with his own
gravy, as it were.
have
plucking, and staining, he had bfen transformed
into a stumpy, rusty looking nondescript. Only

man. To the practised eye, Rory was still

he’ll be killin’ half the cocks in the district, an’
gettin’ me into trouble with the neighbors. Wait
till I go an’ look for him.”
Rory, tired with fighting, had squatted at his
master’s feet and was blinking like a broody hen.
“ You needn’t trouble,” soliloquised Ike, shoul-
dering his swag and clearing down the road like a
lamp-lighter. “He’s not billin’ the neighbors’
fowls. He’s been killin’ yer own; and he’s now
takin’ a well-earned rest, hatehin’ gravel just
forninst you.”
PERCY ABBOT.
A South Brisbane (Q.) magistrate has de-
cided that it is impossible to use obscene, in-
decent or abusive language in the legal sense unless
the abusive person speaks English. A Syrian
woman was prosecuted by another Syrian for
personal abuse, libel and indecency in their native
tongue, but though the expressions were bad
enough the South Brisbane beak decided that the
law couldn’t protect a person who was insulted in
any language except English. The writer has a
holiday in view, and he is going up to Brisbane to
call that magistrate a hog and a thief in French,
and a murderer and a deceiver of women in Italian,
and to charge him in Spanish with being a wife-
beater, Fenian, anarchist, escaped convict, em-
bezzler, lunatic, larcenist, larrikin, procrastinator,
incendiary, and uncivil person generally, and also
with robbery under arms, keeping an illicit still, and
being a “ pro-Boer.” He doesn’t know the magis-
trate from Adam, but he proposes doing this just
to see if the new legal ruling will hold water.
If any person chances to find a leper and
doesn’t want him for his own use, he is invited to
return him to the S.A. Govt., at Port Darwin,
Northern Territory, and no questions will be
asked. The Territory’s official leper has gone
a-missing. Up there the leper is isolated on an
alleged island; which is only an island at high-tide.
He is always isolated at high-tide, being taken to
the island in a boat in order to keep up the illu-
sion. At low-tide he either stays on the island
and remains isolated, or else he walks ashore and
leaves for parts unknown. The last leper was
solemnly deposited on the island at falling tide
with his stock of provisions and other necessaries,
and cut off from all communion with his kind.
Then when the tide fell a little further he can-
tered over the wet sands on to the mainland, and
over the shore and the adjacent hills, and fled like
a baseless aspersion into the mists of space.
“ A.F.W.” : Capt. Montmorency, of Mont-
morency’s scouts, whose death is cabled, was
attached to Gatacre’s column. Montmorency used
to report himself to Gatacre every morning for
instructions, but I never knew him to receive any
during the time I was at Sterkstroom (till 3/l/’00).
His men would have followed him to perdition.
He used to say—“ Well, come on, boys, and we’ll
see if we can’t shake those d d Boers up a
bit ! ” Then away they would go at any hour of
the night, and would never return without loot of
some kind—cattle, sheep, horses, &c. On one
occasion, when the troops were on short rations,
M. reported himself to Gatacre for orders, and on
receiving the usual reply : “Do as you like,” he
took all his men to the hotel, gave them a beauti-
ful “blow-out,” charged it to the Govt., and then
went out and captured 400 head of cattle. Mont-
morency was born in King Williamstown (S.A.)
of French parents, and educated in England.
